Then pour a small amount of hot water into the tube: hold your hand, and rotate it with compression of your hand a little, so that the edge of the tube is impressed in the root of the nail, and the hot water finds a way to the depth, to the root of the nail: and let the patient wait a little over the stinging of the hot water for one hour.
Then leave it, for the nail will be eradicated with its roots.
Do thus with them one after another, until you come upon all of them that are in the body.
Then treat them after they are eradicated, with that which generates flesh from the ointments.
On the method of cauterizing for Tremors.
Chapter 56.
When someone experiences tremors from cold in the nerve, or from quartan fever, or something else: then it is necessary that you cauterize them with four or five cauterizations above the vertebrae of the back, and between each vertebra with a cautery, and on their chest with a cautery, and above their stomach with a cautery.
Indeed, tremor subsides with olive-shaped cautery, and healing of cold illness hastens.
On the method of cauterizing Albathir [pustules].
Chapter 57.
Sometimes foul Albathir appear on the body, that is, pustules, which form from thick corrupted matter.
Therefore it is necessary when they first appear, that they be cauterized above every pustule head with a fine cautery using myrtle wood, whose end is lit with fire.
Or with the root of long aristolochia.
Or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes ademenul are cauterized, at the beginning of their emergence according to this method: and they do not increase: and the excess causing them breaks open, and is healed from these.
However, it is necessary that this be done after evacuation through bloodletting.
On the method of cauterizing for blood flow from an artery in a wound.
Chapter 58.
Often blood flow occurs from an artery which is cut near a wound that happens externally, or near the incision of an abscess, or cautery of a limb, and similar things: and it becomes difficult to stop it.
When therefore this happens to someone, then hurry with your hand to the opening of the artery, and place your index finger over it: and press it firmly, until the blood is contained under your finger, and nothing comes out from it.
Then place small and large olive-shaped cauteries, several in number, in the fire: and blow on them until they become very hot.
Then take one of them, either small or large, according to the wound and location where the artery is torn, and place the cautery on the vein itself, after you quickly remove your finger: and hold the cautery until the blood stops.
If it spurts out when you lift your finger from the artery opening, and the cautery cools, then quickly take another cautery from those prepared in the fire.
Do not cease doing this with one after another, until the blood stops.
And take care not to burn the nerve that is there, lest another misfortune befall the patient.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, then it is not possible to stop it: especially when the artery is large, except by one of four methods.
Either with cautery, as we have said.
Or with extraction, when it has not been extracted.
For when it is extracted, its ends contract, and the blood stops.
Or it may be tied with thread in a strong binding.
Or medicines may be placed over it whose property is to stop blood, and it may be compressed with a cushion with proper pressure.
But he who attempts to stop it with bandaging, and tightening with cloth, or placing burning things and similar items, it helps him not at all, except rarely.
If however this happens to someone, and no physician or medicine is present: then let him begin by placing the index finger over the opening of the vein itself, as we have described, and pressing it very firmly, until the blood is contained from the upper part of the wound, and above the artery.
And the finger should not be removed, [but] pour very cold water over it continuously, until the blood congeals, and thickens, and stops.
And meanwhile consider what is needed from cautery or from medicine.
End of Part One.
Surgical Book of Albucasis Part II.
¶ On Incision, Perforation, and Bloodletting in wounds, and similar matters.
We have already spoken in the First Chapter about every illness in which cauterization with fire and burning medicine is appropriate, and its occasions, its causes, and its instruments, and the forms of cauterization.
And I have placed chapters there from head to foot.
And I shall proceed in this chapter in the same way: so that the search may be made easier for the seeker.
And before I begin it, you should learn, O sons, that in this chapter there is as much terror as there is in the first chapter about cauterization.
And because of that it is necessary that caution in it be more vigilant, since in the operation which is in this chapter, many times evacuation occurs with blood, with which life stands, during the opening of a vein, or incision upon an abscess, or opening of a swelling, or treatment of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision for a stone, and similar things: from all of which trembling and fear are associated, and in many of them death occurs.
And I warn you, lest that in which there is hesitation should fall upon you, for sometimes in this art types of men come to you with types of infirmities.
Among them is he who becomes angry with his illness, and death is light to him: because of the severity of what he finds from his infirmity and the length of its malice.
And with the illness there is fear which signifies death.
And among them is he who gives you his wealth, and enriches you with it, hoping for health, while his illness is fatal.
Therefore you should not consent to him who comes to you from among them, whose manner is such.
And let your caution be more vigilant than your desire and your greed.
And do not presume upon anything from that, except after certain knowledge, which verifies for you that to which praiseworthy success leads.
And use in the treatment of all your patients, prognosis and indication of that to which health suitably follows.
For in that there is help for you in acquiring fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire in you, O sons, His understanding, and not take away from you guidance and inspiration: since that is in His hand.
And indeed I have arranged this Chapter by distinguishing it into chapters, according to what preceded in the chapter on Cautery, from neck to feet: so that the quest which you want from it, and which you need, may be made easier for you.
¶ On the treatment of Water which collects in the heads of children.
Chapter 1.
Know that this illness often happens to children at birth: or when the midwife squeezes the child's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it also happens from a hidden cause which is not known.
And indeed I have not seen this illness in others than in children: and death hastened to all of them whom I saw: therefore because of that it seemed to me better to abandon operation in it.
And indeed I saw among them a child, whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger every day, until he could not sit up by himself, because of the size of his head: and the moisture increased until he died.
And indeed this moisture either collects between the skin and bone: or collects under the bone over the membrane.
And the operation in that, if the moisture is between the skin and bone, and the swelling is small, then it is necessary that you make in the middle of the head one incision in width: and let the length of the incision be about two knuckles, until the moisture runs out.
And this is the form of the spatula.
If therefore the moisture has increased more, and the swelling is larger, then make these two incisions, according to this form. 
And if moisture is under the bone, and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open from all sides: and the water is depressed when you compress it with your hand towards the inside, and this is not hidden from you: then it is necessary that you make three incisions in the middle of the head, in this way. 
And after the incision extract all the moisture. 
Then bind the incisions with cloths and small pads. 
Then foment it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day. 
Then loosen the bandage, and treat the wound with probes and ointments. 
And do not cease to bind the head evenly. 
And feed the patient every dry food of little moisture, until the limb is strengthened and healed. 
And another method of incision is that you observe where the size of the swelling appears, and the collection of water. 
Since sometimes it is more in the occiput, or in the sinciput, or on the right, or on the left. 
Therefore ascend with the incision where the swelling and fullness of water appears to you: and cut it according to what is possible for you. 
And observe, lest you cut an artery, and bleeding occurs, and the patient dies from that flow with the evacuation of moisture. 
¶ On the Incision of Arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter 2. 
When catarrhs occur to someone in the eyes, or in the chest, and this becomes chronic, and treatment with medicines does not help: then the most ultimate treatment in that case is the incision of these two arteries. 
Therefore it is necessary when you wish to cut them, that the patient shave his head. 
Then rub the area with a rough cloth, so that the arteries appear. 
Then let the patient bind his neck with whatever cloth of his. 
Then observe where the vein pulses. 
And the locations of both are the depressed places which are behind the ears: and rarely is it hidden, except in certain men. 
Then mark with ink over both: and cut both with a double-sharp spatula, with an incision reaching to the bone: and let that be done across the width of the head. 
And if you wish, insert the spatula under the artery, and cut it upwards with an incision. 
And let the length of the incision be about two joined fingers. 
For when the vein is cut, blood exits in a pulsing manner, which leaps inward with frequent jumping. 
If however the artery does not appear to observation, then it is necessary that you measure from the ear the space of three fingers. 
Then mark with ink, and cut until reaching the bone. 
And that which is necessary to emit of blood is 6 ounces and perhaps you emit more or less from it. 
All that according to the amount which appears to you from the patient's strength and the fullness of his artery. 
Then attend to the wound. 
Then if anything remains of the membrane at the bone, then cut it, so that a hot swelling does not occur: then bind the wound with linen cloth strips. 
Then treat it with ointment, until it heals.
On the extraction of arteries that are in the temples.
Chapter III. 
When a person suffers from chronic migraine, or acute catarrhs due to sharp humors, and heat in the muscles of the temples, or chronic severe headache, and similar conditions: and when treated with methods of medical treatment, and it does not help: then we have tested in these illnesses the extraction of arteries from the temples, or their cauterization, as we have described. 
And the method of operation in extracting them is that the patient should shave the hairs that are on the temples, and attend to the visible artery in the temple. 
For it shows itself to you by its pulse, and rarely is hidden except in few people, or in extreme cold. 
But if it is hidden from you, then the patient should bind his head with any of his cloth: then rub the area with a rough cloth. 
Or steam the area with hot water, until it appears to you with clear appearance. 
Then take the spatula, which I formed a little while ago. 
Then carefully strip the skin with it until you reach the artery. 
Then insert a hook into it, and pull it upward until you extract it from the skin, and make it free from the membranes that are beneath it on all sides. 
If the artery is thin: then wrap it with the end of the hook. 
Then cut a portion of it according to the length by which its ends separate and contract, and bleeding does not occur. 
For if it is not cut, and if it is not severed, blood does not rise at all. 
Then drain from the blood six ounces, up to three. 
If the artery is large: then you must tie it in two places with a strong double thread, of silk: or with strings of gut, so that decay does not hasten to it before the wound consolidates, and bleeding occurs. 
Then cut off the excess that is between the two ligatures. 
Do that in that hour, or after another hour. 
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery up to the bone, with a knife-like cautery: until its ends are cut, and it will remain in place of the same operation. 
And it is the better procedure, unless the patient has fever, or is of hot complexion. 
Since cautery is among those things that help to end humidities. 
Therefore it is the better aid. 
And after the extraction of arteries, it is necessary that the place be filled with old cotton: and properly made compresses be placed upon it. 
And after loosening treat with dry medicines, which make flesh grow, and with lint pledgets, until it heals. 
But if meanwhile while you are operating bleeding occurs from the artery, then hasten to stop it with cautery, or to fill the place with vitriol: and press your hand, until the blood stops. 
But if none of those things is present with you, then place your finger over the place, until the blood congeals. 
And bathe the place with very cold water, until the sharpness quiets, and it is bound as necessary. 
And among what is lighter and easier than extraction of the artery, is that you cauterize the vein with this cautery having two knives: and let the sharpness of the two knives be similar to almagdatem. 
However they are of much less sharpness than two knives. 
For if they are sharp, like two knives, cold hastens to both: and they do not cut flesh quickly. 
When therefore there is some thickness in them, the heat of fire is retained in them, and flesh is cut quickly: with this therefore the procedure is better than every procedure, and lighter, and easier. 
And let the space that is between the two knives be the thickness of a finger, after you mark two places with ink: and set both down very hot, until they reach the bone, and cut the vein in two places: so that what is between its ends separates. 
For they do not consolidate at all with this cautery. 
And this is the form of the cautery.
¶ On the treatment of constant acute tear flow from the eyes.
Chapter 4.
When tears are constant, and their flow is from the veins that are on the outside of the skull of the head: and no medical treatment with medicines helps them, and you see the patient's face becoming red, and he feels in his forehead a movement, like the movement of ants: and his eyes are weakened, moist, so that their eyelashes have been eroded with corrosion: and his eyelids are heated from the sharpness of tears: then the ancients needed this operation in treating them, and they called it treatment with the Sword.
And it is that you should order the patient to shave the hair that is on his forehead.
Then make three equidistant incisions in the forehead according to the length of the forehead: and let the length of the incision be about two fingers, and let one incision be parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second along the length slightly away from the movement of the muscles, and the third on the other side: and beware of cutting the arteries that are on the two sides: and keep your hand away from the connection of the two jaws, and let the space of each incision be the width of three joined fingers: and have a piece of sponge prepared, or many dried cloths, with which to dry the blood.
Then insert a spatula with two sharp ends, whose form follows next.
From the incision which is on the side of the temple, up to the second incision: and scrape with it all the skin that is between the two incisions, with the membrane that is over the bone.
Then do that also from the middle incision up to the second incision.
Then remove this spatula, and insert into the first incision again another instrument, which is called a sharp knife, sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And this is its form.
And place its sharp side toward the flesh attached to the skin, and its smooth side toward the bone: and push it until it reaches the middle fissure: and cut with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes: except that the incision should not continue up to what appears of the skin.
Then do that in the middle fissure up to the other fissure.
And after an equal amount of blood has flowed, squeeze the areas to remove pieces of clotted blood.
Then place in every fissure a wick of old cotton, and place over them compresses soaked in wine and oil, or vinegar.
And see that no hot inflammation occurs.
And on the third day loosen the bandage, and apply plenty of infusion with lukewarm water.
Then treat it with basilicon ointment and the other things with which wounds are treated: until it heals.
¶ On the treatment of acute tears and catarrhs to the eyes from the interior of the head.
Chapter 5.
When someone experiences acute, sharp, constant catarrhs: and you see his eyes weakened, small, and their sight has already been weakened: and the eyelids ulcerated, and hairs fall from them, and in the depth of the head there is sharp, harmful pain, and consequent sneezing: then take from these symptoms, that those materials and catarrhs come only from many veins.
And the best treatment for them is this.
And it is that you should order the patient to shave his forehead.
Then make one incision in it in the middle of the forehead, or slightly to the other side across the width, and begin with the incision from the left temple to the right temple: and let the incision be down to the bone: and keep your hand away from the muscles of the temples, which move during chewing: until when the bone is exposed: and you will dry all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate between the two lips of the fissure with old cotton, or with linen wicks.
Then bind over it with compresses: and soak the compresses with wine and oil, so that no hot inflammation occurs.
And when you loosen it, and you see that the hot inflammation has already diminished: then it is necessary that you rub the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that makes flesh grow: as if you take two parts of wheat flour, and four parts of colophony: and prepare from them a plaster, and let it be administered for the growth of flesh in these wounds.
On the treatment of what falls into the Ear.
Chapter 6.
Everything that falls into the Ear is one of four types: either a mineral stone, or something similar to stone, like iron and glass; or it is from a plant, like chickpeas and beans; or it is a flowing substance, like water and vinegar and similar things; or some animal. 
When therefore a stone falls into the ear, or a type of stone from those which neither expand nor grow in the ear, expose the Ear to the sun: if therefore you see the stone, then drip into the Ear something from violet oil, or sesame oil. 
Then administer its extraction, and cause sneezing with condisi, and block the nostrils at the onset of sneezing, after you place pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and stretch the ear upward. 
Many times indeed it comes out with this treatment. 
If however it does not come out, then administer its extraction with Gesti, that is, fine tweezers: whose form is this. 
If therefore it comes out with tweezers, it is good. 
And if not, then administer its extraction with a fine blunt hook of little doubling. 
If therefore it does not come out by that means, then make a tube from bronze, and insert the end of the tube into the ear canal deeply, and block what is around the tube with wax softened with oil, so that there is no path for air except through the tube: then extract with your breath by strong suction. 
Many times indeed it comes out with what we have described. 
If however it does not come out this way: then take from helkalimbat, or from birdlime, that is, prepared glue, with which birds are caught, a small amount: and place it on the tip of a probe after you wrap it with cotton properly positioned: then insert it into the ear canal with care, after you dry the ear from moisture. 
If therefore it does not come out with all that we have described, then hurry to cut, before a hot abscess occurs, or spasm. 
And the method of cutting is that you first bleed the patient in the cephalic vein: and draw blood from him according to the measure of his strength. 
Then have the patient sit between your hands, and turn his ear upward, and make a small incision at the root of the ear near its fatty part in the depressed place there: and let the incision be crescent-shaped, until you reach the stone: then remove it with whatever instruments are possible for you. 
Then sew the incision immediately with speed, and treat it until it heals. 
But if the thing falling into the ear is from one of the grains that grow and swell: then administer its extraction. 
If however it does not yield to you for exit, then take a thin, fine spatula, according to this form. 
And administer with it the cutting of that type of grain which falls into the ear, and do not do that when you think that grain has already been moistened by the vapor of the ear, until you make it into many pieces: then extract them with hooks, or with fine tweezers, or with suction, as we said, for its extraction is made easier. 
For water however which enters the ear, it is necessary that the patient use sneezing with condisi first: and he has already filled his ear with some old cotton: and he is lying on that ear in which the water is. 
If therefore it comes out by that means, it is good. 
And if not: then take many stones, the length of a finger, thin, smooth, and heat them with fire a little, and let the patient insert one of them into his ear canal: and let him hop on one foot on that side, and strike with another stone upon the stone which is in the ear. 
Therefore he should not cease doing that with stone after stone, until all the water comes out. 
And water indeed comes out so that you take from papyrus, or from reed one piece, and insert one end of it into the ear: and light the other end with fire, until most of it burns. 
Then repeat with another reed, and do that many times, until all the water comes out: and extract it with a tube according to what was described previously for the stone.
In extracting small animals that enter it (the ear), if it is of small body, like a flea or similar, treat it as I described in the division. 
But if it is of large body, which is visible to the senses: then perform its extraction with forceps and hooks: and this matter is easier than all things that are held in the ear. 
In extracting a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we described in the division and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment does not help: then you should examine the ear in sunlight. 
If therefore something appears there from the worms, then extract it with forceps or with fine hooks. 
If however nothing appears there, then take a small tube, which has this form, narrow below, wide above: and insert the narrow end into the ear according to the amount that the patient can tolerate: then suck with it using strong suction. 
Do this many times, until all worms come out. 
If however they do not yield to you for exit, then seal the circumference of the tube with wax, as I said regarding the stone. 
But if they do not come out with what we described: then administer the distillations which the ancients have already tested in killing worms: and you will find that in the treatise on distillations. 
And let your pouring with which you pour oils and medicines into the ear be with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. 
As you see make it from silver, or bronze, narrow below, in which there should be a small opening, wide above. 
Therefore let the plunger which is in the cavity of the tube be made appropriately from bronze. 
And if you wish, take a probe, and wrap cotton at its end with a proper wrapping: then pour the oil, or juice, or whatever you want from these medicines into the tube: and let it be in the ear: then insert the probe with cotton from above, and press it with your hand with moderate pressure, until the oil is forced into the cavity of hearing, and the patient feels it inside: and let that which is poured into the ear be already slightly warmed with fire: and take care that the thing which is distilled into it is not very cold: for medicines do not tolerate that.
On the treatment of blockage occurring in the Ear.
Chapter 7. 
Sometimes certain infants emerge from their mothers' wombs with [ears] not perforated.
And blockage in the ear canal also happens to some people from a wound, or flesh growing in it.
And this blockage sometimes occurs deep in the ear hole, which sight cannot reach: and sometimes it occurs near the exterior of the hole, and sight can reach it.
Therefore that which occurs deep in the ear, and which sight cannot comprehend, is in most cases difficult to cure.
And for that which sight can reach, you should place the patient's ear in the sun, and look into it: then if you see an apparent blockage, open it with a thin spatula, whose form follows here, let its tip have some small width, of which some part should be sharp: and let the rest of the spatula have two smooth sides: so that it does not harm the ear. 
If therefore the blockage is from flesh grown in the ear canal: then hold it with a fine hook, and cut it with careful force, until all the flesh is removed.
But if the blockage is deep in the ear: then take a thin, smooth probe, and heat it slightly with fire: then insert it into the ear canal.
If therefore you sense the blockage with it, which the patient finds with difficulty of hearing, attempt to open it carefully: and beware, lest you wound the ear nerves.
Then place in the ear a lint according to the size of the hole's width, which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment: do this for several days, until you are assured of the wound's healing.
Or take lint, and soak it in rose water, and sprinkle ground vitriol over it, and use it. 
If you see the ear becoming infected: then you should remove the lint, and replace it with another lint, which you will wrap in a wax preparation made with rose oil, until the hot inflammation subsides: then treat it until it heals.
But if bleeding occurs: then submerge a sponge, or cloths in cold water, and place them over that ear: and use the remaining treatment, which stops bleeding as described in the treatise on distillations. 
On the treatment of warts of the eyelids.
Chapter 8. 
These warts that occur on the eyelids of the eye are sometimes moist, and are dry.
Therefore you should hold them with forceps: or with a hook, and cut them with a spatula from their roots.
Then place ground vitriol over the areas.
But if blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a fine lenticular cautery.
And cauterizing them is better after incision.
For they often return when something remains from their roots.
And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned, and do not return. 
On the treatment of Hailstones of the eye's eyelids.
Chapter 9. 
Often occurs in the eyelids something that resembles Hailstone in its hardness, and its firmness, and because of that it is called Hail: and it is an accumulation of thick moisture in the upper eyelid, and lower eyelid.
Therefore the operation on them is, that you should consider then, if it is Hail proceeding in what appears of the eyelid, and they move to every part, and its matter is easy.
Therefore make an incision over it lengthwise.
Then carefully excise it from all sides until it comes out.
Then suspend it with a hook, and cut it off.
But if its removal is not possible for you, except after you penetrate the eyelid with a small incision, then that harms the patient somewhat.
But if the incision is large: then bring it together with sutures: and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it.
For ointment restores it, and makes it heal.
And if the hail is declining toward the interior of the ear toward the inner surface, then invert the eyelid, and suspend the hail with a hook: rather than needing incision, and press it from all sides.
But if the eyelid is elongated by the incision, that does not harm in any way.
Then wash the eye after the incision of the hail, with salt water: and treat the area with that which heals, until the patient recovers.
On the treatment of Axirnath of the eyelids.
Chapter 10.
Axirnath is a fatty substance that forms in the layers of the upper eyelid: and indeed it occurs mostly in children: and it weighs down their eyes, and they get catarrhs in the mornings, and they cannot look at sunlight, because tears come quickly to them, and because of this you see them always lying on their faces, and they sleep on them, and their eyelids under the eyebrows are moist, which swelling has already covered: and indeed the prominence of Axirnath is visible to the eye, and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel Axirnath between them. 
And the method of operating on it is that the patient should place their head in your lap. 
Then take a linen cloth, and prepare strips from it, and make from that strip a circle according to the size that contains the Axirnath from all sides. 
Then place it over it, and press with your fingers from all sides, so that the Axirnath gathers in the middle of the circle. 
Then make an incision in the middle of that moisture with a cutting spatula, making cuts across the width, and let the incision not be deeper than what is made in phlebotomy. 
Either it is necessary that you cut the entire skin, until you reach the Axirnath for in many cases the Axirnath comes out of the incision in the form of a piece of white fat. 
Therefore extract it with a cloth, which you have worked between your fingers toward the outside: and you turn your hand to the right and left, until it disperses: and take care not to deepen the incision, lest you reach the eye with the spatula and harm it. 
If however the Axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the incision, you should deepen the incision a little bit carefully, until the Axirnath comes out. 
Then extract it, as we have said. 
Then soak cloths in vinegar and water, and place them over the area, and bind it with small cushions. 
And among people there are those who grind salt and place it in the cavity of the incision, so that what remains of that moisture may dissolve. 
Then treat it until it heals. 
If however a hot abscess occurs in the area, then treat it with soothing plasters until it heals.
¶ On the types of Ataxmir of the eye, that is, when the eyelid becomes soft, whereupon it wrinkles in a way it shouldn't.
Chapter XI.
When additional hairs grow in the eyelids beneath the natural hairs and become established: then they harm the eye and cause various types of ailments, such as constant tearing, softening of the eyelids, and white spots, until this becomes the cause of the eye's destruction.
And indeed Ataxmir of the eye is treated in four ways.
Either by cautery with fire.
Or with sharp medicine, according to what was described in the chapter on cautery.
Or Ataxmir is treated by incision and suture.
Or by tubes, according to what I will describe.
It is necessary that you place the patient's head in your lap.
Then turn the eyelid with your left hand.
If therefore it turns, it is good.
And if not: insert a needle with thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate through with the thread from above, and let this be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the margin of the eyelid where the hairs grow: and pull the thread upward with the eyelid, and turn it with a probe.
Then make an incision in the inner part of the eyelid under the additional hairs with a cutting spatula from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct.
Then remove the thread, and place under the eyelid a small pad of cotton or cloth: then mark on the eyelid with ink a figure like a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that the figure be according to the size and elevation of the eyelid: since this varies among people.
Among them indeed is one who needs to have cut from the eyelid a large amount, according to the amount that has become soft from the eyelid.
Then with the spatula cut along the two lines which you marked, and begin from the larger tear duct up to the smaller tear duct, and let one cut be near the natural hairs, like the thickness of a probe.
Then insert a hook into one of the two angles of the skin.
Then excise all of it.
Then join the two edges with sutures, using a needle and fine wool thread: and wipe away the blood, and make what is excess of the threads adhere above the two eyebrows, with certain sticky substances, if you wish to do this: and if not, don't worry about it.
Then leave the suture and threads for three or four days: afterward treat it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without sutures, and treat it with something that dries and constricts.
For the eyelid is elevated during the sealing of the wound and its joining: but suturing is better.
The ancients taught this method of treating Ataxmir.
However, in it there is concern for the patient, and it is from good practice, and there is no fear in it.
And there is another method also in Ataxmir: and it is that you mark on the eyelid a figure that is like the figure of a myrtle leaf, as we described.
Then elevate the eyelid with three hooks separated or joined, according to this form which follows, then cut what is excess with small scissors, according to this form, with evenness of incision.
If however it is not possible for you to hold the hook, and that is not even: then take a needle with thread, and insert it in the middle of the figure: and insert another thread near the larger tear duct, and a second thread near the smaller tear duct: and gather the threads between your fingers evenly.
Then raise your hand with an even elevation: and elevate the skin over which the cotton is marked, as we described.
Then join the two edges of the wound with sutures: and treat it until it heals.
If however swelling occurs at your incision or your cut: calm that swelling with wax ointment, and similar things, from soothing ointments.
And sometimes it also happens to the eyelid that its hairs turn inward.
Therefore it is necessary that you use in it what we said in incision: but suturing in both cases is more skillful.
On the treatment of Ataxmir with reeds. 
And it is thus: you turn over the eyelid and make an incision in the inner fissure, according to what I have described. 
Then skillfully make two reeds, or two thin pieces of wood: their length should be according to the length of the eyelid, and their width should be less than the width of a spatula: and you have already made cuts in the extremities of both on both sides where you hold the threads. 
Then carefully bring both together over what protrudes from the eyelid, and bind the two reeds tightly on both sides with a strong binding, and leave it for several days. 
For the constricted skin will die, turn black, and rot, until it falls off by itself. 
If therefore it is delayed, then cut it with scissors. 
Then treat it until it heals. 
When therefore it heals, the eyelid is raised: and the hairs do not prick. 
And this is the form of the two reeds, which you will make according to this figure and measurement in length and width. 
On raising pricking hairs in the eye with a needle.
Chapter XII. 
The operation in this case is that you take a fine needle, and insert into it a fine, smooth silk thread. 
Then gather its ends, and tie both with a very fine knot: and let the length of the threads be one palm. 
Then arrange in the Alinxotati another fine thread below it briefly, and tie its ends if you wish. 
Then let the patient place his head in your lap, and let it be near the sun so that the operation is clear: since it is a delicate or minute operation. 
Then insert the needle with the alinxotati into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly, until you draw it out above the natural hair. 
Then draw the hair up above the alinxotati: and insert into the alinxotati that hair: whether it is one, or two, or three, not more. 
Then pull your hand with it, until the hair comes out with the alinxotati in the eyelid with the natural hair. 
But if the alinxotati is pulled and the hair does not come out with it: pull the alinxotati downward, with the thread that you arranged in it, until the alinxotati comes out from the upper opening, and you see it with your eye: then indeed pull the alinxotati and the thread: and extract both, and close the eye: and leave it closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh grows over it. 
But if the hair is short, then either join to it a long hair from the natural hairs, and it is raised with it: or leave it until it lengthens after a time, and then raise it. 
On the treatment of Alxatrati which occurs in the upper eyelid.
Chapter XIII. 
When this Alxatrati occurs in the eye, it is called Leporina (Hare-eye). 
And this Alxatrati is either natural or accidental. 
The accidental therefore occurs from the healing of a wound, or an incision, or a cautery, and similar things to these. 
And the method of operation in it is that you split that healed area, and separate its edges, and place between them a lint of flax, and bind it until it heals. 
And it is not proper that you use in its treatment things which dry out and constrict: for if you do that, the Alxatrati returns worse than it was: rather use in it things which soften, like fenugreek: and foment with water in which marsh-mallow and flaxseed have been cooked: and with diachylon ointment, which has been liquefied with some of the oils: and wrap the lint with it, and treat with it. 
And the treasure of your treatment is that you strive with all ingenuity, that it does not heal according to the form which was there at first. 
And this operation which we have described is nothing but a certain rectification of Alxatrati, so that the form of the place does not return entirely to what it was.
Concerning the treatment of Alxatrati which occurs in the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkling or contraction upward to the right and left.
Chapter 14.
This Alxatrati which occurs below is what is truly called Alxatrati.
And it is natural, and it is accidental.
And the accidental type occurs from a wound, or a fissure, or cauterization, and similar things.
And the method of operation for it is that you should take a needle with a double thread, and push it into the flesh, and make it penetrate from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
Then stretch the flesh upward with the needle, and cut it with a broad spatula.
If therefore the shape of the eyelid returns as it should, it is good.
And if not, take a probe, and place it in the fissure, and turn the eyelid with it: and make two fissures on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the ends of the two fissures be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and form an angle from them: until when they are joined their shape becomes this figure, which is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh by the amount that is its sharp lower edge, from what follows the eyelid.
Then join the separated parts: sew it with wool thread.
Then treat them with what we mentioned, with softening medicines, and lint, until they heal.
And if Alxatrati occurs from a fissure, and suture, or cauterization: then you should make a flat fissure under the eyelid hairs, also according to what preceded.
Then separate between the two lips with lint, according to what we said.
And the summary of the discussion about treating Alxatrati, whether it is above or below, is that the operation should proceed in it according to how the form of Alxatrati presents itself.
For it has many variations in form: and the skilled craftsman prepares his technique in whatever way is possible for him, until he makes the shape return to its natural form, or close to it, and the patient himself, according to what suits him in terms of operation and instruments in most conditions.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid with the conjunctiva, or cornea.
Chapter 15.
Sometimes this consolidation happens to many people, such that the upper eyelid consolidates with the white of the eye, and prevents it from its natural operations.
Therefore it is necessary that you insert the end of a probe under the eyelid, and raise it upward: or stretch it with a hook: then cut the adhesion with a fine spatula, which should not have a sharp cutting edge, rather it should be somewhat blunt: so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula is removed, and does not harm the eye with its sharpness: and your incision should be as if you are skinning that adhesion with ease, until when the eyelid returns to its natural form, and the entire adhesion heals.
Then pour salt water into the eye, wash it with water: or drip into it from an eye-salve made from verdigris dissolved in water.
Then separate between the eyelid and eye with linen lint: and place over the eye wool soaked in egg white: and after the third day use eye-salve, and with healing agents, until it heals.
¶ On the incision of the pterygium and the prominent flesh of the tear ducts.
Chapter 16.
Know that the pterygium occurs in two forms.
Either it is fibrous: and it is similar to a hard, thin membrane.
Or it is non-fibrous, similar to white congealed fluid: which when iron touches it, or when you try to grasp it with a hook, it breaks apart, and the hook does not hold in it.
And both types begin only from the larger tear duct, until it gradually progresses until it covers the vision, and blocks light, and movement of the eye.
And the method of operating in its incision is: that the patient places their head in your lap.
Then open their eyes, and lift the eyelid with your hand.
Then gather the pterygium with a hook of little doubling, and extend it upwards.
Then take a needle, and insert into it a hair from a horse, and ox, or cow: and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle slightly, and fix it in the middle of the pterygium: and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the pterygium with the thread, and extend it upwards: and strip away with the hair the side of the pterygium that follows the pupil, as if you were sawing it with the hair all the way to its end.
Then cut the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a fine spatula, or with small scissors: and leave the natural flesh of the tear duct, so that constant tearing does not occur from its incision.
And sometimes the pterygium is extended with just a hook: or with thread, as we have said.
Then it is stripped away with a smooth spatula, whose form is this.
And be careful, lest the spatula touch the cornea, and a rupture occurs in it, and the pupil is perforated.
Then after the incision, throw into the eye some ground salt, or from an eye-salve made from verdigris, and leave it until another day.
Then treat it with what is appropriate, until it heals.
But if the pterygium is non-fibrous, and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor does a hook hold in it, then many times this type of pterygium is treated with medicines, and heals as I described in the division.
But if you wish to cut it, then open the patient's eye, and take a fine spatula, smooth, of weak sharpness, according to this form.
And scrape the pterygium from above with a scraping motion with delicacy.
But if you see that it becomes thin and breaks apart, and the scraping makes an impression in it, then drip at that time into the eye from an eye-salve made from verdigris, or from red eye-salve, or ground salt: and cover the eye until another day.
Then repeat the operation upon it, until all of it is removed.
Unless there is opposing you in the eye a hot abscess: then indeed leave it, and treat the abscess, until it heals.
Then repeat the operation upon it with scraping, until it heals.
The prominence of the tear duct flesh, however, if it causes great harm in the eye, let it be suspended with a hook, and cut from it a part of it: and do not complete the incision, so that constant tearing does not occur.
Then drip into the tear duct red eye-salve, or one made from verdigris, until it heals.
On the incision of Alguardenic, and flesh growth in the eye.
Chapter 17. 
Sometimes in certain human eyes there grows red flesh, either composite or superimposed, until it covers the pupil, or approaches it, or binds over the eyelids, and perhaps turns the eyelids outward: and it resembles a pomegranate flower. 
And the operation for this is also that the patient should place their head in your lap. 
Then open their eyes, and gather all that flesh with hooks suitable for that purpose: and hold it with one forceps, or surgical instrument. 
Then first cut the flesh, until all of it is removed with the cutting instrument. 
And be careful with the eye, so that you do not harm it during the operation: and let your incision with which you cut it be either with the small spatula, which we described in the section on pterygium incision: or with small scissors, with which asebel is collected, according to the form that will come after this chapter. 
Therefore when your incision is complete, and all of the alguardenici is removed, then fill the eye with ground salt: or drip in red powder, and similar corrosive medicines: and place externally on the eye a cotton pad with egg white: so that it may be protected from hot inflammation. 
If any of the alguardenici remains, and hot blood dominates you, and you fear hot inflammation: then leave the eye, and treat it with that which calms inflammation. 
Then return to it with the operation, until it is healed. 
And similarly do with additional flesh which occurs in the eye of this type. 
However, it is necessary that you avoid treating any eye that is weak by nature or by accident by operating with iron: and do not introduce your hand in any of these operations until you see that that member can tolerate that operation. 
On the collection of Sebel from the eye.
Chapter 18. 
Sebel are red veins which are woven over the eye, and prevent vision its operation: and weaken the eye with the length of days. 
Therefore it is necessary for you first to consider if the eye in which there is Sebel is strong, and there is no other illness in it besides Sebel: then collect its Sebel. 
And it is that you should instruct the patient to place their head in your lap. 
Then suspend those veins with one hook, or two according to your judgment: and let the hook be of subtle doubling, according to this form, or let there be two hooks graduated in one body, according to this form. 
Then collect with subtle scissors those veins with subtlety, and wipe away blood hour after hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye, and they are dissolved: and be careful not to harm the eye with the ends of the spatulas. 
And let your operation be at noon before the sun, and be very firm in your operation: so that you cut nothing except those veins. 
And when you have finished, then drip green or red sief into the eye: so that by its sharpness it may corrode what remained of the sebel. 
If however it is not possible for you to collect all of it in that hour, then plaster the eye with that which sedates the pain of the eye: and leave it for some days, until its pain is sedated: and it is secured from hot inflammation: then return to it in the same way, until it is healed. 
And this is the form of the scissors.
On the Reduction of Arissati to the Nose.
Chapter 19.
Doctors call Arissati a Fistula, which you treat with cautery, or with sharp burning medicine, according to what was previously described.
And if it does not heal, then there is no technique for it except to cut open above the abscess at its origin, and extract all the moisture that is in it and the pus, until the bone is exposed.
When therefore the bone is exposed, and you see corruption in it, or blackness, then scrape it with an instrument, whose form is this.
And it is called "of the rough head."
Let it be made from Indian iron, and let its head be round like azir, which should be dotted with file-like dotting, or with aliskilfer dotting with fine points: and place it over the corrupted part of the bone.
Then rotate it between your fingers, and press your hand slightly, until you know that the corruption has been scraped away: and do this several times.
Then restore the area with drying astringent medicines.
If therefore the area heals, and flesh grows in it, and the flow of pus stops: and it remains for forty days, and does not renew, and does not form an abscess, and no complication occurs in it: then know that it has healed.
And if not, then there is no technique for it except to redirect the fistula to the nose opening, according to this form.
That is, expose it from the second bone with iron, or with sharp medicine: and when the bone is exposed, then take a drill of this form, let the iron tip be triangular: and its wood should be a pyramid tapered to the tip, as you see.
Then place it on the bone itself: and let it be near the tear duct: and keep your hand a little distance from the eye.
Then rotate your hand with the drill, until it penetrates the bone, and the patient feels it: by this that he finds wind coming out of it, when he holds his hand over his nose.
Therefore then restore the area with astringent drying medicines, as I taught you.
When therefore flesh grows, and the area hardens: then the matter which was pouring to the outside returns to the nose: this is therefore easier for the patient.
On the Reduction of Eye Protrusion.
Chapter 20.
When the Eye protrudes in its entirety, and no harm occurs to vision, nor diminishment: then it is necessary that you loosen the patient: then perform phlebotomy on him: afterwards place cupping glasses on the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then place on the Eye a liniment which is made from acacia, and aloe, and olibanum, and sarcocolla.
Then bind the Eye from above over the plaster with many compresses.
And the method of compresses is: that you take many soft cloths, and each cloth from them should be folded in four: make many of these: then place them one above another, according to the quantity that the Eye needs: then bind over them with a hard cloth with strong binding.
And repeat the cupping glasses with suction without scarification.
Then remove the cupping glasses, and leave the eye bound for a day and night.
If however the bandage becomes loose again, then it is necessary that it be bound tightly: then remove the bandage after a day and night, as we said.
If therefore you see the eye has returned to the uveal tunic, it is good: and if not, then repeat the plaster, and compresses, and binding, and cupping glasses, until it heals.
On the Incision of the Uveal Membrane.
Chapter 21. 
When a rupture occurs in the uveal membrane, and then it protrudes and proceeds outside the eyelids like a grape, and the person's appearance is disfigured because of it, and you wish to cut it: then the operation on it is as I describe: that is, you should insert a needle at the root of the uvea from the lower to the upper part. 
Then insert another needle with a double thread from the lacrimal side, and make it penetrate: and leave the first needle in its position. 
Then cut the place of the double thread, and tie with it the part of the uvea around the needle: and tighten it firmly. 
Then remove the needle, and place wool soaked in egg white on the eye: and leave it until the thread falls off with the uvea. 
Then treat the eye with that which strengthens it, until the wound heals. 
And sometimes the uvea is constricted from all sides with a circle of cloth, until the eye produces a large protrusion. 
Then it should be perforated with a fine spatula, which should reach to the depth of the eye, and the albuminous fluid should flow, and the eye should descend, and enter immediately: then compress it until it heals. 
On the Treatment of Alcomnati.
Chapter 22. 
This Disease which is called Alcomnati is nothing but pus, which collects in the eye, similar to descending water, but is not the same. 
And the method of operating on it is that you should have the patient sit on a prepared or straight seat. 
Then take his head in your hands from both sides, and move it until you see the pus reach the lower part with your eyes. 
Then it should be fixed, and not descend, and light should be released: and the patient should see things as he used to see: and if it does not descend lower, know that it is water. 
If however the descent of pus is not achieved with what we have said, then have the patient sit between your hands. 
Then take a fine spatula, and make a small incision with it above the corneal membrane at the junction of the conjunctiva with the corneal tunic in the corona, until the pus comes out. 
When therefore it comes out, then drip warm water mixed with honey into the eye: or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey: then it should be treated with the remaining treatment until it heals.
On the treatment of water that descends in the eye, or cataract.
Chapter 23. 
We have already discussed the types of water in the division, and what is suitable for piercing with explanatory and expository discourse, so take that same according to health. 
Therefore you should have the patient sit square between your hands, facing the light, near the sun: and bind his healthy eye, and bind it tightly: then [hold] his eyelid with your right hand, if the eye in which there is water is the left eye: or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one. 
Then take the needle (almagda) in your right hand, if the eye is left: or in your left hand, if the eye is right. 
Then place the tip of the needle near the corona [iris] about the width of a radius in the white of the eye appearing at the smaller tear duct: then push the needle with force: and you rotate your hand with it, until it penetrates into the white of the eye: and you feel that the needle has reached an empty space. 
And the measure of the needle's path to the depth should be the measure of the space that is from the pupil to the end of the blackness: which is the corona of the eye. 
For you will see these things in the pupil itself by visual inspection, due to the clarity of the corneal membrane. 
Then place the needle up to the place where the water is. 
Then press it downward one time after another. 
If therefore the water descends immediately, then the patient sees that upon which he opens his sight immediately, with the needle still in his eye. 
Then rest for a little while. 
If therefore the water descends, then lower it a second time, before the needle comes out. 
When therefore it is fixed and does not rise: then extract the needle with care: and you rotate your hand gradually. 
Then dissolve some clear rock salt in water: and wash the inside of the eye with it. 
Then place outside the whole eye either tow or wool soaked in rose oil and egg white: and bind the healthy eye with it. 
We moreover, and those of our time, place ground cumin with egg white upon it. 
But if the needle does not yield to you for entry into the eye due to its hardness, since among men there are those whose eye is very hard: then you should take the small spatula which is called Alberid, of which this is the form. 
Therefore perforate the conjunctiva itself only with it, and do not complete your hand in the hole: and it is only so that you make a subtle path to the place for the needle. 
Then insert the needle, according to what we have said. 
And at the completion of your operation, let a properly made sleeping place be prepared for the patient, in which he may sleep on his back in a dark room: and let him be prohibited from all movements and from coughing: and make his food that which softens his nature: and let him not move his head right or left at all. 
And let the bandage remain according to its arrangement until the third day. 
Then loosen it in that dark room, and test his vision: and make him look at things. 
Then replace the bandage until the seventh day. 
And he should not do that at the time of treatment, or immediately after the incision: rather you should keep that away: because the water rises quickly through intense looking. 
But if a hot swelling occurs: then you should loosen the eye before the seventh day: and correct it with that which calms the swelling, until it is calmed: then free his vision, and place over his face a veil by which his vision may be regulated under it for some days, while he remains in that dark room. 
Then let him leave the room gradually, and spend time in his houses. 
And know that in surgery the student cannot be excused from observing sometimes: for then he may dare to perform the operation. 
And indeed there has come to us someone from those who are from Alayrach, who said that in Alayrach a perforated needle (magdan) was made, by which water is sucked out. 
But truly I have not seen anyone in our land who has done that: nor have I read it in any of the books of the ancients: and it is possible that this is new. 
And these are the forms of the types of needle, so that one who is ignorant of them may understand them. 
Let them be made specifically from brass: and let their ends be with this same subtlety triangulated, and the end sharp. 
Forms of the needle.
¶ On the Treatment of Flesh Growing in the Nose.
Chapter 24. 
Various types of additional flesh sometimes grow in the nose, among which is one that resembles a scorpion with many feet; and among them is cancerous flesh, stone-like, of dark color; and among them is flesh that is smooth, not of dark color. 
Therefore in the case of flesh among these that is smooth, not deceitful, nor cancerous, you should have the patient sit before you facing the sun, and open their nostrils, and insert a hook into that flesh; then draw it outward, and cut what you have grasped of it with a thin sharp spatula from one side, until you know that all the flesh has been removed. 
If any should remain whose removal is not possible: then scrape it with one of the fine instruments gently, until nothing of it remains. 
If however blood overwhelms you, or a hot swelling occurs: then treat it as appropriate: or if it is among the malignant swellings, then hasten to cauterize it, until the blood is stopped, and all flesh is removed. 
Then after the incision, pour vinegar and water, or wine into the nose. 
If however the nose opens, and moisture proceeds from it down to the palate, then know that it is already healed. 
If truly the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then know that within it there is flesh grown on the upper bones of the nostril, which the instrument cannot reach with cutting. 
Therefore then it is necessary that you insert a linen thread of some thickness, and tie many knots in it: and place between each knot the distance of a finger, or less: and the patient should contrive to insert one end of the thread into the nose with a probe, or with whatever is possible for them after making it similar to azer: and draw in their breath until it reaches the palate and comes out above their throat. 
And indeed many times the school boys do similar to this in schools: and it is an easy thing for one who wishes. 
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end which comes out above the nose, and the other which remained in the nose. 
Then administer the sawing of the flesh with the knots that are in the thread. 
Do this until you know that the flesh has been caught with the knots of the thread. 
Then extract the thread: and place in the nose after cleaning the blood a plug which you have soaked in Egyptian ointment: do this for three days, or more, until the ointment corrodes all that remained of the flesh. 
Then at the end of the matter place a lead tube in the nose, for several days, until it heals. 
If however it needs treatment that dries, use that. 
For making head-purging, with which oils and other medicines are dripped into the nose, make that from silver, or bronze: which should be similar to a small lamp, open like a small shell: and its passage likewise. 
And if you wish make a closed tube, like a reed. 
And the shell of the instrument for making head-purging should be full, uncovered: which should have a handle at its back, with which you hold it when you heat oil in it, or whatever you want from juices and flowing substances. 
And this is the form of the instrument. 
¶ On the Treatment of Thelul of the nose.
Chapter 25. 
Many times Thelul is born at the extremity of the nose, and grows large, and increases with days, until its appearance becomes foul. 
And because of that it is necessary that you cut it at the beginning of its appearance, and root out all of it. 
Then place over the spot either cautery, or burning medicine which serves in place of it. 
If therefore its cutting is delayed until it grows large, then consider, if it is stone-like, hard, of dark color, with little sensation: then do not touch it with iron: since it is a cancerous swelling. 
For many times I have seen one who cut such a swelling, and great harm occurred from it to the one who had it. 
And if the swelling is soft to touch, not of dark color, and you see that cutting is possible in all of it, then evacuate the patient, and cut it without fear, without dread: and treat the spot with that which dries and constricts, until it heals.
On the suture of the nose, lip, and ear.
Chapter 26.
Know that when a break in continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, treatment is of little benefit, except in certain people.
Therefore when something of this sort happens to someone, you should consider whether it is a fresh wound with its blood: and then join the two lips of the wound with a suture: then treat it until it heals.
And if the lips of the break in continuity are already separated, and each part has become healthy: then you should scrape each part from its visible skin until it bleeds.
Then join the two lips with a suture, and bind them, and sprinkle over it dragon's blood and ground frankincense: and place above the powders a piece of diaphoenicon plaster, and some other consolidating plasters: and leave it bound for two or three days.
Then loosen it, and change the medicine: and leave it until the threads fall off by themselves.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.
The method of suturing is to join the break in continuity, either with needles, as we described in the suture of the belly: or join the break with thread, as I made known to you. 
On the extraction of nodes from the lips.
Chapter 27.
Indeed, many people often develop hard swellings similar to vetch seeds on the inside of their lips: and some of them smaller, and larger.
Therefore you should turn the lip, and cut into each of its nodes, and lift it with a hook, and cut it away from all sides.
Then fill the place after the incision with ground vitriol, until the bleeding stops.
Then let it be washed with vinegar, and let the area be treated with something astringent, until the wounds heal. 
On the cutting of flesh grown over the gum.
Chapter 28.
Often flesh grows over the gum additionally, which the ancients called abuluss.
Therefore you should lift it with a hook: or hold it with forceps, and cut it at its root, and let the pus or blood flow.
Then place ground vitriol on the spot, or other astringent, drying powders.
But if that flesh returns after treatment, for it often returns, then cut it a second time, and cauterize it: for it does not return after cauterization. 
On the scraping of teeth with iron.
Chapter 29.
Sometimes rough, unsightly crusts gather on the surfaces of teeth from the inside and outside, and between the gums, and sometimes they become black, and yellow, and green, until corruption reaches the gum from this, and the teeth become foul because of it.
Therefore you should have the patient sit before you, and their head in your lap, and scrape the molar and tooth in which crusts and things similar to sand appear to you: until nothing of them remains, and similarly do this with blackness, and greenness, and yellowness, and others until they are cleaned.
If therefore what is in them is removed from the beginning of scraping, it is good: and if not, then repeat the scraping on them another day, a second, and third time, until finally you achieve what you want.
And know that the tooth needs scrapers of different forms, of many shapes: according to what is prepared for your operation: because the scraper which scrapes the tooth from below is different from the scraper which scrapes between teeth, according to other forms.
And these forms of scrapers should be prepared with you, which you see.
¶ On extracting tooth pain.
Chapter 30.
It is necessary that you treat the tooth and its pain with all skill, and delay its extraction.
For there is nothing that can replace it when it is extracted: since it is an elevated substance: until when there is no excuse for its extraction, then it is necessary when the patient is forced to extract it, that the painful tooth be confirmed and verified by you.
For many times the pain deceives the patient, and he thinks that it is in a healthy tooth and extracts it: then the pain does not go away, until the diseased tooth is extracted.
We have indeed seen this from the work of bloodletters.
When therefore the painful tooth itself is verified by you, then it is necessary that you scarify around the tooth with a small spatula, in which there should be some force, until the gum is loosened from all sides: then move it with your fingers, or with fine forceps at first gradually, until you shake it loose.
Then place large forceps on it with good positioning, and the patient's head between your knees: which you have already held firmly, so that it does not move: then pull the tooth straight, so that you do not break it.
If therefore it does not come out, then take one of those instruments, and insert it under it from all sides with care, and attempt to move it, as you did at first.
If however the tooth is perforated, or corroded: then it is necessary that you fill that hole with cloth: and tighten it with the end of a fine probe, so that it does not break at the moment of your gripping it with the forceps.
And it is necessary, that you act precisely with scarification around the gum from all sides extensively.
And guard your strength, so that you do not break it, and part of it remains, and return to the patient from it an evil which would be greater than his first pain.
And beware, lest you do what ignorant bloodletters do in their haste, and their boldness in extracting it, without administering what we have described.
For many times they cause great illnesses to happen to people, the more difficult of which are that they break, and all or some of their roots remain: or that they extract it with some bones of the jaw: as we have witnessed many times.
Then after its extraction it should be washed with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
If bleeding occurs from the sites (for it often occurs) then grind some vitriol, and fill the place with it: and if not, cauterize it if the vitriol does not help you.
The forms of fine forceps with which the tooth is first moved, should be of long tips, short handle, thick, so that they do not bend when pressed upon the tooth with them: they should be as you see of thick handles, so that when you grip with them, they do not give way, nor bend: of short tips, and they should be made of Indian iron, or of steel properly made, with straight tips: and in their tips there should be teeth, some of which enter into others, and grip with proper, strong constraint.
And sometimes the tips are made like the form of the mouth of a bird which is called a stork, and they are of strong grip.
¶ On the Extraction of Teeth and Removal of Broken Jaw Bones.
Chapter 31.
When something remains broken during the extraction of a tooth, then it is necessary to place cotton with butter on the spot for one or two days, until the area softens: then insert the forceps, or pliers whose tips resemble the beak of a bird called the stork.
This is the form of the forceps.
Let their tips be made like a file on the inside: or like the aliskilfegi.
If therefore it does not yield to extraction with these forceps, then it is necessary that you hollow out above the root, and expose all the flesh, and insert the instrument which is called a small lever, whose form is this.
Let it be short at the tip, somewhat thick: and not be absorbed, so that it does not break.
If therefore the root comes out by that means, it is good: and if not, one must assist with these other instruments whose forms are these.
Form of the first with triangular tip, in which there is some thickness.
Form of the thin triangular tip.
Also one is aided by this instrument having two branches, whose form is this.
And with other instruments and iron tools which preceded in the scraping of teeth.
Also there is help with this instrument which resembles a large hook, of this form, with a twisted triangular tip, in which there is some thickness, so that it does not break: and let it not be absorbed.
And know that dental instruments are many: and similarly the remaining instruments are not counted, and the prudent craftsman, skilled in his art, finds by himself an instrument, according to what the operation and the illnesses themselves indicate to him.
For among illnesses there are those for which the ancients did not prescribe an instrument, because of the diversity of their species.
If however a bone from the jaw breaks, or from other bones of the mouth, or decays: then examine it in its place with what is suitable for it from the instruments and forceps which I mentioned in the extraction of roots, and assist with the gesti, whose form is this.
Let there be in it some thickness gradually, so that the bone may be gripped, or held with it, and not escape and go away, until the bone comes out: and restore the place with medicines suitable for that.
If however there is small decay in the bone, then scrape it from its decay, and treat it until it heals.
On the Filing of Teeth Protruding Above Others.
Chapter 32.
When teeth protrude not according to their natural course, the form becomes ugly: and especially when this happens in women, and girls, namely those for sale.
Therefore it is necessary that you consider.
For if a tooth is born after another tooth, and is not located, or its filing is possible, nor its smoothing: then extract it: and if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, whose form is this.
It is similar to a small beak, and is made from Indian iron, with a very sharp tip.
And let your cutting with which you cut it be over many days, both because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that another of the teeth is not moved.
If however it is protruding exposed to its filing: then file it with a file made of Indian [iron], whose form is this.
Let it all be from Indian [iron], and its handle very finely pointed, like the file with which needles are made: with which the tooth should be filed gradually over many days with ease, so that the tooth is not moved and falls: then smooth it, finally scrape it with certain scrapers.
But if it is a tooth, from which a part breaks, and hurts the tongue: then it is necessary that you also file it, until the roughness of that break is removed, and it is made even: and smoothed, and does not hurt the tongue, nor corrupt speech.
¶ On Binding Teeth with Gold Wires
Chapter 33 
When the front teeth suffer concussion and loosening from a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite firmly on what they eat without it falling, and you have treated them with astringent medicines without success, then the clever solution for them is to bind them with gold wire, since silver turns green and deteriorates after some days, but gold never changes. 
And let the wire be medium in thinness and thickness according to the space it must fit between the teeth. 
And indeed the form of binding is thus: take a wire and insert its double between two healthy teeth; then weave with the two ends of the wire between the loose teeth, whether one or more, until you arrive with the weaving up to a healthy tooth on the other side. 
Then repeat the weaving back to the side where you began, and tighten your hand with subtlety and wisdom until it does not move at all; and let your tightening with which you secure the wire be at the roots of the teeth, so it doesn't slip away. 
Then cut off the excess wire ends with scissors, and bring both together, and turn them with a tool, and hide them between the healthy tooth and the loose tooth, so they don't harm the tongue; then leave it bound thus while it remains. 
If however it loosens or breaks, bind them with another wire, and it will stay thus for the entire time. 
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the binding of two healthy teeth and two loose teeth, as you see. 
And sometimes it happens that one tooth or two return after the fall of either in their places, and are retained as we have described, and remain; and none does this except a skilled, prudent, and experienced craftsman. 
And sometimes something from cow bones is fitted, and is made into the form of a tooth, and is placed in the spot from which the tooth was removed and is bound, as we said; and it remains, and serves for a long time. 
¶ On Cutting the Ligament that Occurs Under the Tongue and Prevents Speech
Chapter 34 
Sometimes this ligament that occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, from a wound that has already healed. 
And the operation on it is thus: open the patient's mouth with their head in your lap, and raise their tongue; then cut that sinewy ligament across its width until the tongue is freed from its restraint. 
But if there is any hardness and knotting in it, and this is from the healing of a wound, then insert a hook into it and split it across its width until you cut the ligament and the knot is loosened. 
And beware lest the cut be too deep in the flesh, and you cut an artery there, and bleeding occurs. 
Then immediately wash the incision with rose water, or with vinegar, or cold water. 
Then place under the tongue a linen plug, which the patient should keep there every night, so it doesn't heal a second time. 
If however bleeding occurs, then place ground vitriol on the spot. 
But if the blood overwhelms you, then cauterize the spot with a lentil-shaped cautery, which is suitable for this; then treat it with the remaining treatment until it heals. 
¶ On Removing the Ranula that Forms Under the Tongue
Chapter 35 
Sometimes an swelling occurs under the tongue that resembles a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural operation; and perhaps it grows so large that it fills the mouth. 
And the operation on it is thus: open the patient's mouth facing the sun, and examine the swelling. 
If therefore you see it is of a dark or black color, hard, which the patient cannot feel, then do not touch it, for it is cancer. 
And if it tends toward whiteness, in which there is moisture, then insert a hook into it and cut it with a fine spatula, and free it from all sides. 
But if blood overwhelms you during your operation, then place ground vitriol on it until the blood is stopped; then return to your operation until you extract it completely. 
Then let it be washed with vinegar and salt; and treat it with the remaining suitable treatment until it heals.
On the treatment of apostemes (abscesses) of the two Tonsils.
Chapter 36. 
Sometimes there occur between the throat godad, that is, glands, which resemble the algodad that occur externally: and they are called the two Tonsils: when you treat them with what we have described in the division, and they do not heal, then consider. 
If therefore the aposteme is hard, of dark color, with little sensitivity: then do not touch it with iron. 
And if it is of red color, and its root is thick: then do not touch it also with iron, fearing blood flow, but rather leave it until it matures. 
And then either you will pierce it: or it will break by itself. 
And if it is of white color, round, and its root is thin: then it needs to be cut. 
And the operation on it is that you should consider before its operation, if its hot aposteme has completely quieted, or has diminished somewhat: then therefore have the patient sit facing the sun, and his head should be in your lap: and open his mouth, and let a servant take between his hands, and press his tongue down with an instrument, whose form is this. 
Let it be made from silver, or bronze, which should be thin like a small knife. 
When therefore you press the tongue with it, and the aposteme is shown to you, and your sight falls upon it: then take a hook, and fix it in the tonsil, and draw it outwards as much as you can, without pulling with it anything from the membranes. 
Then cut it with an instrument, whose form is as follows. 
It resembles scissors: however its ends should be curved, so that the orifice of each of them is directly opposite the other sharply: hence ensure that it is made from Indian iron, or soaked alfuled. 
If however this instrument is not present, then cut it with a spatula, whose form is this. 
Let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side. 
And after you cut one tonsil, cut the other according to this same type of incision. 
Then after the incision let the patient gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water. 
But if bleeding occurs, let him gargle with water in which pomegranate peels have been boiled: or myrtle leaves, and similar astringent things, until the blood flow is stopped: then treat him until he is healed. 
And sometimes other apostemes grow in the throat, besides the two tonsils. 
Cut them therefore according to what I said in the incision of the two tonsils equally. 
And I indeed once treated a woman for an aposteme, which had grown between her throat, which tended toward darkness, with little sensitivity, which had already nearly closed the throat: and the woman was breathing through a narrow passage, and it had already prevented her from eating, and drinking water: and she would have reached death, if she had remained one day, or two days. 
And the aposteme had ascended so much, that it had sent out two branches, until they came out above the two nostrils. 
I hurried therefore hastily, and fixed a hook in one of them. 
Then I drew it, and a large portion was drawn from it. 
Then I cut it off, where I had grasped it from the nostril. 
Then I did the same to what had proceeded from the other nostril. 
Then I opened her mouth, and pressed her tongue: and afterward fixed the hook in the aposteme itself: then I cut off part of it, and only a little blood ran from it: therefore the woman's throat was freed, and she immediately began to drink water, then she took some food. 
Therefore I did not cease to cut from that aposteme many times over a long time: and the aposteme would succeed in the place of what I had cut, until it was prolonged excessively with her. 
Therefore I devised, and cauterized the aposteme between the throat.
