Therefore it stopped from increasing: then I went away, and I did not know what God did with her after me.
On the incision of an abscess of the uvula.
Chapter 37.
When catarrh descends to the uvula, and it becomes swollen, and is long: then it is called perpendicular; and if it is thick and round in its lower part, then it is called grape-shaped.
When it is treated with what we described in the section, and the treatment does not help: and you see that the hot swelling has subsided from it, and it is thin: then it is necessary to cut it.
And that which of it is collected round, and has no length, and is bloody, or of dark color, or black, and has no feeling: then you must avoid cutting it.
For in it there is danger to the patient.
Therefore when you see it according to the manner I described with whiteness and length, you should have the patient sit facing the sun: and press his tongue with the instrument, whose description preceded: then fix a hook in the uvula, and pull it downward: and cut it with one of the two instruments which I mentioned in the incision of the two tonsils.
And you should not cut from it except that which extends beyond the natural limit without addition.
For if you cut more from it, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after the incision use what we described in the incision of the two tonsils: and treat it until it heals.
But if the patient is frightened of the incision: then you must use skill in cauterizing it without fear and dread.
And the method of cautery in it is only with sharp water: and it is that you place the head of the patient in your lap.
Then press his tongue with the instrument we mentioned.
Then take from the water which I described to you in the chapter on cauterizing, and mix with it unslaked lime, and place it neither thick nor thin, and fill with it the cavity of this instrument, whose form is this.
Let its extremity in which the medicine is placed have a cavity like the cavity of a probe's spoon.
And place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself: and let the patient lie on his side, so that saliva may run from his mouth from the internal medicine, so that none of it descends to his throat and harms him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine.
And press it on the uvula for half an hour, until you see it has become black, and the stinging of the medicine has subsided.
And if you wish to take cotton, and wrap it around the end of the probe, and soak the cotton with the medicine, and insert the probe with the cotton in the tube from above, until the cotton adheres to the uvula.
Do that many times, until you achieve what you want from cauterizing the uvula.
Then leave it: for it will dry up and fall off after three or four days.
If you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it: and after cauterization wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in butter, and clean what is around it from the medicine: and let it be rinsed with cold water.
And let it be treated externally with embrocations, and internally with gargles, until it heals.
This type of treatment is therefore safer than incision and further from danger.
And sometimes the uvula is also treated with what is more subtle than cautery and incision: for it is treated with fumigation, according to this method.
Take calamint, and hyssop, and oregano, and rue, and sseah, and chamomile, and southernwood, and similar herbs: and gather them all, or some of them in a pot, and submerge them with vinegar, and let them boil: and let the pot be properly sealed with clay: and let there be in the middle of the pot a hole, over which a hollow instrument is placed according to this method.
Let it be made from silver or brass: and let the extremity, in which there is a pomegranate, be inserted into the patient's mouth, until the vapor rises to the uvula through the tube, until the uvula becomes quite dark.
Then repeat over it many times, until it dries up.
And beware, do not perform this treatment at the beginning of the appearance of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling: and you should not do it except during the decline of its hot swelling.
If however this instrument is not available to you, then take a reed, and attach an eggshell at its end: so that the patient's mouth is not burned: since the eggshell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth: and this is among the good treatments with safety.
¶ On the extraction of thorns and bones from the throat.
Chapter 38.
Often a bone, or fish bone, or something else gets stuck in the throat.
Therefore you should extract what is visible of these upon which sight falls, after you press down the tongue with an instrument in sunlight, and what is in the throat becomes clear to you.
And what is not visible to you and is hidden in the throat, you should make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach.
For perhaps the attached thing will come out through vomiting.
Or let the patient swallow a piece of turnip, or lettuce root, or swallow a morsel of dry bread: or take a piece of soft dried sea sponge: and tie it with a thread, then let him swallow it.
When therefore it reaches the place where the thorns or bone are, and is withdrawn, then it comes out.
If therefore it does not come out with what we have mentioned: then use a lead instrument according to this form.
Let it be somewhat thicker than a probe, and at its end let there be a curve, which he should insert into his throat with ease: and he should have his head raised upward.
And he should beware of touching his epiglottis, lest coughing occur, and push the bone or thorn with it, or let the physician insert it with his hand.
Indeed it is better that the patient insert it: because of his knowledge of the location of the attached thing: and push downward, or pull his hand with the instrument upward, all that according to the amount that is prepared for him, until it comes out.
¶ On the extraction of Leeches.
Chapter 39.
When a leech is treated with what I mentioned in the division about treatment with medicines, and it does not help, then examine the patient's throat in front of the sun, or by the sun after you press his tongue with the instrument I described to you.
If therefore your sight falls upon the leech, extract it with a small hook, or with fine forceps appropriately.
But if it is not possible with these: then take a hollow reed, and insert it into the patient's throat up to near the throat: then insert into the hollow of the reed an iron heated with fire.
Do this many times: and let the patient abstain from water all day.
Then let him take a glass full of cold water, and open his mouth in it: and let him rinse his mouth with it, and not swallow any of it: and let him move the water one hour after another with his hand.
For the leech immediately falls when it senses water.
If however it does not come out with what we have described, then fumigate the patient with bedbugs, or with asafoetida using the instrument I described for fumigation of the uvula.
Do this many times, for it will fall.
And the method of operation in fumigation is that you take a pot, in which there are live coals with fire: and let the pot be covered with a lid, in whose middle there is a hole: and let the end of the instrument be placed in that hole.
Then cast the fumigation, and let the patient put his mouth on the end of the reed, and close his mouth, so that the fumigation does not escape, until you know that the fumigation has already reached the leech.
For the leech will immediately fall.
But if it does not fall, then repeat the fumigation many times.
And let the patient endure thirst, and eat salty things, and garlic, and not drink water.
It is therefore necessary that it come out with this regimen.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech is extracted from the throat, when sight falls upon it.
It is similar to forceps, as you see: except that it has this curve which is inserted into the throat.
And its end is similar to the mouth of a bird which is called a stork, in which there is the roughness of a file: when it grasps something, it does not let it go at all.
On the opening of Abscesses, and their incision, and treatment in general.
Chapter 40.
There are many types of abscesses, of many kinds, according to what has been mentioned about them, namely one by one in this book: and they differ in their perforation and incision in two ways.
One of these is from the type of abscess itself, and what it contains of fluids.
And the second way is because of the places in the body where it occurs.
For an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the anus: and an abscess occurring in a fleshy place is different from an abscess occurring in the joints: and each of them has its own judgment regarding treatment.
And among abscesses there are those which should not be opened except after the maturation of pus in them and its completion: and among them are those which should be opened while they are raw and not fully matured.
Like abscesses that are near to joints.
For when an abscess occurs near a joint and its existence is prolonged until what is around it putrefies, perhaps the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted: which is a more immediate cause of contraction of that limb.
Or it is an abscess near a principal member: for if you delay its opening until it matures, it will harm either it or through it the principal member.
Or it is near the anus, therefore pierce it while immature: and you should not pierce a raw abscess of incomplete digestion, except what is near the anus, so that it does not putrefy deeply and extend to the interior of the anus, and become a fistula, or reach a point where it cannot be healed.
You should know the time for opening an abscess that is fully matured: and it is upon the settling of pain, recession of fever, and diminution of redness and pulsation, and sharpness of the head of the abscess: and other signs.
And you should make the same perforation in the lower part of the abscess and at its most prominent point: and let the perforation go in the length of the body, if the abscesses are in parts of the hands and feet, and in places of muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries, and finally in all even places which have no bend.
In those that do bend, proceed with the perforation according to that member.
When the abscess is in fleshy places: then it is better to postpone its perforation until its digestion is confirmed, as we said, to completion.
For if you pierce it before that, the flow of virus from it is prolonged, and there is much filth and dirt, and perhaps its lips and its depth become hardened.
And certain abscesses are perforated according to the width of the body when necessary: or according to what the member needs.
And you should administer in small abscesses a small perforation, and in large abscesses a wide perforation: or many incisions according to the similarity of the size of the abscess.
And there are among abscesses those in which it is necessary to hollow out the skin: and cut it when it has already become like cloth, and has reached the point where it is already dead: like that which occurs in many, such as dubelet and buboes.
And among them are those which are cut with a triangular incision.
And among them is that from which is cut like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Like the abscess of the groin.
And among them are those in which a round incision and a lunar incision are administered: and similar ones from incisions.
That which has no head, like superficial smooth abscesses, you should pierce it with a superficial or flat perforation only.
And it is necessary, when the abscess is large and has already collected much pus, and you pierce it, that you do not hurry and extract all the pus at that time: rather extract a portion of it.
Then plug the abscess until another day.
Then extract some more pus again.
Do this many times gradually until it comes out: especially if the patient is weak in strength, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or a decrepit old man: for the vital spirit often leaves the body with the release of pus: and perhaps the patient dies, and you do not perceive it.
Therefore be very careful with this chapter.
And after your perforation, with which you pierce these abscesses, you should clean the wound: and observe.
If therefore the wound of the abscess is small, or the incision is flat, then apply lint made from linen or old cotton.
And if the abscess is large, and there are many fissures from the perforation, then it is necessary to insert lint into every fissure, until they meet each other.
And if in the abscess you have cut away part of the skin, or hollowed it out around the circumference: then you should fill it with old cotton, or as when cloth is woven, or with scraped flax fiber without moisture: and bind it until the third day.
Then remove it, and treat it with whatever ointment is necessary, until it heals.
If however bleeding occurs at the time of your operation, then apply cold water and vinegar, after a cloth has been soaked in them: and place it over the site of bleeding many times.
If however the bleeding persists, then you should administer the regimen, and treatment, and powders, which I have described in many places of this book of ours, and from the division.
If however it is in winter time, and the site of the abscess has many nerves: then you should soak the compresses with warm wine and oil: and place them over the areas.
And if it is in summer, and the areas are fleshy: then you should place compresses soaked in water and oil, or with wine and oil, and all of that cold.
Until when it will be the third day, as we have said, then you should loosen the abscess, and clean it, and administer in its treatment what is suitable for it, until it heals.
This therefore is what is necessary to know about the treatment of abscesses in general.
According to the path of distinction, I have already spoken about every abscess, how there is a way to its treatment chapter by chapter individually.
On the incision of abscesses that occur in the skin of the head.
Chapter 41.
Small abscesses occur in the skin of the head, and they are of the types of Nodes, which are contained by tunics that are like sacs to them: as if they were like the crop of a chicken.
And their types are many.
Among them are fatty ones: and among them are those that contain moisture similar to mud: and among them are those that contain moisture similar to ground grain and porridge: and similar things, and among them are those that are stone-hard.
And in the incision of all of them, and their extraction there is no fear, as long as during their incision no artery presents opposition.
And the procedure in cutting them is that you examine them with an instrument, whose form comes after this, which is called the Probe, until you know what it contains.
For if it is that which contains fluid, then cut it with an incision along the length with a superficial cut: according to this form.
And begin with the incision from line b. to line g. Then when the fluid is evacuated, strip away the cyst which contains that fluid, and do not leave any of it at all.
For many times it returns, if anything of it remains.
Then submerge cotton in Egyptian ointment, if it is available: and if not, in water and salt: and fill the wound with it, and leave it until another day.
For it will corrode what remained of the cyst.
Then repeat the cotton with Egyptian [ointment] a second and third time, and if you need it, until you are confident that nothing remains of the cyst, then treat the wound with ointment until it heals.
But if the abscess contains a fatty node: then cut it according to this form, with an incision having the form of a cross: and insert hooks between the two lips of the incision, and strip it away from all sides: and concentrate your strength in extracting the tunic which contains it.
But if an artery presents opposition to you, then do what I will tell you later.
And similarly do in the case of an abscess, if it is stony, with the same incision and treatment, according to what we have said.
And the incision upon a stony abscess is easier: since it has little blood and moisture.
And sometimes there are certain of these abscesses which are in the head in some people, with which there is absolutely no moisture.
That is: because I cut into an abscess in the head of an old woman, and found the abscess like a solid and hard stone, rough, white, which could not be broken: and if someone were struck by throwing it, it would wound them.
In other abscesses occurring in the head, besides these namely abscesses which occur in the heads of children, and near the roots of the ears, the incision of all of them should be a superficial incision.
Or place their perforation always at their lower part, so that the flow of pus is eased downward.
Then treat them with what is suitable for them in terms of treatment.
On the Excision of Scrofulas in the Neck.
Chapter 42.
These swellings often occur in the neck, and under the armpits, and in the groins: and sometimes they occur in the rest of the body.
And those of them that occur in the neck are single or multiple: and they generate from one another: and every Scrofula among them is within its own membrane, as is the case with nodes and swellings of the head, as we have described.
And the types of these Scrofulas are many.
And among them indeed some are stone-like: and some of them contain fluids: and some of them are malignant, which do not respond to treatment.
Therefore, for the one among them that you see to be of good condition to the touch, whose appearance is close to skin color, and moves in all directions, and is not attached to the nerves of the neck, nor to the jugular, nor to the artery, nor is deep: you should cut it with a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body from line b. to line g. and separate it from all sides: and stretch the two lips of skin with a hook, or with many hooks, if you need that, as we said regarding swellings of the head, and extract it gradually.
And one must be cautious not to cut a vein or nerve.
And let the spatula not be too sharp: so that it does not slip in your hand during cutting, or disturb the patient, and you cut what is not necessary.
If however you cut a vein, or artery, or it prevents you from operating, then place ground vitriol in the wound, or certain powders which stop blood, and bind the wound, and leave it until the acuteness of the swelling subsides, and the wound softens: or it begins and wants to putrefy.
For the blood is stopped at once. Therefore return to your operation, until you complete it.
Then investigate with your index finger if any other small Scrofulas remain: therefore cut them out and clean them.
But if there is a large vein at the root of the Scrofula: then you should not cut that Scrofula from its root: rather you should tie it with a strong doubled thread, and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old cotton, which you have already immersed in Egyptian ointment.
Then treat it.
If the whole Scrofula is cut out, then you should bring together the two lips of the wound: and suture it immediately, after you see that absolutely no excess remains.
If however you see that excess remains due to the size of the Scrofula, then you should aim to cut its root, and extract it: and apply the suture which we mentioned.
And for those Scrofulas which contain fluids, pierce it with a superficial perforation: so that its point of maturation appears to you: and make the perforation from that which follows below the body, as we said.
Then after perforation, apply dressings with ointment, and similar things, to remove what remains of the corruption, until when you see the wound is now clean: then treat it with flesh-generating ointment until it heals.
¶ On the incision of the Epiglottis from an abscess occurring inside the throat.
Chapter 43.
The ancients spoke of this incision in the Epiglottis, and I have not seen anyone in our land who has performed it: and this is the writing of their discourse.
In those having quinsy, the cutting of the Epiglottis should be avoided: since no benefit comes from it: because all the veins and the lung are weak.
But in those who have a hot abscess in the mouth, or throat, or both tonsils, when there is no disease in the airway, it is necessary to use incision of the Epiglottis, to escape from the destruction that comes from suffocation.
Therefore when we wish to do this, we should cut the Epiglottis below three rings of the Epiglottis, or four, with a small incision across the width, in that which is between two rings by such an amount, that the cut is in the membrane, not in the cartilage: and this indeed is the suitable place for cutting, since it is devoid of flesh, and blood vessels are far from it.
But if the physician is timid, then he should stretch the throat skin with a hook.
Then split the skin, until when he reaches the airway: avoid blood vessels, if he sees any of them.
Then cut the membrane which we described: and the cutting of the airway is indicated by phlegm which comes out of it, with that which bursts forth, and loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
When therefore the time passes in which suffocation is feared, bring together the two lips of the skin wound, and sew it alone without the cartilage.
Then apply medicines which cause flesh to grow, until it heals.
Albucasis says:
The sum of this discourse which we have related, the explanation is only when they see that one of these abscesses has already blocked the throat of the sick person: and the sick person has been raised above death, and his breath wants to be cut off: they proceed to cut the Epiglottis, so that the sick person may breathe through the wound site with some breathing, and be saved from death: and because of this they instructed to leave the wound open, until the malignancy of the disease is consumed, and its malignancy is three days, and almost the same: then therefore they instructed to sew the wound, and treat it until it heals.
And that which I saw myself is that a maid took a knife, and put it in her throat, and cut with it part of the lung's airway.
And I was called to treat her, and I found her moaning, like the moaning of the dying.
I thus uncovered the wound, and found the blood which had come out from the wound was little.
I was thus confident that she had not cut a vein, nor the jugular: and wind was coming out of the wound.
I hurried therefore, and sewed the wound, and treated it until it was healed: and nothing happened to the maid, except hoarseness in her voice: and after days she returned to her better conditions.
Hence therefore I say: that in cutting the Epiglottis there is no fear.
¶ On cutting the Hernia of the throat.
Chapter 44.
This Abscess named Hernia of the throat, is a large abscess, according to the color of the body: and it is common in women.
And it is of two types: for either it is natural: or it is accidental.
In the natural certainly there is no remedy.
But the accidental is of two types: of which one is similar to fatty nodes.
And the other type is similar to an abscess which occurs from knotting of the artery.
And in it there is danger, and one should not make an attempt at it with iron, except in that which is small: and you test it, and investigate it with a probe.
If therefore you find it similar to a fatty node, and it is not suspended with any of the veins: then cut it, as nodes are cut: and extract it with that which contains the cyst, if it is in a cyst: and if not, then search out all of it.
Then treat the area with what is necessary from treatment, until it heals.
¶ On the surgical treatment of various types of Nodes
Chapter 45.
There are many types of Nodes, and I have already described all their types in the classification: and here I must explain the difference between a Node and an Abscess, since it can cause confusion.
Therefore I say that with an Abscess there is heat, and fever, and severe pains, until the boiling of the superfluity subsides, and putrefaction is complete: for then the fever and heat cease.
With a Node however there is no heat, nor fever, nor pains, and they are contained within a membranous cyst, which is their proper sac, and it is the same color as the body: and they begin the size of a chickpea, and become like a melon, and larger and smaller.
And they are of two types: either fatty, or containing fluid.
And the colors of the fluid are many, according to what I said in the classification.
Therefore, when you come to treat a Node, you must examine it and investigate it first with an instrument called the Probe, according to the form that will be shown in the chapter after this.
And the method of investigating swellings and all Nodes is that you take this instrument and insert it into the most fluid place that you find in the swelling: and you rotate your fingers with it gradually until you know that the instrument has penetrated the skin.
Then advance your hand according to the size of the swelling.
Then withdraw the Probe and examine what comes out in its track.
If therefore fluid comes out, whatever color it may be: then cut it with a superficial incision, according to what I said regarding other swellings.
And if no fluid comes out in the track of the Probe, then know that it is fatty: therefore cut over it with a cruciform incision, according to this form, as I taught you: and hold it with hooks, and carefully separate the skin from all sides: and preserve the cyst if you can, so that you may extract it intact with the Node.
But if the cyst ruptures during the operation, and it is not possible to extract it intact, for this often happens, then extract it piece by piece until none of it remains.
For if any of it remains, whether little or much, the Node usually returns.
But if it defeats you, and a little remains: then fill the wound upon your completion with some sharp corrosive powders: and bind the wound, and place upon it what calms a hot swelling, and treat it with the remaining treatment until it heals.
If therefore the Node is large, then suture its two edges, and treat it with what causes flesh to grow.
But if you encounter a pulsating or non-pulsating vein: then hurry and fill the place with ground vitriol, and leave it bound for two or three days, until the wound putrefies and the blood's boiling subsides: then return to cutting what remained of the Node.
¶ On the forms of instruments for cutting and perforation.
Chapter 46.
Among these are the forms of probes, and they are of three types.
Of these, one is large, one is medium, and one is small.
They are made of iron with squared ends appropriately, so that they may quickly enter into abscesses.
And these are the forms of probes, and of perforating instruments: and one of them is called beris, that is, perforator: and they are of three types.
Of these indeed is large, medium, and small.
They are suitable for investigating abscesses, lesions, fistulas, and swellings from what lies between them in the bones.
And others are made round, smooth, polished, like large needles with which cushions are sewn, made from brass, or from bronze, or iron, or silver.
And the best of them are those made from brass.
And probes are also made from black lead, which are suitable for examining fistulas that have bends in their cavity, so that they may bend with their smoothness along those curves.
And again they are of three types.
For among them are long ones, medium ones, and short ones, according to the size of the width of the fistula and its narrowness.
The form of hooks, and they are of many types.
For among them are simple ones, namely those which have one curve: and they are of three types as you see, Large, Medium, and Small.
And among them are blind hooks: and they are of three types.
And among them are hooks having two curves, and they are of three types: and among them are hooks having three curves, and they are of three types: and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the forms of scalpels, with which nodes and abscesses are cut and excoriated: and they are of three types: for among them is large, medium, and small.
Their ends with which cutting is done are sharp, the other ends are not sharp: and they are not made thus, except that with them help may be given in the excoriation of nodes when there is fear of cutting a vein or nerve: and that through them the patient may be healed, and find some relief from the burning which occurs during the excoriation of an abscess.
These are probes (Magda), and they are of three types.
They are made of bronze, and are similar to the stick with which kohl is applied: and in the broad end there is a hidden spatula point: and in it runs to the interior and to the exterior when you wish, as you see.
The form of spatulas which are hidden between the fingers when perforating abscesses: and patients do not perceive them: and they are of three types, large, medium, and small.
The forms of cupping vessels with which blood flow is stopped: and they are of three types, large, medium, and small.
They are made of bronze or brass, round, somewhat elongated, as you see: and they should be rather thin.
And it is necessary that these types of cupping vessels be available to you, large and small: so that you may stop blood flow quickly when necessary, and then when medicine is not present to you.
However, do not use them for stopping blood in every part of the body, and do not use them except in fleshy places: such as the muscles of the leg, and thigh, and muscles of the arms, and breasts, and belly, and groin, and similar to these, from among the fleshy, moist muscles.
And sometimes small instruments are made from these similar to pistachio shells, according to their form: with which blood is stopped when it flows from the place of bloodletting, or during the cutting of a vein or artery: and sometimes they are made round according to this form.
On the treatment of men's breasts which resemble women's breasts.
Chapter 47.
Sometimes the breasts of certain men swell at the completion of sleep (that is, when they begin to have nocturnal emissions) until they resemble women's breasts: and they remain swollen in an unsightly way.
Therefore, whoever abhors this condition should cut above the breasts with a lunar incision, according to this form from line b. to line g. Then strip away all the fat.
Afterward, fill the wound with flesh-generating medicine.
Then bring together the two lips of the wound with sutures, and treat it until it heals.
If however the breast droops downward and becomes soft due to its size, as happens in women: then it is necessary to make two incisions on its upper sides similar to the lunar figure, each of which connects with the other at both ends, so that the larger line contains the smaller one, according to this form from line b. to line g. Afterward strip away the skin that is between the two lips, and remove the fat, and apply what we mentioned about sutures and powders, which is necessary for treatment, until it heals.
If however you cannot complete the incision of what you need to cut, due to the patient's restlessness, or due to blood flow: then you should fill the wound with cotton soaked in corrosive ointment, and leave it until it corrodes what remains of the fat.
Then treat it until it heals.
On the perforation of swellings that occur under the armpit.
Chapter 48.
These swellings that occur under the armpit are of the types of hard scrofula which contain cysts, and some of them contain fluids.
Therefore those of them which contain fluids should be cut with a lunar incision, according to this form, from line b. to line g. When therefore all the pus that is in the swelling is evacuated, then fill it with old cotton, and leave it until another day.
Then remove the cotton, and treat it with suitable ointment until it heals.
But if the swelling is of the type of scrofulous nodes, then cut into it, just as we described in the section on scrofula similarly.
If however the wound extends to emit pus, then apply cautery to it, according to what we described previously.
On cutting swellings that occur due to arteries and veins.
Chapter 49.
When an artery ruptures, and the skin above it heals over, then often a swelling occurs from this: and similarly it happens with veins also, that swelling and inflammation occur in them.
And the signs by which it is known that the swelling and inflammation are due to an artery and vein are: that when the swelling is due to an artery, it is long, gathered in the depth of the body: and when you push the swelling with your fingers, you feel as if it has a whistling sound.
And that which is due to a vein is round and visible on the body's surface.
And cutting into this type of swelling is an error, especially that which is under the armpits, and groin, and neck, and in many places of the body: and it is very dangerous.
Therefore its treatment with iron [surgical instruments] should be avoided, and those of them which are in the extremities, or in the head, should be avoided.
Therefore for that which is from inflation of the artery's opening, make an incision in the skin lengthwise.
Then open the incision with hooks, then the artery: and free it from its coverings, until it is exposed.
Then introduce a needle under it, and make it penetrate to the other side, and tie the artery with a double thread in two places, according to what I taught you in the extraction of the two arteries which are in the temples.
Then puncture the place which is between the two ligatures with a spatula, until all the blood which is in it comes out, and the swelling is resolved.
Then administer treatment which generates pus, until the ligatures fall off: afterward treat it with ointments suitable for that, until it heals.
If there is an abscess due to cutting a vein, then you should hold with your hand as much of the abscess with the skin as you can.
Then insert a needle under the place which you held with your hand, and make it penetrate: and let there be a double thread in it, until you draw it out from the other side.
Then bind the abscess with this thread with a good binding: according to what I told you about binding a grape, from two parts with the two ends of the thread.
If however you fear that the thread might come out, then insert another needle with another thread under the whole abscess near the first needle's incision: press, and tighten your threads in four places.
Then cut the abscess in its middle, until what is inside comes out: then cut away the excess skin, and leave what is bound from it.
Then place upon it a compress which you have soaked in wine and oil.
Then administer treatment, which is done with lint and ointments, until it heals.
¶ Concerning the abscess which occurs from twisting of Nerves.
Chapter 50.
Just as an abscess occurs in Artery and Vein, similarly an abscess occurs in Nerves: which occurs in them from a blow, or occurs from excessive work, and similar things: and it happens most often in those places of the upper arms, or heel, and in every place where joints move: and it is a serious abscess.
Its color resembles the rest of the body, and is in most conditions without pain: and when it is pressed firmly, the patient feels something like numbness in it.
And it is not an abscess gathered in the depth of the body, but rather is under the skin, and it moves to every part, and does not go forward, or ahead, nor backward to the rear.
Therefore those of them that are in joints, should not be invaded with iron.
For perhaps contraction will occur.
But in that which is of them in the head, or in the forehead, split the skin with a spatula.
If therefore the abscess is small, then hold it with forceps, and cut it off from the root.
And if it is large, then suspend it with a hook, and flay it: then remove it, and join the wound with suture: and treat it until it heals.
¶ Concerning the cutting of an abscess, which occurs in the belly.
Chapter 51.
There occur in some people many times in their bellies, and in other parts of their bodies, warts, which are called Fungal, due to their similarity with mushrooms.
Whose root is thin, and their head is thick, and their edges are turned outward.
And some of them are small: and some of them become very large.
And I indeed have seen a man, and in his belly there were two warts, which were more similar to mushrooms than other things: there was no difference between them, white, of minute root, and their edges were turned outward, and they were split, and moisture always ran from them.
Therefore I cut them, and found in the weight of one about 18 ounces and in the other about 6 ounces And the operation in cutting them is, that you consider then, if the patient is moist, and the color of the wart is white, moist, of thin root: then cut it with a broad spatula, and let there be cauteries in the fire in your presence.
For many times during its cutting much blood is expelled: hurry therefore if blood overcomes you, then cauterize it.
But if you see the patient fearful and afraid of cutting with iron, then take a thread of lead properly made, and bind the wart with it: whose method is what we have said, and leave it for two days.
Then increase in the tightness of the lead.
Therefore do not cease to tighten the lead, as often as it makes an impression in the wart, until it is cut off, and falls by itself without effort.
If however the wart should be of thick root, then in cutting it there is something of fear and dread: especially that which is in the belly.
Nevertheless it is possible, that you cut half of it.
Then cauterize it, so that it does not return.
Then treat it, until the wound heals.
And beware lest you presume to cut a wart which is of dark color, of little sensation, of foul appearance: for it is a cancerous abscess: and I will come with the remembrance of Cancer after this.
On the Treatment of Protruding Navel.
Chapter 52.
A protruding navel occurs from many causes.
Either from tears in the sifac [abdominal wall] which is above the belly: and from it emerges the althub [omentum], or intestine, according to what happens in other ruptures.
Or from blood that flows from a Vein, or from an Artery, according to what was mentioned before.
Or from trapped wind in it.
If however it is due to a tear in the sifac, and exit of the omentum: then the color of the swelling is similar to the color of the body: and it is smooth, without pain: and appears in different positions.
And if it is due to the protrusion of intestine, then its position, with what we have described, is of greater variability.
When therefore you press it with your fingers, it disappears: then returns, and perhaps there is a sound with it: and it greatly increases upon entering the bath, and with vigorous exercise.
If indeed it is due to fluid, it is smooth, then it does not disappear when you press it with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor does it increase.
But if it is due to blood, then with these signs the swelling appears declining toward blackness.
If however it is due to grown flesh: then the swelling is heavy, thick, hard, and fixed in one size.
If indeed it is due to wind, then its touch is smooth.
And the procedure in that case is, that you should consider then, if the protrusion of the navel is due to blood from an Artery, or Vein, or wind: then you must refrain from treating it.
For in that there is fear and dread, as I taught you in the chapter in which I spoke of swellings due to artery and vein.
But if the protrusion of the navel is due to intestine, or omentum: then you must instruct the patient to hold their breath, and stand upright extended.
Then mark with ink around the entire navel.
Afterward command them to lie back between your hands upon their back.
Then encircle with a broad spatula around the navel above the place which you marked with ink.
Afterward extend the middle of the swelling upward, with a large hook.
Then bind it around firmly with thread, or with silk cord in a secure binding: and let the knot of the binding be of the alenxotati type [special surgical knot].
Then open the middle of the swelling extended above the binding.
If therefore you find it has been caught by the binding: then remove the alenxotati, and insert your index finger, and push the intestine to the interior of the belly: and if you find omentum, then take it with a hook, and cut off its excess.
If however an artery or vein opposes you: then encircle it extensively.
Then return to your procedure.
And take two needles, and insert in them two strong threads: and insert the two needles around what you made around the swelling in the form of a cross, which you make penetrate.
Then tighten the swelling in four places above the needles: and if you wish, remove the needles, and leave the place until the extended flesh putrefies: and falls off by itself: or cut it when it becomes thin and putrefied.
Then treat it with what is necessary from ointments, until it heals.
But if the protrusion of the navel is from flesh grown in it, or from fluid: then you must hollow out the swelling, as we said.
And extract the blood, or fluid, which you find in it.
Then treat it with what solidifies the area.
On the treatment of CANCER.
Chapter 53.
We have already discussed in the classification the types of Cancer, and how it may be treated with medicines: and that treatment with iron [surgery] should be avoided, lest it become ulcerated: and we discussed Cancer generated in the womb, and the precaution in treating it.
And the ancients said that sometimes Cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible: like Cancer that is in the breasts, or in the hip, and similar ones, from members situated for extracting all of it: especially when it is small and beginning.
When it is old and large, you should not approach it.
For I have not been able to cure any of it: nor have I seen another before me who achieved that.
And the operation on it, when it is situated as we said, is that you should proceed first and purge the patient of black bile several times.
Then bleed them if you find obvious fullness [of blood].
Then prepare the patient with preparation, in which they are positioned for the operation.
Then cast suitable hooks into the Cancer.
Afterwards hollow it out from all sides with the skin, according to careful examination, until none of its roots remain: and let the blood flow, and do not cut it off quickly, rather compress the areas, and wipe away all the thick blood with your hand, or with whatever instruments are possible there.
If however the flow of thick blood from cutting an artery or vein gives you trouble: then cauterize the vein until the blood is stopped.
Then treat it with the remaining treatment until it heals.
On the treatment of DROPSY.
Chapter 54.
We have already described in the classification the types of Dropsy, and how water accumulates: and the signs of every type, and its treatment with medicines.
And that which is treated with iron is only the ascitic type alone: and the tympanitic type should not be approached with iron: nor the hyposarca type at all: for that is fatal.
When you have already treated this tympanitic type of Dropsy with medicines, and your treatment has not helped: then consider.
For if weakness has already followed in the patient, or there is another illness besides Dropsy: such as if they have a cough, or looseness of the belly, and similar things: then beware of treating them with iron: since that is dangerous.
But if you see the patient has abundant strength, and they have no illness besides Dropsy alone: and is neither a child nor elderly: then the method of operation on them is that you raise the patient standing between your hands, and an assistant behind them compresses their belly with their hand pushing the water to the lower part of the pubic area.
Then take a spiny spatula, according to this form.
Let it be of two sharp parts, long, with a sharp end like a Spatula.
However let there be some gradual shortness in it, so that you do not pass through with it during the operation all the way to the intestine, and harm it.
Then consider.
For if the generation of Dropsy is from the part of the intestines, then you should distance yourself with the incision from the navel by the amount of three fingers, downward in line with it above the pubic area.
If however the generation of Dropsy is due to illness of the liver: then let your incision be to the left of the navel by three fingers.
And if its generation is due to the spleen, then let the incision be on the right side: and also the incision should not be on the side on which the patient wants to lie, so that excess fluids do not run to that weak place.
Then pierce through the whole skin with the instrument.
Then introduce into that incision, and raise your hand with the Spatula between the skin and the peritoneum, as if you are skinning it: and let the amount that is skinned be the amount of a fingernail, or thereabouts.
Then pierce the peritoneum, until the Spatula reaches the empty space: which is the place of the water.
Then remove it, and introduce into the hole an instrument, of which this is the form.
And it is like a small tube: made from silver, or bronze, or brass, smooth, polished: which has a small hole in its lower part, and in its sides are three holes, two on one side, and one on the other side: and its end is cut, in the manner of cutting a reed, as you see: at its upper end is a ring.
For when the instrument reaches the water, then it descends immediately upon the instrument, therefore evacuate half the quantity of water at that time.
Since if you evacuate more of it than necessary, at that moment the patient may die through the dissolution of his vital spirit: or he may suffer a fainting spell that approaches death.
Indeed, evacuate from it according to the measure of his strength, and according to what the patient's conditions indicate to you from his pulse strength, and from its quality.
Then withdraw the instrument, and retain the water.
Which is because it is retained immediately due to the skin that holds the opening which is above the peritoneum: about which I told you to pierce it according to that method.
Then repeat the instrument the next day, if you see the patient can tolerate it: and draw out also a small quantity of water.
Do this for several days without fear, and guard yourself from error: until nothing remains of the water except a little.
But if you are worried about the patient, and you leave much water: then treat him with burial in hot sand, and much sweating in the bath and sun, and let him endure thirst: and treat him with drying medicines, until he is healed: and cauterize above the stomach, and liver, and spleen, after extracting the water: according to what I described in what preceded.
¶ Concerning the treatment of infants who are born with their urinary passages not perforated.
Chapter 55.
When any of the infants emerges from his mother's womb, and the head of his penis is not perforated, or the opening is narrow, or not in its proper place.
Therefore it is necessary to assist with its perforation immediately when he is born with a very fine probe, according to this form.
Then place in the opening a thin nail of lead, and bind it: and keep it there for three or four days.
Therefore when he wants to urinate, let it be removed, and let him urinate.
Then replace it.
And if you do not place the lead, it is indeed possible that the urine which flows over the area will not allow it to close.
He whose opening is narrow is treated with lead, as we said, for many days, until it is widened.
Those in whom the opening is not in its proper place are harmed.
Which is because among those who are born, the opening is at the end of the head of the penis: wherefore he cannot urinate forward, until he lifts the penis with his hand upward.
Nor does he have procreation, because the sperm cannot reach the womb in a straight line: and it is a very foul illness.
And the method of operation in that case is that the patient should lie back upon his back.
Then extend the head of his penis with your left hand with strong extension, and cut the tip of the head of the penis from the place of the priapus, with a sharp point, or sharp probe: like the cutting of a reed, or as you scrape something, so that its middle comes to resemble the head of the penis: and so that the opening falls in the middle as it should.
And guard yourself during your operation from the flow of blood.
For this often happens.
Therefore oppose it with that which stops blood, and treat the wound until it heals.
¶ On Albather (warts) which occur on the foreskin and head of the penis, and on corruption, blackening, and adhesion.
Chapter 56.
These Albather often occur on the penis, and it is an emergence of unsightly flesh.
And of these, some are malignant, and others non-malignant.
For the non-malignant one, you should suspend it with a fine hook, and cut it away until you have cleaned it all away.
Then place upon it cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment.
Then treat it afterward with diafinicon ointment until it heals.
But if the Albather is malignant, of foul color, then you should apply cautery to it after cutting and scraping it.
If however the Albather is on the foreskin of one uncircumcised, and part of the Albather is between the foreskin and part of it outside: then you should first remove the Albather that is inside, until when it heals, then treat it externally: because when you treat it all at once, the foreskin is not secured from being perforated.
And blackening and corruption also occur in the testicles and foreskin.
Therefore, you should hollow out all that has already blackened, and what is about to corrupt, or has already corrupted.
Then spread over it afterward honey that has been mixed with powder of pomegranate peels, and sifted ground vetch.
Then treat it with the remaining treatment until it heals.
If however there occurs a flow of blood, then apply cautery with a lunar cautery, according to this form.
For cautery is beneficial for two conditions simultaneously, namely for blood flow, and when the wound becomes corrupt.
But if the head of the penis is corroded, and is completely destroyed in an hour: then you should insert into the urinary passage a small tube of lead, so that the patient may urinate through it.
Regarding adhesion of the foreskin to the head of the penis, and this adhesion indeed does not occur except in one whose foreskin is healthy and circumcision is not required on it: and sometimes adhesion occurs because of a wound or abscess: therefore you should separate it with a blunt spatula until the attachment is loosened, and the head of the penis is freed on all sides.
But if it is difficult to separate them precisely, then you should scrape something from the head of the penis and from the foreskin.
This is because when the foreskin is thin, perhaps it becomes perforated quickly because of its thinness.
Then separate between the foreskin and head of the penis with a thin linen cloth, which has been moistened in cold water, so they do not adhere again.
Then let it be treated with astringent wine until it heals.
On the cleansing of infants, and the treatment of errors occurring to them.
Chapter 57.
Circumcision is not a break in continuity like other wounds: however, since it is done by our operation, by our will, and its practice is specifically for boys, it is necessary that we indicate in it the better operation and the easier way leading to health.
Therefore I say that the ancients did not speak of circumcision in any of their books: since they do not use it in their laws, and it is nothing except what we have acquired through experience.
Which is because I found the greater part of practitioners and phlebotomists using cleansing, circumcising with a razor and with scissors: and they use the thimble, and binding with thread, and cutting with a fingernail.
And I indeed have already tested all these methods, and have not found better than cleansing with scissors and binding with thread.
Since in cleansing with a razor, many times the skin escapes.
Since the skin of the foreskin has two layers: therefore perhaps the upper skin is cut, and the lower membrane remains: thus one is forced to cut to cut the other, and future pain.
And with cleansing which is done through the thimble, that is, the finger-guard which cobblers use, the cutting of the extremity of the male member is not secured: since perhaps it enters into its opening.
In cleansing with the fingernail, perhaps the iron slips and corrupts your operation: and indeed a boy's skin is naturally short, and many times they are born such that they do not need cleansing: and I have seen this.
However, cleansing with scissors and binding with thread, good experience has revealed to me: since scissors are of proportional cutting: because the sharpness which is above is like the sharpness which is below.
Therefore when you press your hand, the two sharp edges are proportioned, and they cut according to one operation in one time: and the compression happens in a similar figure, containing the skin of the penis from all sides, with which error does not occur at all.
And the mode of proper operation is that you make estimate, especially the boy, if he is among those who understand a little, that you do nothing except that you bind a thread on his penis only: and you leave him until another day.
Then make him happy and joyful in every way possible for you, from that which his understanding receives.
Then make him stand between your prepared hands in position, and let him not be sitting: and hide the scissors in your sleeve, or under your foot, lest the boy see them at all, or any of the instruments.
Then introduce your hand to his penis, and blow on the skin, and pull it upward, until the head of the penis comes out.
Then clean it from that which gathers in it of dirt.
Then bind the marked place with a double thread.
Afterward bind a little below it with a second binding.
Then hold with your index finger and thumb the place of the lower binding with good retention, and cut between the two bindings.
Then lift the skin upward quickly, and extract the head of the penis.
Then let the blood run gradually (for it is better) and put powder above: and above the powder a cloth in which has been spread egg yolk cooked in rose water, mixed with fresh good rose oil: and leave it upon him until another day: and treat him with the remaining treatment until he is healed.
However, this is the form of scissors for cleansing.
Let the two extremities be blunt, and let them be cutting: let no branch of nail protrude in them: and let the length of the two sharp edges be like the length of the two handles equally.
Indeed the error falling in cleansing is: since perhaps the inner skin escapes entirely, or part of it during cutting.
Therefore it is necessary that you extend it from your time with your nail before swelling of the place: and cut it according to equality.
But if you cannot hold it with your nail, then extract it with a hook, and cut it.
If three days have passed, and what remains under the penis is swollen and inflamed: then leave it until the hot inflammation subsides, and carefully remove the skin, and cut it according to what is prepared for you: and be careful of the head of the penis.
Indeed this will not harm him.
Therefore treat it with that which consolidates the wound from the powders, which we described in the treatise on powders: and if it is cut from the skin above measure, and escapes to the upper parts, this also does not harm him.
Therefore treat it with what we have said, until it heals.
¶ On the extraction of Urine retained in the bladder.
Chapter 58.
Retention of urine in the bladder occurs from blockage caused by stone, or congealed blood, or pus, or flesh growth, and similar things from those which we mentioned in the types of treatment in the division.
And if the Urine is not released, and you see its retention is because of a Stone, which is now in the neck of the bladder: then the patient should be on his knees bent over.
Then his feet should be placed on his back, and he should extend his belly from the upper part of his back, and the patient should hold his breath as much as possible for him.
Therefore he is compelled with the neck of the bladder to expel the Stone to the posterior: whereby the Urine is released.
But if it is not released with what we have said, and the matter becomes severe for the patient: then you must administer its extraction with an instrument, which is called a syringe, whose form is this: Let it be of silver, and let it be thin, smooth, hollow like a bird's feather quill in the thinness of its shaft, long in the likeness of one and a half palms: which should have a thin tube at its head.
And the method of urine's exit through it is that you should take a doubled thread, and tie wool or cotton at its end with a good knot: and insert the end of the thread in the lower part of the Syringe: and cut with scissors if anything from the wool protrudes, but insert it into the tube with force, like alzer.
Then anoint the syringe with oil, or with butter, and egg white: and have the patient sit on a seat, and bathe the bladder and his penis with moist oils, and olive oil, and warm water.
Then insert the syringe into the penis carefully, until it reaches the root of the penis.
Then double it above from the region of the navel.
Then push the syringe within it, until it reaches the Bladder, and the patient feels that it has already reached an empty space: and this does not happen according to this order, except because the passage, in which it goes, is one in which there is tortuosity.
Then extend the thread with the wool gradually.
For the urine will follow the wool.
Then extract it, and make the Urine flow through the urinary path: and repeat the syringe, and do not cease doing this, until the bladder is emptied, and the patient finds relief.
¶ On the method of administering an enema to the bladder with a Clepsydra.
Chapter 59.
When an ulcer occurs in the bladder, or blood congeals in it, or pus becomes confined in it, and you want to instill waters and medicines into it: then it should be done with an instrument which is called Clepsydra, and this is its form.
For it is deaf, that is, closed.
And in it there are three holes, two on one side, and one on the other side, as you see.
And the hollow space, in which is that which pushes, should be of such size that it blocks itself without addition, until when something is drawn with it, from the fluids it is drawn up: and when it is pushed, with it it is expelled to a distance: just as does the clepsydra, with which naphtha, that is, Greek fire, is thrown in the battles of Mars.
Therefore when you want to inject fluids into the bladder, insert the end of the clepsydra into the fluid, and draw with that which pushes upwards, for the fluid is drawn into the cavity of the clepsydra.
Then insert its end into the penis, according to what we described in the syringe.
Then push the fluid with that which pushes for that fluid reaches the bladder immediately, so that the patient feels it.
Form of the fine clyster with which the bladder is given an enema.
Let it be made from silver, or brass: whose higher end should be similar to a small alcama, and below it a cavity between which the ligament may fall.
Then take a ram's bladder, and put in it the fluid with which you wish to give an enema to the bladder.
Then bind it between two cavities with a strong binding with a double thread: and warm that fluid a little over fire.
Then insert the end of the clyster into the penis, afterwards squeeze your hand over the bladder with the fluid with proper pressure, until the patient feels that the fluid has now reached the bladder.
But if you do not have a bladder present, then take a piece of parchment, and make from it a circle, and perforate it around its whole circumference.
Then insert a strong thread in those holes, as they are wound around.
Then gather the circle, as the alsofratu is gathered: after you put in it what you want from fluids, and oils, and waters.
Then bind it to the instrument: and do with it as you did with the bladder by squeezing with your hand, until the fluid reaches the bladder.
¶ On the extraction of Stone.
Chapter 60.
We have already spoken about the division of types of Stone, and its treatment with medicines: and I have spoken about the difference between Stone generated in the kidneys and bladder, and the signs of all that: and I have spoken about Stone in which operation by hand and cutting is appropriate: and it is Stone generated specifically in the bladder: and that which becomes fixed in the penis: and I am narrating that with clear exposition and brevity.
Therefore I say that Stone generated in the bladder most often occurs in boys.
And among its signs is that urine exits from the bladder similar to water in its thinness: and white sand appears in it, and the patient rubs his penis, and struggles with it, and many times it hangs down.
Then it becomes erect, and the anus comes out with it in many of them.
And the healing of boys is easy, from it until they reach fourteen years.
And it is difficult in old men.
In young men however it is intermediate, in what is between that: and he whose Stone is larger, treatment is easier: and if small, its treatment is the opposite of that.
Therefore when we come to treatment, it is necessary first that we clean out the patient with an enema, which extracts all the feces that is in his intestines: for it sometimes prevents the finding of the Stone during examination.
Then let the patient be taken by his feet, and shaken, and moved downwards: so that the Stone may descend to the bottom of the bladder, or let him jump from a high place several times.
Then make him sit between your hands prepared, and his hands should be under his thighs, so that the whole bladder becomes inclined downwards.
Then examine him, and touch him from the outside.
If therefore you feel the Stone in the space, then proceed immediately with an incision over it.
If it cannot be felt under your touch at all, then you must clean the index finger with oil of your left hand, if the patient is a child: or the middle finger if it is a grown youth: and insert it into his anus, and search for the Stone, until it comes under your finger: and gradually turn it toward the neck of the bladder.
Then press upon it with your finger, and push it outward toward the place where you wish to make the incision: and instruct the assistant to press the bladder with his hand, and instruct another assistant to pull the testicles upward with his right hand: and with his other hand, to remove the skin that is under the testicles in the part from the place where the incision is.
Then introduce the cutting Spatula, whose form is this.
And make an incision in the area between the anus and testicles, and not in the middle, but to the side of the left buttock: and let the incision be over the stone itself, with your finger in the anus: and let the incision be transverse, so that the external cut is wide and internally narrow, according to the size that would allow the Stone's exit, no larger.
Perhaps indeed the finger that is in the anus compresses the Stone at the incision, and it exits without difficulty.
And know that among Stones there are those that have angles and edges, which makes their exit difficult because of that.
And among them are smooth ones, similar to an acorn, and round: and their exit becomes easy.
For those that have angles and edges, increase the incision.
If it does not exit thus, then you must devise a way to deal with it: either grip it with a suitable instrument whose tip is like a Moon, which grips the Stone and doesn't let it escape: or introduce beneath it a thin instrument with a curved end.
If however you cannot manage it, then widen the opening a little.
If some blood overcomes you, stanch it with vitriol.
If the stone is more than one, then first push the large one to the mouth of the bladder.
Then cut over it.
Afterward push the small one after that: and similarly do if there are more than two.
If it is very large, then it is ignorance to cut over it with a large incision.
For one of two things happens to the patient, either he dies, or continuous urinary dripping occurs: because the area does not heal completely.
Rather, manage its expulsion until it exits: or devise a way to break it with forceps, until you extract it piece by piece.
When therefore you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with frankincense, and aloe, and dragon's blood: and bind it, and place over it a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or rose oil, and cold water: to calm the hot swelling.
Then lay the patient back on his neck, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day.
When therefore you have loosened it, bathe the area with water and plenty of oil.
Then treat it with diaphoenicon ointment and basilicon ointment until it heals.
If however there occurs in the wound increased hot swelling, and erosion, and similar things: as if blood congeals in the bladder, and urine is prevented because of it: and this is recognized by the exit of blood with urine.
Therefore insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood.
For if it remains, it leads to corruption of the bladder and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, and water, and salt: and counter each type with what is suitable from the treatment, until it heals.
And it is necessary during all hours of treatment to bind the two thighs together, and gather them, because of the medicines which are placed over the area.
If the stone is small and is lodged in the passage of the penis, and gets stuck there, and blocks the exit of urine: then treat it with what I will describe before you proceed to cutting.
For many times you may be spared from cutting through this treatment, as I have indeed proven it.
And it is that you should take a Perforator made of iron from Africa, which has this form: with a triangular end, sharp, fixed in wood.
Then take a thread and tie the penis with it below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then insert the perforating iron into the penis carefully, until the Perforator reaches the stone itself: and rotate the Perforator with your hand on the stone gradually: and attempt to perforate it until you make it penetrate through the other side.
For the urine is released immediately.
Then press your hand over what remains of the stone on the external part of the penis.
For it breaks and exits with the urine, and the patient is healed.
But if this treatment is not suitable for you, due to something that prevents it: then tie a thread below the stone, and another thread above the stone.
Then cut above the stone in the penis itself between the two ties.
Then extract it.
Afterward, loosen the binding, and clean away the congealed blood that forms in the wound.
And the binding of the thread below the stone is only necessary so that it does not return to the bladder: and the other binding above, only so that when the thread is loosened after the exit of the stone, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound: and because of that it is necessary for you when you tie the upper thread, to lift the skin upward, so that it returns upon your completion and covers the wound, as we said.
¶ On the Extraction of Stones in Women.
Chapter 61.
Stone rarely forms in women.
But if a stone occurs in any of them, then the treatment is difficult and is prevented for many reasons.
One of them is: that the woman perhaps is a virgin.
And the second is, because you do not find a woman who will reveal herself to a doctor, when she is chaste, or among those having husbands.
The third is, because you do not find a woman well-versed in this art, especially operation by hand.
And the fourth is the distance to the location of the stone: wherefore it requires deep cutting, and in that there is fear.
But if necessity drives to that, then it is proper that you take a female physician, well-knowledgeable: and few are found.
But if you are deprived of her, then seek a chaste, skilled physician, and present a midwife well-trained in women's matters, or a woman who in this art suggests a portion of skill: therefore make her present, and instruct her especially, that she do everything that you instruct her regarding the investigation of the stone.
First what is necessary, is that she look if the woman is a virgin: then it is proper that she insert her finger into her anus, and search for the stone.
If therefore she finds it, let her constrain it under her finger, and then instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the vulva of the patient, and investigate the stone, after she places her left hand over the bladder, and compresses it with good pressure.
If therefore she finds the stone, then it is proper that she gradually move it from the orifice of the bladder downward with the full extent of her strength, until she arrives with it at the root of the hip.
Then let her cut above it at the opposition of the middle of the vulva at the root of the hip, from whichever part is prepared for her, and feels the stone in that part: and let her finger not be removed from the stone, constraining under it: and let the cut be small at first.
Then let her insert the probe over that incision.
If therefore she feels the stone, then let her increase the incision according to the proportionate amount that the stone exits from it.
Know that there are many types of stones.
Among these there is small and large, smooth and rough, long and round having branches.
Therefore know its types, so that through this you may be informed about what you want.
If therefore blood overcomes you, then add ground vitriol to the place, and hold it for one hour, until the blood is stopped.
Then return to your operation until the stone comes out: and prepare with you the instruments which I mentioned for extracting stones in men, so that you may be aided by them in your operation.
But if the flow of blood overcomes you, and you know that the discharge of blood is from an artery that has been cut: then put powder on the place, and bind it with bandages with proper binding: and do not change it: and leave the stone, do not extract it.
For perhaps the sick woman will die.
Then treat the wound.
When therefore the sharpness of the blood subsides after days, and the place becomes putrid, then return to your operation until the stone comes out.
¶ On cutting for watery Hernia.
Chapter 62.
A watery hernia is nothing but a collection of fluid in the white sifac [membrane], which is under the skin of the testicle containing the egg: and it is called sifac.
And sometimes it is in its own proper membrane, which nature prepared for it in the part of the egg, until it is thought: that it is another egg, and it is the skin of the testicle between the white sifac, which we mentioned: and this happens only rarely.
And indeed the generation of this hernia is from weakness which happens to the testicles: wherefore this matter flows to them.
And sometimes it happens from a blow to the testicles.
And this fluid has many colors.
For either its color tends toward yellow, or it is bloody red, or murky, black, or it is watery white, and this is what is more common.
And the signs by which it is known where the collection of water is, are these.
For if it is in the white sifac which we mentioned, then the swelling is round with slight length like the shape of an egg: and the testicle does not appear, since the fluid contains it on all sides.
And if the fluid is in its own proper membrane, then the swelling is round with part of the egg, and because of this one thinks that it is another egg.
And if the fluid is between the skin of the testicles and the white sifac, then it falls under perception.
When therefore you want knowledge of the color of the fluid, then test the color of the fluid in the swelling with the square probe, whose form preceded.
According to that therefore which comes out in the trace of the probe, judge what is within it.
When therefore we come to treatment with the iron [surgical instrument], then it is necessary that you order the patient to be bled if that is possible for him, and you see his body is full.
Then let him lie back on his back on something slightly elevated, and place many cloths under him.
Then sit you on his left, and order the assistant to sit on his right, who should extend his penis to one of the two sides of the skin of the testicles, and to the part of the abdomen wall.
Then take a broad spatula, and cut the skin of the testicles from the middle lengthwise, up to near the pubis: and let the cut be according to the straightness of the parallel line which divides the skin of the testicles, until it reaches the containing white sifac: and excise it and observe, or beware of this, that you cut it: and let your excision by which you excise it be from the part which is suspended with the egg more: and carefully scrutinize with the excision according to the amount that is possible for you.
Then perforate the sifac full of water with a wide perforation, and extract all the water.
Then separate between the two lips of the cut with hooks, and extend the sifac upward, and do not touch the containing skin of the testicles: and cut the sifac however it is possible for you to cut it, either in its totality, or piece by piece, especially its thin side.
For if you do not carefully examine in cutting it, there is no security that the water will not return.
But if the egg proceeds outside from its skin at the time of your operation: then when you will have completed the cutting of the sifac, return it.
Then bring together the two lips of the skin of the testicles with suture.
Then treat it like other wounds, until it heals.
If you find the testicle corrupted by another blackness, then you must tie off the vessels which are the suspensory, fearing blood flow.
Then cut off the testicle with its suspensory, and remove the testicle: afterward treat it as we have described.
If water is collected in both parts, then know that there are two hernias: therefore cut the other part just as you did in the first one equally.
And if it is suitable for you to make it one operation, then do so: then wipe away the blood.
And insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil, or in rose oil: and place outside other wool which you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread it over the two testicles and the abdominal wall: and place folded cloths above, which are small cushions: and tie them above with a bandage, which has six [parts] according to this form.
Then let two cloths be taken and sewn, and filled with carded wool, according to this likeness: and let the ends which press be made from cotton, or moist wool: and let them be gathered with tightness in the waistband of the breeches: and let one end extend over the anus up to the back, and the second extend to the pubic region: and two ends over the two thighs below, and two other ends over the two groins, and let all the tightness be gathered in the waistband of the breeches.
And sometimes this hernia is skillfully treated with cautery in place of cutting with iron: that is, take a fine knife-like cautery, and cut the skin of the testicles with it: and it is heated, as we have described: until when the white membrane containing the water is exposed: then take a cautery, according to this form.
It resembles a Greek letter.
Then pierce that membrane with it while it is heated, until all the fluid comes out.
Then stretch the membrane with hooks, and strip it away with a fine heated cautery, and cut it as much as is possible for you, until you remove all of it.
And be careful of the testicle, lest the fire touch it.
And know that this operation is safer, and further from blood flow, and better than the operation with cutting.
If however during the time of cutting, or during the time of cautery, you encounter opposition from a hot swelling, or blood flow, or something else: then you must treat whatever opposes you from that with the treatment that suits it, according to what was previously stated.
If however the patient is fearful, and does not await this operation, because of its horribleness: then perform the perforation according to what I describe: which is.
When it is certain to you through what we said about the signs, that the hernia is watery: then you must have the patient sit on a raised seat.
Then pierce the swelling in its lower part with a broad spatula: and make the perforation according to the length of the body, and let the opening be wide, until all the water runs out.
Then place cotton on the spot, and bind it, and leave it so that the remaining water runs out: then treat it until the wound heals.
If however there is resistance to the water's exit at the time when you pierce it: and this is only because the white membrane creates resistance at the opening of the wound, and prevents the water's exit: then you must insert a quill into the wound, or the instrument with which dropsy water is extracted, whose form was described before: or increase the operation of the wound slightly.
And know that the water returns and collects after six months, or thereabouts.
Therefore when it collects, let it be pierced in the same way, and the patient will pass his days.
¶ On the treatment of fleshy hernias.
Chapter 63.
Know that in cutting into this hernia, there is a fear leading to destruction in most conditions.
Therefore it seems to me better to leave it alone than to operate on it.
And I am recalling the operation for it, and the types of swelling.
I say therefore, that many swellings occur in the bodies from which the testicles are composed: and this happens from many causes, namely:
Either from sharp or acrid excess fluid, which flows down to the testicles.
Or from a blow.
And the color of the swelling matches the color of the body, and no pain is found in it: and it is a heavy swelling.
And perhaps it is a stone-like swelling, whose color is dark, and it has no sensation.
And sometimes it occurs from knotting of arteries, and their inflation: as has already been mentioned before: or from inflation of veins.
That which is from inflation of arteries is recognized by the condition of the swelling, when you compress it with your fingers.
And you should not touch it at all.
That which occurs from inflation of veins does not disperse at all when compressed with fingers.
And it is indeed possible that an incision be made on this type and on the remaining flesh: that is, that you cut the skin of the testicles.
Then pull the egg upward, and extract it with the white membrane: and free the suspensory from the vessels, and tie off the vessels: and cut the suspensory after you free it from every part of the egg parts.
If therefore the egg is consolidated with those grown flesh parts, then you must pull the egg and cut it.
And if there is adhesion between any part of the membrane, or in what is between the vessels: then you must free all that adhesion, and cut it with a circular incision.
If however there is flesh growth in the place of adhesion which is behind: then you must cut all of it, and extract the egg, as we said.
When therefore your operation is complete, then fill the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine: then treat it with the remaining treatment until it heals.
¶ On the treatment of hernia which is with Vine.
Chapter 64.
