Vine is a swelling wrapped in a certain wrapping, similar to a grape cluster, with softening of the testicles: and movement and exercise become difficult for the patient, and walking.
And in the treatment of this illness there is fear, because of that which was mentioned before.
But it is necessary that we describe the operation for it according to what the ancients did.
I say therefore, that you must have the patient sit on an elevated seat.
Then push the suspensory of the testicles downward.
Then hold the skin of the testicles with your fingers, with the vessels that are near the penis: and let another assistant hold them, and stretch them with strong extension.
Then cut with a broad sharp spatula a transverse incision in line with the vessels, until the vessels are exposed.
Then peel back from every part, as we told you in the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
Afterward fix in them a needle in which is a double thread, and tie it in the first of the places where the Vines occur: and tie it again in the last of them.
Then cut them in the middle with a straight incision according to the length of the body, and extract what is collected from the corrupt feculent fluids.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds, in which you want pus to form, so that the vessels which nourish one of the testicles fall off.
For there is no harm in that.
If however Vine occurs in all vessels, then you must extract the testicles with the vessels because of this: so that the egg is not deprived of nourishment, because of the incision, and wastes away, or dries up, and there is no benefit with them.
On the Treatment of Intestinal Hernia.
Chapter 65.
The occurrence of this hernia is from a tear that happens in the peritoneum stretched over the belly in the region of the groin from the abdominal wall.
Therefore the intestines descend from that rupture to one of the testicles.
And this rupture is either from the peritoneum, or from its stretching.
And these two types occur from many causes: either from a blow, or jumping, or shouting, or lifting a heavy object, and similar things.
And the sign, when it is from stretching of the peritoneum:
It is that it happens gradually over a long time, and does not happen suddenly: and the swelling is even towards the depth: because the peritoneum compresses the intestine.
And the sign when it is from a tear in the peritoneum, is that it causes from its beginning a sudden severe pain: and the swelling is irregular, appearing close under the skin: and that is because of the exit of the intestine, and its path to the outside of the peritoneum.
And sometimes the omentum comes out with the intestine: and this is called intestinal and omental hernia: and sometimes it occurs with gas.
And sometimes feces run into the intestine, and are retained there: and with this is the patient's peril: since it causes terrible pain, and especially when pressed.
And in the treatment of the types of this disease with iron [surgery] there is fear: therefore one must be cautious of mishap in it.
And the method of operation is that you instruct the patient to return the intestine with his hand to the interior of its cavity, if it submits to return.
Then he should lie back upon his neck between your hands: and raise his legs.
Then stretch the skin that follows the skin upwards, and cut the whole skin of the testicles lengthwise.
Then fix hooks in the two lips of the incision, according to what you need to open the incision with them: and let the incision be of such size that it is possible for an egg to come out through it.
Then strip away the covering membrane and what is under the skin of the testicles, until when the white, hard peritoneum is exposed from all sides: then insert your index finger into what follows the testicle, into what is between the white peritoneum, which is under the egg's skin, and between the second peritoneum, and free with it the adhesion which is in the posterior part of the egg.
Then move the skin of the testicles inward with your right hand: and with this pull the white peritoneum upward with your left hand: and lift the egg with the peritoneum to the part of the incision: and instruct the assistant to pull the egg upward: and you release the adhesion which is behind with complete separation.
And search with your fingers so that there is not anything there from the intestine wrapped in the hard white peritoneum.
But if you find something of it: then push it down into the belly.
Then take a needle in which there is a thick thread, made into a wick from ten threads: and insert it at the end of the peritoneum, that is, of the membrane which is under the skin of the testicles which follows the incision: then cut the ends which protrude from the thread, until there are four threads.
Then arrange some of them over others in the form of a cross: and tie with them the peritoneum, of which we spoke, which is under the skin of the testicles with a strong ligature, until none of the vessels which nourish them can send anything to them: so that no hot swelling occurs from it.
And place again a second ligature outside the first ligature, distant from it less than two fingers.
And after these two ligatures leave from the peritoneum, which is under the skin of the testicles, the quantity of the size of a finger: and cut all the rest according to the circle, and remove the egg from it.
Then cut in the lower part of the skin of the testicles an incision, from which blood and pus flows: as we narrated in what was presented before.
Then apply wool soaked in oil, and place in the wound: and apply the ligature which we described.
And sometimes the white peritoneum which we mentioned is cauterized, after its incision fearing the flow of blood.
For many times that happens.
And leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling is delayed, then foment it with hot water: and place upon it that which we mentioned, until it falls.
Then treat the wound with the remaining treatment of wounds, until it heals.
¶ Concerning windy hernia.
Chapter 66.
In this type of hernia, I have not seen anyone who dared to treat it with iron [surgical instruments].
And indeed the ancients said that it is done as we described in the hernia which is with the vine.
That is, that you should bind the vessels after cutting above them with care below: then cut in the middle, and finally treat it with what opens it, until the vessels fall off: and treat the wound, according to what we said, until it heals.
¶ Concerning the rupture which occurs in the groin.
Chapter 67.
When a rupture occurs in the groin, as we said, and the area protrudes, and nothing from the intestines descends to the testicles: and if it descends, it is little, and returns at all times: but if time prolongs with it, the fissure in the peritoneum increases, until the intestines descend into the peritoneum.
And this occurs from the extension of the peritoneum, which is in the groin, as we said.
That is, because the peritoneum stretches: then it softens, and the area swells, and protrudes.
And its treatment is with cautery, as was previously described.
And sometimes it is treated with iron [surgical instruments], in this way: that is, that the patient should lie on his back between your hands.
Then cut the swollen and protruding area of the rupture, with a cut across the width, about three fingers wide.
Then compress the peritoneum and what is under the skin, until when the white peritoneum that is under the skin following it is exposed.
Then take a probe, and place it over the protruding area of the peritoneum, and press it toward the depth of the belly.
Then sew the two protruding places over the end of the probe from the peritoneum, and bind them together with sutures: then remove the end of the probe.
And do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor stretch an egg, nor anything else, as I made you know in the treatment of intestinal hernia.
Then treat it with treatment of wounds.
When you therefore cut the threads, clean it, and restore the wound, until it heals.
Indeed the peritoneum contracts, and the protrusion of the rupture does not increase.
And cautery is better in this place: since it is closer to safety.
¶ Concerning the softening of the skin of the testicles.
Chapter 68.
Often the skin of the testicles becomes soft in some men, because the flesh between them softens, and its appearance becomes unsightly.
Therefore it is necessary for one who is asked to treat it, to make the patient lie on his back, and cut all the skin that has softened above the natural skin.
Then join the two lips with sutures: and if you wish, first sew the excess of softened skin with three or four sutures, and secure the suture.
Then cut between the sutures.
Then treat with the treatment of other wounds, until it heals, and the threads fall out.
¶ Concerning Castration.
Chapter 69.
Know that Castration in our law is excommunicated: and because of that it would be permitted for me not to mention it in this book of mine: and its mention is not necessary, except for two reasons.
One of which is that it should be in the physician's knowledge when he is asked about it: and that he should know its treatment when it happens to someone.
And the other reason is that we often need castration of certain animals, for our benefit through that, such as rams, goats, and cats, and similar animals.
I say therefore, that Castration is done according to two methods.
Either through crushing.
Or through splitting and cutting.
In that which is done through crushing, the way of operation is: that we make the animal sit in hot water, until its testicles soften, and become smooth, and hang down: then crush them with your hand, until they dissolve and do not appear under touch.
In castration through splitting and cutting, it is necessary that you hold the animal, and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory, and cut above each egg with one cut, until when the two eggs come out: then cut them after you have skinned both, and do not leave on them any of the peritoneum except the thin peritoneum, which is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than that which is done through crushing: since crushing perhaps leaves something of the testicles, wherefore the animal desires coitus.
Then treat the wound until it heals.
On the treatment of two types of Hermaphrodites.
Chapter 70.
A hermaphrodite in men exists in two forms.
One of them is when there appears in the following space, or in the skin of the testicles, in what is between the two testicles, a structure like a woman's vulva, in which there are hairs: and sometimes urine flows from what is in the skin of the testicles.
In women however there is one type, and it is when a woman's vulva above the pubic area has what look like very small male testicles, protruding outward: of which one is like a man's penis, and two like two testicles.
And the treatment of the three types, two in men and one type in women, is: that you should cut away the additional flesh until their impression disappears.
Then treat them like other wounds.
In the second type in men, from which urine exits, which is in the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor is there any treatment at all.
On cutting the Clitoris and protruding flesh in women's vulvas.
Chapter 71.
The clitoris sometimes grows beyond what is natural, until its appearance becomes unsightly and ugly: and sometimes in certain women it grows so much that it expands like in men, and reaches the point of coitus.
Therefore you should hold the excess of the Clitoris with your hand, or with a hook, and cut it: and do not cut too deeply, especially at the deep root: so that bleeding does not occur.
Then treat it like wounds, until it heals.
The grown flesh is flesh that grows in the opening of the womb, until it fills it: and sometimes it extends outward like a tail: and because of this some of the ancients call the condition "tailed."
Therefore we should cut it as we cut the Clitoris, and treat it until it heals.
On the treatment of Alratica, which is an unpierced woman's vulva.
Chapter 72.
Alratica is when a woman's vulva is not pierced, or has a small opening.
And it is either natural or accidental.
The accidental type is from a previous illness: and it indeed occurs either from additional grown flesh, or from thin or thick membrane: and occurs either in the depth of the womb, or in its sides, or in its upper part, or its lower part: and it prevents coitus and pregnancy, and birth, and sometimes prevents menstruation.
And knowledge of it comes through the midwife's examination, if the blockage is visible and close.
If however it is not visible, then seek it with your fingers, or with a probe.
If however the blockage is from thin membrane, and is close to the two lips: then proceed quickly, and break it open.
And it is done by placing cushions under the two lips.
Then place the index fingers of your two hands together, and the woman should lie on her back with her legs spread.
Then stretch the two lips forcefully, until the thin membrane breaks, and the blockage opens.
Then take wool, and soak it in oil, and place it on the spot: and let the woman have intercourse every day, so that the area does not heal over again.
If however the membrane is thick, dense: then you should cut it with a broad spatula similar to a myrtle leaf.
If however the blockage is from grown flesh, then suspend it with hooks, and cut it: and have with you medicines that stop bleeding without causing pain, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then apply a wide lead tube, so that the wound does not heal too quickly, keep it there for several days: and apply a dry linen wick, then treat with the remaining treatment until it heals.
And sometimes other flesh growths occur in the womb: therefore they should be cut according to this method: as long as it is not a cancerous tumor for a cancerous tumor that is in the womb should not be opposed with iron at all.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, athehelil, and red bothor in women's vulvas.
Chapter 73.
Hemorrhoids are indeed openings of vein orifices, from which much blood flows continuously.
When hemorrhoids become old, they become thelil: and I have already discussed their types in the classification, and their signs: and I will say here which of them receive treatment, and which do not.
Therefore I say that hemorrhoids which are in the orifice of the womb and are not apparent to the senses: then there is no treatment with iron [surgical instruments].
However, those of them that are in the orifice of the womb which can be sensed are those which can be treated.
Therefore it is necessary to bring the woman into a cold room.
Then extend the athehelil with forceps, or with rough cloth, and cut them from their roots.
Then powder them during the blood flow with one of the cutting powders without causing pain: such as acacia, dragon's blood, frankincense, and similar things.
Then have her press her feet against the wall for one hour.
Afterwards, place her in cold water.
If the blood flow persists: then make her sit in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate rinds, galls, and similar things.
Then take barley flour, mix it with honey and vinegar, and make a plaster with it on her back.
If therefore the blood is stopped with this, it is good: and if not, apply cupping vessels to the back and breasts, without scarification.
Then take wool, and submerge it in tarathit juice, or plantain juice, or juice of alaulat branches, that is, oak, with Pontic wine, and apply it to the area.
When therefore the swelling subsides, then treat the area with ointments until it heals.
The red albarth is similar to the heads of alhaasse, and has a rough appearance.
Therefore it is necessary that you cut what appears of it, according to what I said about thelil equally: and treat it until it heals.
On the perforation of Swelling which occurs in the womb.
Chapter 74.
Many types of swellings occur in the womb, such as cancer, and stony swelling, and ulcers, and adubelati, and alekilati, that is, herpes, gangrene, and fistulas, and hemorrhoids, and fissures, and thelil, and hot swelling.
And I have indeed already discussed all these diseases and their types, and their signs, and their treatments in the classification: it is necessary that I discuss in this treatise the hot swelling that occurs in the womb, when it is among the swellings that collect pus, and you want to know how its opening is made with iron [surgical instruments].
Therefore it is necessary that you consider.
Then if the pain of the swelling in its beginning is acute with its striking, and inflammation, and fever: and you see it redden: if sensation falls upon it, then do not hurry with its perforation: and treat it with that which helps with maturation, until when the acuteness of the symptoms subsides.
Then it is necessary that you make the woman sit on a seat that has two seats, and let her recline, or lean back on her back, and gather her feet to the lower part of her belly: and let her thighs be spread, and let her place her arms under her knees, and bind both with a binding suitable for that.
Then let the midwife sit at the right side, and administer the instrument by which the orifice of the womb is opened.
Whose form comes in those which are after.
And it is necessary when you want to administer this instrument, that you fix with the Probe the depth of the woman's womb: so that what enters from the instrument is not more than the depth of the womb, and harm the patient.
If however the instrument should be greater than the depth, then it is necessary that you place small cushions above the covering of the depth of the womb: so that the entrance of the whole instrument into the depth of the womb is prevented: and it is necessary that she place the pivot, which runs in the instrument, from the upper side: and that she hold the instrument, and that the assistant turn the pivot, until the depth of the womb is opened.
When therefore a swelling appears, and is touched by hand, and is soft and yielding: then it is necessary that you cut it at its head with a broad spatula.
When therefore the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary that you place in the wound a pledget soaked in rose oil: or green oil, in which there is some astringency: and place the pledget, and draw out the incision in the depth of the womb.
And place externally over the womb and over the pubic region clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
Then after two days it should be treated with an ointment that is suitable for that, until it heals.
And sometimes the womb and wound are washed, such that it is cleansed with water and honey: then with water in which lily root or long birthwort has been boiled: then return to the ointment.
But if the wound should be hidden inside the womb, then you must refrain from treating it with iron, rather treat it with what we have described in the division.
¶ Instructions for Midwives, how they should treat living embryos that have emerged in an unnatural position.
Chapter 75.
It is necessary for the Midwife to know first the form of natural birth.
Among its signs therefore is that when you see a woman bearing down, and desiring to breathe air, and what is above her of the birth becomes lighter for her, and the exit of the embryo hastens: then know that this birth is according to the natural position, and its exit is upon its head, and the afterbirth with it: or suspended, or hanging from the umbilical cord.
When therefore you see these signs, then it is necessary that her belly be compressed, so that the embryo descends quickly.
For when it descends upon its head, the afterbirth descends with it, and it is cleansed from those superfluities with good cleansing.
But that which is other than this birth is unnatural, unfavorable.
For sometimes the fetus emerges upon its feet, and its body emerges before its head, and its feet, and one hand, or one foot: or its head and its hands emerge: or it emerges belly-first: and perhaps it turns upon its neck, and similar unfavorable positions.
It is necessary therefore for the Midwife to be prudent, subtle in all these matters, and to beware of error and deception: and I am showing every position of these, and what the technique is in it, and it is signified through that, and one should stand upon it.
When the fetus emerges upon its head in its natural emergence, and this becomes difficult for the woman, and birth becomes hard for her, and you see her strength now weakened: then make her sit upon a seat, or let women hold her: and steam her vulva with cooked fenugreek, and with moist oils.
Then let the Midwife introduce between her fingers a small spatula, and split with it the afterbirth, and cut it with her nail, until what is in it of moisture runs out: and press the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
If however it does not descend, then it is necessary that the woman be given an enema of fenugreek mucilage, with sesame oil.
Then command her after the enema to bear down: and make her sneeze with condisi, and hold her mouth and her nose for one hour.
For the fetus will emerge immediately.
But if two hands of the fetus emerge first, then it is necessary that you return them with ease gradually.
If however it does not obey in entering, then place the woman upon a seat, and raise her feet upward: then shake the seat upon the ground: and let the woman be held, so that she does not fall during the shaking.
If therefore the hands do not emerge, and the fetus dies: then cut them, and extract the remainder of the fetus: and bind its hand with cloth, and extract it with evenness: for it will emerge.
¶ Exit of the embryo by its feet.
When the fetus comes out by its feet, then it is necessary to push them upward: then turn the fetus gradually: afterwards straighten it.
Then take one of its feet, and extend it gradually.
When therefore they come out up to the hips, then instruct her to push, and make her sneeze with condisi: for it will come out, if God wills.
And if it does not come out with what we have described, then return the fetus gradually, until you place it according to the natural position.
For it will then come out with an easy exit.
And if it is prevented with all that we have described: then take from marshmallow mucilage, and sesame oil, and dissolved gum: and pound everything in a mortar with good pounding: then anoint with it the woman's vulva, and lower belly.
Then make her sit in tepid water, until it reaches the hypochondria.
When therefore you see that her lower parts have been warmed, then make for her a suppository of myrrh: and insert it.
When therefore she holds the suppository for one hour, then make her sit upon a seat.
Then make her sneeze: and close her mouth and nose: and press the lower part of the belly with gentle pressure.
For the fetus will come out immediately.
¶ Exit of the fetus upon knees and hands.
When it comes out according to this form, then be ingenious in introducing your hands gradually.
Then let the woman lie back upon her neck, and let her feet hang downward: and let her be lying upon a bed, and fold her hands.
Then press above her belly gradually.
Then remove what is under her feet from the pillows, until she is prepared in the lower part.
If the fetus does not come out, then take its feet, and move them with vigorous motion.
Then press what is above the hypochondria gradually, until the fetus ascends to the upper parts.
Then let her introduce her hands, and straighten the fetus gradually: and instruct the woman to push, until the fetus comes out.
¶ Exit of the fetus hanging laterally from the hands.
Be ingenious in returning its hand.
If however one cannot return it, then raise the woman, and make her walk.
If truly she cannot walk, then make her lie upon a bed: and shake her legs with vigorous shaking.
Then administer the liniment which you prepared from the mucilages.
If labor takes her, then make her sit upon a seat.
Then work on returning the hand, and straighten the fetus according to the natural position: and make the woman sneeze, and instruct her to push herself, until it comes out.
¶ Exit of the fetus upon its neck, with hands spread, and its face is to its mother's back.
It is necessary that the Midwife hold the hand of the fetus, then turn it gradually, and straighten it with ease.
When therefore it is straightened, then place upon her vulva what we described of oil and mucilages.
Then instruct her to move gradually.
When therefore you have done that, then make her sit upon a seat, and instruct her to lean to the right side.
Then instruct her to push: and make her sneeze with condisi.
¶ Exit of the fetus prepared upon the side.
When it descends to the opening of the womb according to this manner with the afterbirth, then cut the afterbirth with your nail, until it is emptied of moisture.
If therefore it hangs over the face of the fetus, and its neck, then cut it from the navel: so that it is not retained, and dies.
Then push the fetus to the interior, until you place it according to what is necessary: and instruct the woman to push herself several times: for it will come out with a light exit.
On the Delivery of Twins or Multiple Fetuses:
Know that twins are often born: and sometimes three and four are born.
However, this is unusual, and rarely happens.
Five indeed is something against nature, and they do not live at all: and one placenta contains them, however many they may be: and they are separated by a membrane, distinguishing between them, attached to each of their umbilical cords.
And sometimes they come out as one fetus does in a natural, easy delivery: and they come out in an unfavorable delivery, as we have described.
But if some of them are difficult during birth, and arrive in what we mentioned about unnatural positions, then treat them as we have described: and let the midwife be skillful, and do what she does with ease and patience, and let her be ingenious according to each position with what is prepared for her from ingenuity leading to safety.
On the Remembrance of What Forms in the Womb from Embryos, and Abortions:
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten form in the womb: and it has been verified by me that a woman in miscarrying expelled seven, and another fifteen, all formed.
This was done by the measurement of the glorious wise one.
All this therefore should be in the knowledge of the Midwife.
For perhaps something similar will come to her: therefore let her oppose it with what is necessary.
On the Extraction of a Dead Fetus:
Chapter 76
When you treat a dead fetus with what I said in the section on medication, and it does not come out, and you are forced to use operation by hand, then you must consider.
If therefore the woman is of sound strength, and is not with any of the conditions from which destruction is feared for her: then the woman should lie back on a bed, on her back, and her head should be inclined downward, and her legs elevated: and an assistant should hold her on both sides, or she should be tied to the bed, so that her body is not pulled during the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the mouth of the womb with humid oils with marsh mallow mucilage, and fenugreek, and linseed.
Then let the Midwife anoint her hand with these oils and mucilages: then let her insert it into the womb with ease.
Then let her seek with it a place where she may fix hooks into the fetus, and places suitable for that.
Consider therefore then, if the fetus is prepared to descend upon its head: then fix the hooks in its eyes, or in its neck, or in its mouth, or in its palate, or under its chin, or in its throat, or in places near the ribs, and under the hypochondria.
But if the descent is upon its feet, then in the pubic area, and its middle ribs.
And it is necessary that she hold the hook with the right hand: and place its curve between the fingers of the left hand: and insert it with the hand with ease, and fix the hook in any of those places which we mentioned, until it reaches an empty space.
Then fix opposite it another hook, or a third, if you need: so that the traction is equal, and does not lean to one side.
Then let it be extended with equal extension, and let the extension not be only according to straightness, but rather moved to every side, so that its exit may be easier: as is done with a tooth during its extraction.
And it is necessary in what is between that, that the extension be softened.
But if a part of it is retained, then the Midwife should anoint some fingers with oil, and insert them from the side, so that she may turn with them what is retained.
If therefore a part of the fetus comes out, then let the hooks be moved to another place which is higher: do this until the whole fetus comes out.
If therefore a hand comes out before the others, and it is not possible to reduce it, because of its constriction: then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it.
Then pull it until when it comes out completely, cut it either at the joint of the palm, or at the elbow: and thus it is necessary that you do with the other hand, and with the two feet.
If however the head of the fetus is large, or experiences constriction during exit, or there is water collected in its head: then it is necessary to insert between the fingers a spiny spatula, and split the head with it, and extract the water: or break it with the instrument which is called Almisdac: whose form comes with the forms of the remaining instruments in the chapter which follows this.
Similarly one should do if the fetus has a naturally large head, then it is necessary to cut the cranium: or extract it with the misdach, as we said.
Then pull the bones with forceps.
But if the head emerges, and is constricted at the collarbone: then let it be split, until the fluid which is in the chest is poured out.
If however it does not fold, nor yield to exit: then cut the head piece by piece, according to what is possible for you.
But if the lower abdomen is swollen, or there is dropsy in it: then it is necessary to pierce it, until fluid runs out of it.
If truly the fetus is on its feet, then its extraction is easy, and its direction to the mouth of the womb is light.
If however it is constricted at the belly, or chest: then it is necessary to extract it with a cloth which you have wrapped around your hand: and split the belly, or chest, until what is in them runs out.
If however the remaining limbs are removed, and the head returns: then let the Midwife insert the left hand.
If therefore the mouth of the womb is open, then let her insert the hand into the depth of the womb, and seek with it the head, and pull it with the fingers up to the mouth of the womb: then insert in it one hook, or two hooks, and pull it with it.
But if the mouth of the womb is constricted, because of a hot swelling which happens to it: then one should not labor over it, rather it is necessary then to administer pouring of moist, fatty things: and administer plasters: and let her sit in waters which soften and moisten.
But if the fetus is on its side, then it is possible that it may be straightened: then let be administered what we said regarding the living fetus.
But if that is not possible, then let it be cut piece by piece, and extracted.
And it is necessary that you do not leave anything from the afterbirth inside at all.
When therefore you have completed your operation, then administer what we said regarding hot swellings which occur in wombs.
But if bleeding occurs, then let her descend into styptic waters, and administer the remaining treatment.
And I indeed have seen a woman, in whose belly a fetus was dead: then she became pregnant over it another time, afterward another fetus also died.
Therefore after a long time a swelling occurred in her navel, and it became inflated, until it opened: and began to emit pus.
I was therefore called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time: and the wound was not consolidated.
I therefore placed upon it some plasters of strong attraction: and a bone came out from the place.
Then some days passed for her, and another bone came out.
I therefore wondered at this, since there is no bone in the belly.
I therefore thought that they were from the bones of the dead fetus.
I therefore examined the wound, and extracted many bones, with the woman being in her better conditions: and she has now lived thus for a long time, and emits a little pus from the place.
And I have not confirmed this strange thing here, except because in it there is knowledge, and help for that, which the skilled physician administers with the hand from treatment.
On the Forms of Necessary Instruments for Extracting a Fetus.
Chapter 77.
Here below is the form of the Constrictor, with which books are flattened, which has two pivots equally placed at the two ends of two wooden pieces.
However, this Pivot should be more delicate than the constrictor: and it should be made either from ebony or from boxwood.
And the width of each wooden piece should be about two fingers: and the margin of each of both is about one finger, and the length of each of them is a palm and a half: and in the middle of the two wooden pieces are two extensions of the same kind of wood, which are already firmly fixed in both, whose length is half a palm and a little more: and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two extensions are inserted into the mouth of the womb, so that it may be opened with them when you turn the two Pivots.
Form of the Pivot by which the womb is opened.
Another form similar to that one, more delicate and lighter.
It should be made from ebony or boxwood, similar to forceps.
However, at its end there should be two extensions, as you see here above: and the length of each extension is about a palm: and the width of each is two fingers.
Therefore when you want to open the womb with it, then have the woman sit on a bed, with her feet hanging down, with what is between her legs opened.
Then insert these two joined extensions into the womb: and you are holding the lower end of the instrument between her thighs.
Then open your hand with the instrument, as you do with scissors, evenly according to the amount you want from the opening of the mouth of the womb, until the Midwife does what she wants.
Form of another Pivot which the ancients called the Form of the Impeller by which the fetus is pushed.
Form of the Almisdac, with which a large head is crushed: and it resembles forceps, which has teeth at the end, as you see.
And sometimes it is made elongated, like forceps according to this form, as you see, which has teeth like the teeth of a saw, with which it cuts and crushes.
Form of the Almisdac with which a small head is crushed.
Form of the Hook having one spine.
Form of the Hook having two spines.
Form of forceps which has saw teeth, with which the head of the fetus is crushed.
Form of the Spatula having two ends for cutting off the head.
Form of two broad Spatulas also.
All these instruments are practiced with, and the more their types are multiplied, and are prepared at the craftsman's, the quicker he is at his operation, and the higher among people, due to his quantity.
Therefore do not despise any of these instruments, rather let it be prepared with you.
For their necessity is inexcusable.
¶ On the Extraction of the Afterbirth.
Chapter 78.
When the afterbirth is retained during childbirth, then it is necessary that you instruct the patient to hold her breath.
Then make her sneeze with white hellebore: and press your hand over her mouth and her nostrils.
If therefore it comes out by this means, it is good.
If not: then take a pot, and make a hole in its lid: and put in it herbs that open the mouth of the womb, such as calamint, rue, and dill, and chamomile, and wormwood, and cinnamon bark, and centaury: take all these herbs, or some of them, and submerge them in water, and put them over fire.
Then place a reed tube over the hole in the pot's lid, and the other end in the mouth of the womb: and hold it until the vapor reaches the cavity of the womb.
Then make her sneeze, as we said.
For the afterbirth will come out with ease.
But if it remains after this treatment, and does not come out: then instruct the midwife to dip her hand in sesame oil, or marshmallow mucilage.
Then let her insert it into the vulva, and search with it for the afterbirth.
When therefore she finds it, then let her take it, and extend it gradually.
But if it is attached deep in the womb, then let her insert her hand, according to what we have described, until when she finds the afterbirth, and extract it gradually in a straight line, so that the womb does not prolapse during violent pulling: rather it is necessary that it be moved gently to the right and left sides: then increase the amount of pulling.
For it will yield then, and be preserved, and escape from adherence.
But if the mouth of the womb is constricted, as we have already described: treat it with sneezing, and with the pot treatment with herbs.
However if it does not come out with all that we have described, then beware lest you tire yourself with repeated extraction attempts.
Indeed it is necessary that whatever protrudes to the mouth of the womb from it, you tie to the woman's hip.
Then give her an enema with tetrapharmacon ointment.
For it will cause it to rot after some days, and it will dissolve, and come out.
However when it rots, a foul odor rises from it to the stomach and head, and this harms the patient.
Therefore it is necessary that suitable fumigations be administered for this.
Indeed some of the ancients have tried fumigation with watercress and dried figs.
Form of the instrument by which a woman is fumigated during retention of the afterbirth and menses.
Let it be made of glass similar to an alembic, or of copper: and let the wide end be placed over fire: and let the fumigation be placed over coals: let her hold it, until that fumigation is consumed: and repeat with another.
¶ On the Treatment of an Imperforate Anus.
Chapter 79.
Many children are born, and their anuses are not perforated, which a thin membrane has already blocked.
Therefore it is necessary that the midwife perforate that membrane with her finger: and if not, perforate it with a sharp spatula: and let her beware of the muscle, lest she touch it.
Then place over it wool soaked in wine and oil.
Then treat it with ointments, until it heals.
But if you fear that it may become blocked, then place in the opening a lead tube for many days, and let it be removed when the child wants to defecate.
And blockage of the anus also occurs from the healing of a wound, or abscess.
Therefore it is necessary that that healed tissue be cut, then treated with what we mentioned regarding treatment and lead.
On the Treatment of Fistulas that Occur in the Lower Part.
Chapter 80.
Fistulas that occur in the lower part are Nodules and Swellings that occur near the anus externally: or in the space of one of its parts: and it is either one Fistula, or many.
When therefore this Nodule becomes old, it opens, and from it runs a white watery fluid, or thin pus.
And sometimes these Fistulas are penetrating.
Therefore penetrating ones are recognized by that which comes out with defecation, and wind, when the patient defecates: and perhaps a worm comes out of them.
And among them are Fistulas which, when they are in this space, penetrate up to the bladder, and up to the urinary passage.
And among them are those penetrating up to the hip joint, and up to the tail bone.
That by which a Fistula penetrating to the anus is known, also from a non-penetrating one, is that you should insert your index finger into the anus: and insert a thin probe into the Fistula made of bronze or iron: when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in it, then insert into it a thin lead probe, or a horse hair, until you feel the probe or hair with your finger.
But if you do not feel it at all, nor does anything come out of the opening from defecation, nor wind, nor worm as I said: then know that it is non-penetrating: therefore hasten to treatment.
And when the Fistula is penetrating up to the bladder, or to the urinary passage: then its sign is the exit of urine from it, and its prevention from healing with medicines.
If indeed it is penetrating up to the hip joint, or to the hip bone: then its sign is that the probe reaches there, if there is no tortuosity in it up to the bone: and the finding of pain in the surrounding area, and that pus always comes out of it: and that treatment does not work on it, nor does it heal with ointment.
And in all these penetrating Fistulas there is no cure at all: and their treatment is labor and vanity for him, from foolish physicians, upon whom it is imposed.
In that which is not penetrating, and not old, healing is hoped for with iron: according to what I tell you, and I have already tested: and it is that the patient should lie between your hands on his back: and raise his legs up above his hips, and his hips should be inclined toward his belly.
Then insert a lead or bronze probe, if there is tortuosity in the Fistula, until you know where the probe reaches.
If however the patient feels it near the anus, then it is necessary that you insert your index finger into the anus.
If therefore you feel with your finger that the probe has already penetrated by itself exposed, without feeling between your finger and it membrane or flesh: then therefore know certainly that it is penetrating: therefore do not labor on it, since there is no cure for it, as we said.
They have said however, that flesh heals in some men, and from treatment by which help is hoped for it, it is that you should heat a thin cautery according to the width of the Fistula, as preceded, and insert it heated into the Fistula, until it reaches near the anus.
Then repeat that twice, or thrice, until you know that all those added filtered flesh parts, which are similar to bird quill tubes, have already been burned.
Then treat that with wicks wrapped in butter, until those flesh parts which were burned come out: then treat that with ointments generating flesh and consolidating.
If therefore it heals, it is good.
And if not, then it will never heal with another treatment.
And if the probe comes out, and does not reach up to your finger, which is in the anus, and there is between it and the probe a thick covering of flesh, or membrane: and you see the Fistula in that which follows the surface of the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the Fistula, and you go cutting with the probe what is in the fistula, until the cutting reaches where the probe's tip reaches, and the probe is freed and falls.
Then clean those filtered flesh parts, which resemble bird quill tubes, so that nothing remains of them.
If however the blood overcomes you, and interferes between you and your operation to cut those tissues: then the best thing to do, and of quickest benefit, is to cauterize it with fire, or with sharp medicine.
For cauterization with fire combines good conditions: the burning of those additional tissues, the stopping of blood, and the drying of moistures.
Then treat that with lint rolls soaked in butter, or with ground cabbage, until the site releases pus, and those burned tissues come out in pus.
Then treat the wound with consolidating ointments that generate hard flesh: and these are medicines which have astringent and drying properties.
For when they are restored, then it is already healed: and you need not fear recurrence.
If however the Fistula has already reached to the depth of the anus, and extends from the excess of the body: then insert your finger into the anus, and investigate with it.
If therefore you feel the probe, and between you and it there is a membrane of peritoneum, or flesh, and it is close to penetration: then there is no operation in that case, except according to the way of confidence and hope.
Which is, because one of three methods is administered in it: either that you cauterize it, as we said: or cut it, until you reach its cavity, so that it becomes possible for you to insert lint rolls, and treatment from nearby.
And do not proceed much with the cutting, lest you cut the muscle containing the anus, and involuntary defecation occurs in the patient.
Then treat that with what we mentioned.
Perhaps indeed it will heal: or you may extend that membrane, and clean it after you cut it up close to the anus, as we said, either with a probe, or with another instrument with a sharp tip.
Then clean those filtered tissues which are in the incision, as far as you can with examination.
Then attempt restoration of the whole wound with the incision, and the upper orifice where it consolidates: and the Fistula remains open in the interior part of the anus, and it will be easier on the patient.
And sometimes the Fistula is sutured according to this method: that is, when you insert the probe into the Fistula: and it is on the side of the anus near the surface of the body with skin and the margin of the anus: then take a probe with a perforated tip, which is like the needle of aleskef, that is, of leather preparers, according to this form.
And insert into it a thread wound from five threads, and around them.
Then insert the probe with the thread into the Fistula, until it reaches its cavity.
If therefore it is penetrating in the margin of the anus internally nearby, then extract the thread from that hole: so that you insert your finger into the anus, and extract the end of the thread, and gather the two ends together: and tighten both, and leave that for one day, or two days: and the more the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well, until those tissues which are between the two ends of the thread are cut, and fall: then treat the wound, until it consolidates and heals.
If however the orifice of the Fistula is not penetrating: then make it penetrate in whatever way is prepared for you, unless it is very deep.
For it is not for you to do that because of the muscle, lest you cut it.
Then do with it as we said, until it heals.
Form of the spiny Spatumilis with which the Fistula is split, whose curve is very sharp, and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not cut what is not necessary, as you see.
On the suturing of Hemorrhoids, from which blood flows after their incision: and the treatment of fissures.
Chapter 81.
Hemorrhoids are of two types.
Either they are inside the anus, similar to coal or ember blisters: or as if they were grape seeds.
And of these some are small, and some large: and blood flows from them constantly, and there may be one, or many.
And they are outside the anus, and at its edges.
However, those that are outside the anus mostly have little moisture, from which flows a yellow liquid, or a small amount of blood in continuous flow: and they are the color of the body.
And the treatment of those that are inside the anus is that you should instruct the patient to sit down: and to strain until the anus protrudes and the hemorrhoids appear to you.
Therefore, either suspend them with hooks: or hold them with your nail.
Then cut them at their roots.
If however the hooks do not hold them, due to their moisture and softness: then grab them with rough cloth, or with cotton from a woolen doublet, and pull them with your fingers.
Then cut them, and sprinkle over them after the incision certain hot powders, to serve in place of cauterization.
Or cauterize them according to what was described in the chapter on cauterization.
Then treat them with the remaining treatment until they heal.
But if the anus does not yield to protrusion, then give the patient an enema in which there is a small amount of irritant, so that what is in the anus may be washed, and it may yield quickly to protrusion when the patient strains.
If however the hemorrhoids are external to the anus, then their matter is light, easy: and it is that you should grab them with your nail, or grab them with a hook, and cut them.
Then treat them with what we have mentioned, until they heal.
And for one who abhors cutting with iron, then it is necessary that you administer their suturing according to this method.
Take a wound thread, and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the hemorrhoid upward, and make it penetrate with the needle at its root from the other side: and wind the two ends of the thread at the lower part of the needle, and let them be widthwise: and bind the hemorrhoid with a strong binding.
Then tie the thread, and remove the needle.
Do this with all hemorrhoids, and leave one of them: do not suture it, so that the excess blood may flow from it.
Then place over the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil, or wax salve, and place it over the anus: and instruct the patient to rest.
Then leave it until it falls off.
When therefore the hemorrhoids fall off, treat them with ointments, and the remaining treatment, until they heal.
On the Treatment of Fissures.
For they often occur from the drying of feces, and constipation of nature.
When therefore they become chronic, and medicine does not help them: then it is necessary that you scrape with the point of a Spatumilis, or with your nail, until they become moist, and the upper layer which prevents them from healing is removed from them.
Then treat them, until they heal as necessary.
If however they do not heal: then repeat them with scraping more vigorous than the first, until they bleed much, and are excoriated then treat them, until they heal.
On the treatment of inverted and non-inverted Almirmar, and dry Athelil, and Formica.
Chapter 82.
Know that Almirmar is nothing but a round nodule of the same color as the body, similar to the head of a nail: and it occurs all over the body, especially in the lower parts of the feet and toes: and pain occurs from them when walking.
Therefore it is necessary that what is around the nail be cut, and held with forceps, or with a hook, and uprooted from its root.
Then treat it: and if you wish, cauterize it according to what was explained in its chapter.
Of the dry athelil, one is a small protrusion, rough, filtered, round above the surface of the body.
And they often occur on children's hands.
And its treatment is that you stretch the athelil and cut it: or bind it with a silk thread: or with a hair, until it falls off.
And if you wish, cauterize it as was previously mentioned with fire, or with sharp medicine: and indeed inverted and non-inverted athelil are uprooted with an instrument that I will describe.
Formica is also a small filtered protrusion, thick, on the surface of the body, going very deep: and when cold affects it, it feels like an ant's bite, and it occurs all over the body: and most commonly occurs on the hands.
And its treatment is that you take a small tube from a goose feather, or from a strong vulture feather: and place that tube over the Formica, until the circle of the tube contains its parts.
Then rotate your hand with the tube, until you cut what is around the Formica: and place it deep in the flesh, and uproot the Formica from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a tube of brass or iron, according to this form.
Let the top of the tube be drawn up to a height with a dull spiral, so that its grip and rotation may be easier on the finger.
And if you wish, cauterize the Formica as was previously mentioned in the cauterization of Athelil in the chapter on cautery.
On the forms of Instruments which are administered for Clysters in diseases of the Anus, and loosening of the bowels, and colic.
Chapter 83.
Sometimes a Clyster is made from silver, or from brass, or from cast or hammered copper.
And sometimes small and large ones are made from these Instruments, according to those on whom they are administered.
Therefore that which is administered in the treatment of small children should be small: and for those whose anuses are weak or painful, so too should their clysters be.
Large clyster.
Small clyster.
Its tube, that is, the upper head in which the bladder is tied, should be wide according to this manner: and it should have a distinction so that the bladder may be tied as you see, and its lower extremity which is inserted into the anus should be dull, that is, not open, very smooth, drawing to fineness: and on one of its sides there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole.
And the width of the hole should be according to the thickness of the rays, and a little thicker, and the bladder in which medicines are placed should be from an animal's bladder: or from egg membrane, which should be according to the form of a small asofrati: whose root should be one palm.
And it should be perforated with many holes as it is rotated, and the space between each hole should be the thickness of a finger.
Then insert into those holes a strong thread of raw thread, which should be of ten threads, or thereabouts, and gather with it the bladder like an asofrati.
Therefore when the bladder is placed in it, it should be bound at the head of the clyster between two distinguishing points with strong binding.
Then administer the clyster.
And this is the form of the circle of the bladder depicted here, and of the holes.
The proper method of administering an enema with it is: fill a leather bag or bladder with medicine, which should be warmed according to what the patient can tolerate: and take care that it is neither too hot nor too cold.
Then let the patient lie back on his back, and raise his legs upward: and place under him an inthaan, that is, a leather mat on which people lie, or a linen cloth.
Then anoint the tip of the clyster with oil, or with egg white, or with fenugreek mucilage, or similar substances.
Then insert it into the anus gently.
Then let the assistant take the leather bag with both hands together, and squeeze it forcefully until all that is in the clyster is emptied into the intestine.
Then remove the clyster, and let the patient raise his legs against the wall.
If the medicine compels him to evacuate, then let him hold it as long as he can.
And if he sleeps with it the whole night, it is most beneficial.
If you give him an enema for diseases involving looseness of the bowels, and evacuation happens quickly: then repeat the enema until he is healed.
If there are hemorrhoids in the anus, or painful swellings, then let the clyster be very thin and gentle, from among the clysters used for children.
And this should be the form of a thin clyster over which the skin is tight.
¶ On the treatment of head wounds without bone fracture.
Chapter 84.
I have already spoken in the division on wound treatment what was appropriate for the book, and I will speak here about their treatment by hand and with medicines in full detail.
Therefore I say that wounds differ according to that by which the wounds are made, and according to the place where the wound falls.
The things by which wounds are commonly made are like the throwing of a stone: or cutting with a sword or knife: or striking with a lance, or wood, or arrow: and similar things among many objects.
Wounds that occur according to body locations are like a wound that falls on the head, or neck, or chest, or belly, or liver: and similar body parts.
And I am narrating the treatments of certain wounds, to establish it as reasoning and rule for the remaining wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds: since mention of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore I say.
When a simple wound occurs in the head, and there is no bone fracture: consider then, if it is from throwing a stone, or similar things, and it has only broken the skin: and the wound is large, and you fear the occurrence of hot swelling in the patient: then hurry, and immediately bleed the cephalic vein: and do not delay it: and draw blood from him according to the measure of his strength: if there is nothing preventing bloodletting, especially if the blood coming from the wound is little.
And let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let the patient guard himself against fullness, and wine, and heavy foods.
If however something prevents bloodletting, then let him reduce his food, and not approach meats, nor wines.
And place upon the wound if hot swelling occurs, cotton soaked in rose oil alone, or with wine which has astringency.
If however you are safe from hot swelling, then place upon the wound, if it is fresh with its blood, and the air has not changed it, the powder which I will describe shortly: and if the air has already changed it, then place upon it certain ointments, until it heals.
If however the wound is large, or is from a sword cut, and similar things: and the lips do not come together with compresses, then bring them together with suture, according to what I will describe in wounds of the belly about their suturing.
If however the wound is already separated from the bone, and is hanging, and there is no retention of it, except in a small suspension: then cut it off.
Then treat the wound with medicines, and restore in it hard flesh in place of skin, and prescribe to the patient, that he be nourished with foods in which there is thickness, such as heads, and cooked wheat until it is like porridge: and similar things to these.
If you find putrefaction in the skin, and it does not adhere to the bone: then cut out all the putrefied matter, and remove it: then treat that area.
If however in these wounds there is an artery, or vein from which blood flows: and it is not stopped by medicines, then investigate the artery.
If therefore you find it not cut, then cut it with a Spatumile, or bind it.
If necessity demands when what we have mentioned does not help, then cauterize it, until the blood is stopped.
If however these wounds are small, simple, then their treatment is easy, light: such that you should powder over them this powder: since it will heal them before the air changes them.
¶ Method of Making Incarnative Powder.
Take one part frankincense, and two parts dragon's blood, and three parts unslaked lime: grind all of it thoroughly, and sift it: and fill the wound with it, and bind it with good binding, until it adheres well to it, and coagulates with the blood.
Then bind it from above with small compresses, and leave it, and do not loosen it while it remains, so that it does not form an abscess.
For the medicine is not removed from it until it heals.
And sometimes lime alone does this, when you do not have frankincense at hand, and dragon's blood also performs the same operation in many of the large wounds, when it is appropriate to place it upon them and bind them, and they are fresh wounds with their blood, which the air has not altered.
But if the air has already altered them somewhat, especially when it is in summer time, then there is no purpose in applying this powder: therefore treat it with what we have mentioned, such that you place upon them certain digestive ointments: or place upon them poultices made from barley flour, made with water and honey, until they discharge pus.
Then treat them with the remaining treatment, until they heal.
If however a fracture occurs with the wound in the bone, and it is small, then extract it with a Gesti: and I will already speak about the treatment of head fractures in what is to come.
¶ Concerning Wounds of the Neck.
Chapter 85.
If however a wound occurs in the Neck, then there is no difference between it in treatment, and between a head wound when it is simple.
If however a nerve of the Neck is already cut, or an artery: then if it is a nerve, there is no remedy in it, except that its treatment should be avoided with that which causes severe stypticity, such as verdigris, and vitriol, and similar things to them.
Because they harm nerves, and contract them.
And do not place anything cold at all upon the wound: because the substance of nerves is cold, and its continuation is with the brain, which is the most noble of members: and let that with which it is treated from medicines be gentle, like lime washed with sweet water sometimes infused with oil, or with rose oil, and tutty, when it is also washed with sweet water: and other mineral stones, according to this method, and moist ointments.
If however the wound is large, then administer suturing: or compress its lips with small compresses.
Then treat it until it heals.
If indeed the wound has depth, or a cavity occurs in it in its lower part, in which pus has already collected, then perforate it in the lowest place in it.
If indeed an artery is already cut in the wound, and its blood flow has followed: then separate, or cut it, and bind it: or cauterize it, if necessity drives to that.
If however the vertebrae of the throat have already been cut, or all, and the spinal cord is saved: then gather the lips of the skin with suturing over the windpipe, and do not touch the throat, rather straighten it, and return it to its natural shape.
Then bind it with appropriate binding, and leave it for some days.
Then treat it with ointments in which there is stypticity and drying, until it heals.
If however the wound has made an impression in the bone, then examine it: then if there are bone fragments in it, extract all of them, or what you can of them: and what is not prepared, leave it until the wound putrefies, and separates and its removal becomes easier.
But if the wound is fresh, then sprinkle over it the powder which I described.
However if bone appears in the wound, and it becomes a fistula: then treat it with that which was mentioned in its chapter.
¶ On Wounds in the Shoulder Blades.
Chapter 86.
IF It is a wound from a lance, or knife: and you see its depth, then consider.
Therefore if air exits from the wound when the patient breathes through it, then know that the wound is fatal.
But if it does not have depth, and is fresh with its blood: then do not put powder in it from the first hour, nor bind it, lest blood be retained in its depth, and return to the heart, and kill the patient: but place on it an attractive ointment, and loosen it twice per day.
If indeed the ointment is not available to you, then place old cotton in the opening of the wound, so that it may absorb what exits from it of fluids: and have the patient sleep on the wound, so that what accumulates in it may flow out.
If indeed three days or more have passed since the wound, and neither spasm nor bad heart tremor nor tightness in breathing has occurred to the patient, and you see his other conditions are good: then know that the wound is safe.
Therefore treat it both with lint and the remaining treatment, until it heals.
If however its healing is prevented, and it keeps opening, then know that it has now become a fistula.
Therefore treat it according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from a sword cut, or knife, and is simple on the surface of the chest, or back: then treat it with what was previously mentioned about suturing, if it is large: or with powder, if it is small.
However if it has made an impression in the bone, and pieces have broken off from it: then examine the wound, and hasten to extract those pieces, if they are separated.
If indeed they are not separated, then leave them until the wound putrefies, for their extraction becomes easier.
The judgment in treatment of other wounds occurring in other limbs is the judgment of what we have said.
¶ On Wounds of the Belly, and exit of intestine, and their emergence.
Chapter 87.
The disruption which occurs to the belly is large, medium, and small.
From the large one therefore sometimes the intestine exits, or multiple intestines.
Wherefore their insertion and their suturing becomes more forceful and more difficult.
And small disruption also becomes difficult in another way.
It follows therefore that their reduction becomes more difficult, if you do not hasten in its insertion to its place immediately: since it swells, opens, and thickens: wherefore its entry becomes difficult.
Because of this therefore the medium disruption becomes better than disruptions: since with it the reduction of intestine does not become difficult, as it becomes difficult in these two types.
Know that the suture of the belly is according to four modes.
Two common sutures are suitable in suturing the belly, and in suturing the remaining wounds of the body: and two other sutures properly share with the remaining wounds.
Nevertheless they are more proper with suturing of the belly: and I will tell you about each one with exposition and declaration.
I say therefore that when the wound is small, and something of the intestine exits from it, and it becomes difficult to reduce it: then that is because of one of two modes.
Either because of the smallness of the disruption, as we said: Or because there is swelling in the intestine due to the coldness of the air.
When therefore it is thus, then it is necessary that you warm it, so that you submerge a sponge, or damp cloth in tepid water alone.
Or cook squinant and cyprus and spikenard in water, and foment the intestine with it, until the swelling resolves.
Sometimes wine in which there is astringency does this: and it is better than water alone in resolving the swelling.
When you see that the swelling has already subsided, then moisten the intestine with water in which marshmallow or mallow has been boiled.
For through this its entry is eased with a small matter.
If however its return is prevented after this treatment, then make a small incision in the rupture with the instrument used to cut hemorrhoids.
And the first treatment is better when it is prepared than cutting: and there should be no compulsion to cut after necessity and lack of instrument.
Let one part of it which is twisted be sharp, and its other part not sharp, and let the thin end not be with the thinness of a Spatumilis, but rather somewhat blunter: and that is what resembles a caiada, as you see.
When therefore the wound is widened and the intestine enters, then it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural form in its proper place, if this is possible: since it is better.
When truly the rupture is wide in the lower part of the belly: then it is necessary that you make the patient lie on his back, and place his legs higher than his head.
And if it is in the upper part of the belly, then place his head and his chest higher than his lower parts.
And similarly if the rupture is in one of the two parts of the belly, then direct your attention and your consideration always to this, that the part in which the wound is should be higher than the other part.
And thus it is necessary that you manage this in large wounds and in medium wounds.
In small wounds however, place it according to what is prepared for you.
Then have present at your hands a dutiful assistant, who holds the entire rupture with his hand: and brings together its edges.
Then uncover from it to the one who follows the suture something after something: and this is the method of common single suture.
And it is that you take a needle, or a number of needles according to the size of the width of the wound.
Then leave from the end of the rupture the length of the ring finger, that is, the medical finger: and fix one needle without inserting a thread into it in the two ends of the skin, with the two ends of the sifac which is under the skin from the inside, until you make it penetrate from the other side, and you have already brought together the two ends of the skin and the two ends of the sifac: and they become four rows.
Then tighten with a double thread around the needle several times from both sides simultaneously, until the two edges of the wound are brought together in a proper joining.
Then leave the thickness of a finger also, and fix another needle in them: afterward secure it with thread, as you did with the first needle.
Do not therefore cease doing this with what you need from needles, until you complete the constriction of the entire wound.
And let the needles be medium between thickness and thinness: since very thin needles quickly cut the flesh: and thick ones are difficult to insert into the skin: because of that it is necessary that they be medium in thinness and thickness.
And it is not necessary that the needle be fixed in the two ends of the skin very close: so that the flesh is not cut quickly, and the wound opens before its consolidation, and they should not be extended also with suture, so that the wound is not prevented from consolidation.
Then cut off the ends of the needles, so they do not harm the patient during his sleep: and place on them cushions of cloth from every side, which hold the ends of the needles: and leave them until you know that the wound has already consolidated.
And indeed this type of suture with needles in such a way is more suitable in small wounds.
Since one needle suffices in their suture, or two, or thereabouts.
The second suture is a common one, where you join together with suturing four edges: two edges of skin, and two edges of the peritoneum at once, with a needle threaded with an even thread in terms of thinness and thickness.
Then when you have passed the needle through these four edges, bring the needle back from the side where you began, so that the drawn thread falls from the upper part of the wound: so that the suture is like the stitching with which cloths are bound, and place between each suture the width of a little finger.
This suture is known to men.
And with this suture I sewed up a wound that had occurred to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the tear of the wound was larger than one palm: and about two palms of the middle intestine had protruded, and the tear was in the middle of the belly.
Therefore I returned it after his intestine had remained outside the wound for 24 hours.
The wound was therefore consolidated in a space of about 15 days: and I treated him, and he was healed: and he lived many years after that, going about his affairs.
And the physicians had judged that he would not be healed.
And it was miraculous that I did not treat it with ointment.
For I was in a place where no ointment could be found, nor any medicines.
I therefore placed old cotton on the wound twice a day, and continued washing it with honey water until it was healed.
I will describe two types of specific sutures according to the narrative account.
And it is that you begin with a sharp suture: and insert the needle from the outside to the inside.
When therefore the needle penetrates the skin and the muscle running along the length and width of the whole body: leave the edge of the peritoneum on the side where you inserted the needle, and make it penetrate into the other edge from the inside to the outside at the other edge of the abdominal wall.
When therefore you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate according to this in this same edge of the abdominal wall from the outside to the inside: and leave the edge of the peritoneum, which is on this side, and make the needle penetrate into the other edge from the inside to the outside: and make it penetrate with your penetration which you make it penetrate in the peritoneal edge of the abdominal wall, which is on its side, until it penetrates it completely.
Then begin also from this same side, and sew it with the edge which is from the peritoneum on the other side, and draw out the needle from the skin which it penetrated.
Then return it into that skin, and sew the edge of the peritoneum, which is on the other side, with this edge of the abdominal wall: and draw it out from the skin which is on its side, and do that once, and again: and do that time after time, until you sew the whole exterior according to that example.
And the summary of this suture is that you sew with the furriers' suture with which they sew skins: so that you sew the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin: and that you leave it another time from the other side, until you complete it.
The second type of specific suture, which Galen mentioned.
His account is narrated thus, that you sew it according to the similarity with which some physicians sew them, such that they gather every part to its matching counterpart by nature: and they join the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge, and the edge of the abdominal wall to its other edge.
And that is done according to what I tell you.
It is necessary that you fix the needle in the edge of the abdominal wall near to you from the outside, and make it penetrate to the inside in it alone: and leave the two edges of the peritoneum.
Then turn the needle, and make it penetrate from the outside to the inside in the two edges of the peritoneum, namely on both sides.
Then turn both, also make both penetrate from the inside to the outside, in the two other edges of the abdominal wall, which are on the opposite side.
And this mode of suturing is better than the common easy suture, and it is the suture whose remembrance preceded.
This is therefore also Galen's account.
And some said.
The suture of the belly is rectified with two needles.
That is, insert a single thread into both of them: and begin with the insertion of the needle from your side, and make it penetrate to the other side, according to the suturing method of those who sew leather evenly.
And know that when the rupture is in the middle of the belly, then its suturing is more difficult than in other areas of the belly.
The treatment protocol for the wound is indeed the protocol for other wounds.
That is, when you encounter a fresh wound with its blood, before the air alters it: and you return the intestine, and suture it, and adjust it: then apply the powder that promotes flesh growth.
But if the air has already altered the wound: then apply to it certain ointments which you have at hand, until they cause pus: and the threads fall out, and the sifac and mirach [abdominal layers] heal.
Then treat it as you would treat other wounds, until it heals.
If you don't have medicines at hand, then immediately after it begins to discharge pus, place old cotton on it: and change it twice a day, as I taught you, until it heals.
For you do not need other medication in most conditions, when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound might affect vital organs with injury, then you should soak soft wool in oil of moderate warmth, or in rose oil: and place it around the areas between the groin and under the armpits.
But if he feels pain, or punctures, or putrefaction in his intestines (for this often happens), then give him an enema with astringent, black, tepid wine: especially if putrefaction has already reached the intestine, and an abscess has formed penetrating to its cavity.
And know that what is from the large intestine heals more easily: and what is from the small intestine heals with more difficulty.
The intestine which is called Jejunum does not accept healing from a wound that occurs in it at all: and this is because of the abundance of veins in it, and their size, and because of the thinness of its body, and its proximity to the nature of nerves.
But if what protrudes from the wound is omentum, and you encounter it fresh: return it according to your method of returning intestines evenly.
But if time has passed, and it has already become green or black: then you must tie it with a thread above the place that has blackened, so that blood flow does not occur.
For in the omentum there are veins and arteries.
Then cut what is beyond that ligature, and leave the two ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside it: so that its attraction and extraction may be easier for you when the omentum falls and the wound discharges pus.
¶ A reminder about wounds that occur in the Intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the Intestine, and it is small: then it is possible that it may be restored in some people: because I saw someone who was wounded in his belly by a lance strike, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound became old: and turned into a Fistula, and excrement and wind came out of it.
Therefore I applied treatment, until I was not confident in his healing: nor did I cease to make it finer, until it was healed, and the area was consolidated.
After I saw the area was already consolidated, I feared for the patient, that something bad might happen to the patient in his belly: but nothing bad happened to him from that.
And he remained in his better conditions, eating and drinking, and having sexual relations, and entered the bath, and exercised in his daily routines.
And some experienced practitioners have already said that when a wound occurs in the Intestine, and it is small: then it should be bound according to this method.
And it is that you should take large-headed ants.
Then bring together the two lips of the wound, and place one of these ants which has an open mouth over the two lips of the wound.
When therefore it grabs onto them, and tightens its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres and does not loosen.
Then place another ant near the first, and do not cease doing this with ant after ant according to the size of the whole wound.
Then return it, and sew the wound.
For those heads remain attached to the Intestine until the Intestine is nourished and heals, and no harm whatsoever happens to the patient.
And it is also possible to bind the Intestine with a fine thread: which has been cleaned from an animal's intestine, attached to it after it is inserted into a needle.
And it is that you should take the end of this thread from the intestine, and clean it thoroughly.
Then tie a fine linen thread wrapped at its end.
Then insert that thread into a needle, and sew the intestine with it.
Then return it into the rupture.
And this method of suturing with ants and intestine is only according to the way of confidence and hope.
Know that a wound that is large, wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines: then there is no ingenious solution except making it finer, nor is there any healing for it at all.
¶ On the treatment of Zuchemen and Fistulas.
Chapter 88.
Know that every wound or abscess when it grows old and ancient, and becomes an ulcer, and does not consolidate, and always emits pus, and does not stop: then it is universally called a Fistula in whatever member it may be, and we call it Zuchemen.
A fistula indeed is in truth a hardened, white, filtered nodule: with which there is no pain, which has a cavity like a bird's feather: and because of that some call it Risati, that is, a feather.
And at certain times it is moist, which emits pus.
And perhaps the moisture is cut off at certain times.
And sometimes this moisture is abundant, and it is scarce: and it is thick, and it is thin.
And I have already explained in the division, when I spoke of wounds, that every ulcer that does not heal, nor does flesh grow in it, is only due to one of nine causes.
Of which one is either due to scarcity of blood in the body: or due to its poor quality: or because between it and above its lips there is hard flesh, preventing the growth of flesh: or because it has much filth and dirt: or because the ulcer itself is putrid, and the matter which is sent to it is of poor quality: or because the medicine is unsuitable in its treatment: or because the corruption falling in the region is a kind of pestilence, or by a characteristic in the region, as happens in Caesaraugusta: in which the digestion of diseases becomes difficult, and the healing of swellings is delayed there by nature: or because in it there is one bone, or a number of bones.
