And I have already shown all these causes and their signs, and their treatments with medicines in the division: and it is necessary here that I make you know the treatment of Fistula and Zuken, which is done by way of operation with the hand.
Know therefore, that Fistula occurs in all members of the body.
For among them are Fistulas which reach to the great veins and arteries, or nerves, or peritoneum: or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to any of the ribs: or to any of the vertebrae of the back, or to any of the compound joints in composition: like the hand, and foot: and among them are those which go to the proximity of a principal member, and similar things.
Therefore whichever of these Fistulas is according to this manner, is among the diseases difficult to heal, and does not accept treatment, and occupation with it is labor and ignorance.
But if there is among the Fistulas one whose depth does not reach to these places which I mentioned: and you hope, and trust in its treatment, and wish to arrive at knowledge of it, then take a probe of bronze, or iron, if the Fistula extends in a straight line, and examine it with this.
But if in the Fistula there is tortuosity, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead.
Because lead with the softness of its body is lightened, and curves around the tortuosity.
But if the Fistula has many orifices, it is not possible for you to mark it with a probe.
Therefore irrigate from these one orifice from among its orifices.
For the moisture with which you irrigate it goes toward the other orifices, and runs out of them.
Then carefully inquire according to what manner is possible for you: so that you may know whether there is bone there, or nerve, and whether the Fistula's cavity is distant or near: or whether it is one Fistula which has many orifices, and stand upon all that, with the sum of your strength: with your questioning, by which you question the patient, and finding of pain during compression with your hand over the place, and similar things from signs and causes occurring to the swelling.
When therefore you have stood upon all those things with true standing, then proceed to treatment according to confidence: and it is, that you consider then, if the Fistula is near appearing, or in a safe place distant from a joint, or from a nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places which I told you: then cut the Fistula according to what was set forth from my narrative, and remove what is in it from filtration, and corrupted flesh, which grows in its orifice from flesh, or granulation, and similar things: and treat it until it heals.
But if the Fistula is of distant depth, and is according to straightness: then it is necessary that you cut it in depth as much as is possible for you.
Then clean it from all its corrupted flesh.
Then apply bandages soaked in sharp medicines: and insert them up to the cavity of the Fistula which you have grasped with the iron: do this with it several times, until the sharp medicine erodes all corruption that remained in the cavity of the Fistula.
Then restore it with ointments that generate healthy flesh, until it heals.
If indeed it does not heal through that, then cauterize it according to what we have described.
If therefore the cause of the Fistula is bone, and this is certain to you: then examine it, and cut it according to what I told you: if nothing prohibits you such as a vein, or nerve, or principal member, as we said.
If therefore the bone is exposed to you, and there is some corruption in it, and blackness: then scrape it until all its corruption is removed.
Then treat it with that which causes it to grow flesh, until it heals.
But if it does not heal, and discharges pus as it did before: then know that you have not reached the complete eradication of the corruption.
Therefore expose it again, and carefully perform its scraping, and cleansing with your ultimate strength.
Then restore it.
If therefore it heals, it is good: and if not, then know that that corruption is in a remote depth of the body, which you have not reached.
Therefore you have no means in it, except to leave it to nature.
But if the corrupted bone is small, and it is possible for you, draw it out with fine forceps that are suitable for that.
If however there are many bones, then carefully perform the extraction of all of them, and do not leave any of them with your strength.
If indeed something from the humors opposes you, and there is no fear or error in cutting them: then cut those humors and flesh, if they are there, and free the bones.
If indeed you do not have the preparation for removing the bones at your time, then place upon the wound that which will putrefy it: and leave it until what is around those bones of flesh and humors putrefies.
And guard the wound so that it does not consolidate.
And let the incision which you cut in your operation be constricted.
Indeed place upon it, if you fear, cotton soaked in ground sulfur with oil: or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, or some of the green ointments.
For when those humors putrefy, and the bones separate from them, their removal and extraction becomes easier.
If however it is one large bone, like the shin bone, or hip bone, and similar to those, and what is corrupted from it is a large part: then scrape it with ultimate scraping, until that blackness and corruption is removed.
Then restore the wound.
If indeed what is corrupted in it is a large part, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw it, and cut it all the way to the place where the corruption has reached: then treat it until it consolidates.
And I tell you about zuken [a condition], which happened to a man in his leg: so that you may place it as an example and aid for your operation.
There was a young man aged about thirty years, to whom pain occurred in his leg, from a cause that moved upon him within the body, that is, from the interior, until materials came to the leg, and it swelled with a great swelling: and there was no external cause for him.
Time therefore extended with him with the error of physicians, until the swelling opened, and many materials ran with it.
And his treatment was postponed, until the leg became fistulated, and many orifices were made in it: all of which were discharging pus and bodily humors.
Therefore a multitude of physicians treated him for about two years, and there was not among them one wise in the art of hand [surgery], until I was called, and I saw his leg, and the materials that were running from those orifices with great flow: also all the man's body had already wasted away, and his color was yellow.
Therefore I inserted a probe in one of the places of the orifices, and the probe reached to the bone.
Then I examined all orifices: and I found them reaching to each other from all parts of the leg.
I hurried therefore, and cut above one of those openings, until I exposed part of the bone: and I found it corrupted: which was already eroded, and blackened, and had rotted, and was perforated, until it had penetrated all the way to the marrow.
Therefore I sawed what was exposed to me, and prepared from the corrupted bone.
And I determined that there was nothing in the bone except that corruption, which I cut away and sawed.
And I had already eradicated it.
Then I applied restoration of the wound with consolidating medicines for a long period.
And it did not heal.
Then I tried, and exposed the bone again above the first exposure: and I found the corruption continuous with the bone: and I sawed what appeared to me also from that corruption.
Then I attempted its restoration.
And it was not restored.
Therefore I exposed it again, and did not cease cutting the bone bit by bit, and attempting its restoration: and it was not restored, until I cut from the bone about one palm's width, and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines, and it was quickly consolidated and healed: and this repetition in its operation and its cutting was only necessary because of the weak condition of the patient, and his low tolerance, and my fear of his death: since in all hours he was suffering from bad fainting spells due to excessive evacuation.
Therefore he was healed with complete healing: and hard flesh grew in place of the bone, and his bodily condition became good, and his strength returned: and he went about his affairs: and the injury did not cause him any hindrance in walking at all.
But if a protruding bone in a part of the body is already broken: then it is necessary that you saw it, according to this method: which is: that you take a bandage, and tighten it at the end of the protruding bone: and order someone to extend it upward: and place another bandage of wool thicker than the first bandage.
Then bind it over the flesh that is under the bone: and extend its end, so that you pull the flesh downward: and you expose the flesh over the place which you want to saw, so that the saw does not harm the flesh.
Place wood, or a board under the bone below properly: because when you do that, it does not prevent cutting the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be slightly above the corrupted place, so that there is no corruption in the cavity of the bone: and it does not appear visible to the senses, and force another round of sawing.
But if the bone is corrupted, and not protruding, rather one part continues with another part: and the corruption is in its middle, or in part of it: then expose the flesh completely from all parts.
Then place wood below.
Afterward saw it from the first part, where the corruption is, until the sawing continues from the other part: and let the sawing be lengthwise a little from the corruption, according to what we said.
But if the corruption is in a joint, cut that corrupted joint, and saw the bone where it continues from the other part.
If indeed the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no solution for it except scraping.
But if the corruption is in the metacarpus of the hand, or in the metatarsus of the foot: then the matter in it is very difficult.
Indeed it is necessary that you investigate how the corruption appears to you.
And scrape it, and clean it according to whatever condition is possible for you, and with whatever technique is proper for you: when neither Vein nor Nerve opposes you.
And know that the cutting tools and Saws for cutting these bones are many, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness, and their thinness, and their size, and their smallness, and their hardness, and their porosity.
Therefore it is necessary that you prepare for every type of operation an instrument suitable for that operation.
And know that these operations indicate to you the type of instrument you need, when you have long study with you.
And the knowledge of the methods of this art, testimony of the types of diseases: for then you will find by yourself that which is suitable from the instruments for every infirmity.
And I am confirming to you in the end of this chapter the number of instruments, which you will set as examples upon which you will cut, and the proof by which you will experiment upon others.
Form of the Saw.
Form of another Saw.

Let its upper arch and its sharpness be made of iron: and its handle of boxwood, and let it be properly made pyramidal.

And it is not suitable, except to rub with it the heads of joints, when they are corrupted, or when the bone is wide and large.

¶ On the cutting of extremities, and Sawing of bones.
Chapter 89.
Extremities putrefy, either from external cause, or internal.
When therefore you have treated that corruption with treatment of medicines, and the treatment does not help: and you see corruption advancing into the limb, which nothing repels: then it is necessary that you cut that limb up to where the corruption extends, so that the patient may escape through that from death, or from an evil which is greater than the loss of a limb.
And the sign to which this happens is that the limb blackens, until it is thought that fire has burned it, or it putrefies after the blackness: until that putrefaction is cut up to that which follows that limb, and creeps into the whole body.
Hasten therefore with its cutting, and similarly if the cause of corruption is from the strike of certain venomous things: like the sea scorpion, and viper, and rutella, and similar ones.
If therefore the corruption or strike is in the extremity of a finger, then cut the finger, and do not give space to the corruption to advance up to the rest of the hand.
And similarly if it happens in the hand, then cut it from the root of the forearm bone, and do not give space to the corruption until it takes hold in the bone of the arm.
And if it invades in it, then cut the arm at the elbow in its joint.
If however the corruption passes beyond, and you see it around the elbow: then in that is the death of the patient.
And to administer treatment beyond that is a matter of strength.
And similarly do with the foot, when corruption invades in a toe, then cut it at one of the joints: and if it takes in the instep of the foot, then cut the entire foot.
If however it ascends to the knee, then cut the leg at the knee joint.
But if the corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no device in it, except abandonment, and leaving the patient to death.
And the method of cutting a limb, or sawing it is that you tighten a ligature below, which you wish to cut: and tighten another ligature above the place.
And let one minister extend one ligature downwards: and let another minister extend the other ligature upwards.
And you separate the flesh which is between the two ligatures, with a broad Spatula, until all the flesh is exposed.
Then let it be cut, or sawed.
And it is necessary that you place linen cloth on all sides, and that the saw does not touch healthy tissue: and that no additional injury and hot inflammation occurs to the patient.
If however internal bleeding occurs while you are operating, then cauterize the area quickly: or place upon it certain blood-stopping powders.
Then return to your treatment until it is completed.
Then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it heals.
And I tell you what happened to a man in his foot, this same incident that I am telling you about.
Which is, that blackness occurred in his foot, with a burning that resembles burning by fire: and that corruption first occurred in his toe, until it began to take over the entire foot.
Therefore the man hastened on his own, after he saw the corruption spreading in the limb with the intensity of what he experienced from pain and burning: and he cut it off at the joint, and was healed.
And after a long time had passed, that same corruption occurred in the index finger of his hand.
Therefore he came to me, and I tried to counteract that excess with what I placed on the hand from medicines after purifying his body: and the excess was not repelled, and it began to spread to another finger: until the corruption began in the hand.
Therefore he called me to cut off his hand: and I did not want to do that, hoping on my part for the repulsion of that excess: and I also feared death for him during the cutting off of his hand.
Since the man's strength was declining.
After therefore he despaired of me, he returned to his land: and word reached me about him, that he hastened and cut off his entire hand: and was healed.
And I have not brought this narrative, except that it might be an aid concerning what falls under this type of illness: and that it might be a sign by which it is indicated, and according to which one should act.
¶ Concerning the Treatment of Sinuses, and the Method of Administering Medicines with Clysters.
Chapter 90.
When inflammation occurs in certain fleshy members, and the duration of the inflammation is prolonged, until it makes a collection of pus: then it bursts, or is perforated, and what is in it of pus comes out, and an empty place remains as if it were a vessel: and the skin which is over it is like cloth that has already been thinned: and it has not reached the ultimate in its corruption, nor has it affected bone, nor nerve, nor ligament: it deserves to be named Sinus: and is not named Fistula: however if its duration is prolonged, until the corruption affects any of these members, then it is named Fistula, or zuken.
And its treatment is that you cut all that skin as it rolls back: and especially when it has been thinned, and made like cloth: and you are confident that it does not adhere with the place, because of its corruption.
If however you hope that the skin might adhere, and that ultimate corruption does not follow from it: and if in the skin there is thickness from flesh, then treat it with a Clyster: and it is, that you examine the Sinus.
Then if it is large, and the pus that comes out of it has a foul odor: then treat it with clyster with Egyptian medicine, which is of vinegar, and oil, and verdigris, taking equal parts, collected in a vessel: and they should be cooked over fire, until the medicine becomes red: and comes to the thickness of honey.
Then take from it what is necessary for you: and dissolve it with water and honey, and treat the Sinus with clyster: and tighten its orifice, and leave the medicine in it for the duration of two hours.
Then extract it with pressure: do that for so many days, until the Sinus is cleaned, and the foul smell is removed.
And sometimes it is treated with clyster with ash water, when this medicine is not present for you: and it is, that you take ash of vine wood, or ash from oak wood: and throw water upon it.
Then strain it, and treat with clyster with it, until you are confident that the Sinus has already been washed.
If the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine, nor the water of ashes, then give them an enema with water and honey, to which some ground verdigris has been mixed.
Or give them an enema with mixed honey and wine, since it is a property of honey to cleanse and wash away: and wine makes the Sinus adhere, especially when there is an excess of astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you do this several times, and you are confident that the corruption of the Sinus has been removed: then give them an enema with that which makes flesh grow in it.
And it is thus: take from the diaphenicon plaster and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine: or give them an enema with certain medicines and other ointments, whose properties I have written about in the treatise on ointments.
If the opening of the Sinus is narrow, such that the cannula of the enema cannot enter: then widen it slightly with an iron instrument: or place in it a tent wrapped in Egyptian ointment, or in basilicon ointment, until it is widened.
And similarly if the opening is also wide, then join its lips with sutures: and leave enough space for the enema to enter without addition.
And similarly if its opening from which pus flows is elevated toward the top: then cut it at the lowest point which is in it, so that pus may flow down from it: since when pus is confined in the depth of the Sinus, it prevents flesh from generating in it.
If it is not possible for you to cut the Sinus around its bottom according to what you want: then try to prepare the limb in such a way that pus flows from it easily according to how it is prepared for you.
Try not to retain any pus in it at all: and take consolidating medicines which are placed over the mark, suitable for what you want.
And spread them over a linen cloth.
And let the cloth be of such size that it covers the whole Sinus.
Then cut with scissors directly at the opening of the Sinus a hole, slightly larger than the opening of the Sinus.
Then spread gentle ointments over another cloth, according to the size of the hole: and place it over it at a position far from the opening of the Sinus, so that the exit of pus from it is eased: and do not remove the large cloth except after several days at once.
That which is over the opening of the Sinus is that which you remove every hour, so that what accumulates in it of pus may exit: and so that you may know whether that which flows from it of discharge is much or little: or digested or undigested.
And with this again consider the place of the Sinus itself, whether its owner feels pain in it or there is no pain in it, and whether there is swelling in it or there is no swelling in it.
When therefore your treatment proceeds in such a way, and you see the matter which exits over the opening of the Sinus, then know that the Sinus has already adhered, or approaches adherence: then therefore loose it in the morning, or on the third day: and when you loose the wound, then change another cloth, and renew the ointment.
If however there exits from the Sinus after a long time a thin discharge, then do not despair of its adherence, for its healing approaches, since among men there are those who are slow in generating flesh in their wounds, and among them are those who act contrary to that.
If however there exits from the Sinus after many days undigested pus, then know that the sinus does not adhere.
If therefore the adherence of the Sinus is delayed, and its condition is prolonged: then increase in the drying of medicines, but let physicians make it so that the medicines are moist in their substance, and dry in their virtue: like the diaphenicon ointment, when an excess of addition from zinc is added to it.
And from the most ultimate by which it is treated, is that you take diaphenicon ointment, and it is dissolved with rose oil, and ancient wine equal in its substance is sprinkled over it.
Then prepare it with this, and use it.
And it produces a similar effect, when you don't have diaphenicon ointment present, as honey does when you cook it until it thickens, and you use it.
Or take myrrh, and aloe, and frankincense: grind it all together, and sprinkle it as powder over honey while it is over the fire.
Then spread it on a cloth, and bind it tightly over the Sinus: and spread it with the honey that you cooked until it thickened: and sprinkle the spices over it, and place a cloth over it, and bind it tight.
And sometimes long aristolochia is administered in it, and roots of celestial lily, which is iris: and vetch flour, and centaury.
Use them either individually or combined, grind them, and sift them: and sprinkle them over the honey which I described, and use it.
But if the condition of the Sinus persists, and is not healed by this treatment: then know that there is corruption in its depths: or it has already made an impression in the bones, or in other hard parts, or ligaments: then treat it with the treatment for Fistulas, according to what was previously described.
On the treatment of whitlow and bruised nail, and cutting of additional finger, and section.
Chapter 91.
A whitlow is an abscess that grows under the thumbnail of the hand or foot: and perhaps it grows in the remaining fingers.
When therefore its condition is prolonged, and its treatment is neglected, and it swells with hot inflammation: or becomes corrupted, and discharges pus, until it erodes the root of the nail: and perhaps corrupts it entirely, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, until it has a foul odor: and the tip of the finger becomes wide, and its color becomes dark.
When therefore you treat it with what we have described in the division, and your treatment does not help: then you must cut away with an iron all the excess that remained from the nail.
Then cauterize the wound after that.
For cautery is very helpful.
If indeed the bone is healthy, and the nail is also healthy, and empty additions of the nail are now pressing the flesh inward, and begin to prick it, and harm it: then you must place a thin probe under the angle of the nail which pricks the flesh, and push it upward: and cut that flesh carefully, and place upon what remains of the flesh some burning, corrosive medicines, until all of it is removed.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals.
If however the corruption has already affected the bone, then you must cut that bone and extract it: for it never heals while corrupted bone remains in it.
But if you see the erosion and corruption advancing into the finger, then cut it at one of the joints, according to what was previously mentioned.
Then treat it until it heals.
If however the nail suffers bruising or a blow: and severe pain occurs in it, then you must first bleed the patient.
Then cut the nail with a sharp spatula in a transverse cut from above to what is visible.
And take care that the cut does not reach the flesh that is under the nail, for by that you will cause severe pain to the patient, and it will be a cause for the growth of additional flesh in the place.
Then treat the area with what relieves pain.
The additional finger which grows in some people's hands is perhaps entirely fleshy: and perhaps there are bones in some of them, and perhaps there is a nail in it: and some of them grow at the root of the joint, or its growth is in some of the finger's knuckles.
Therefore that which grows in some of the finger's knuckles does not move: and that which grows near the finger's joint perhaps moves.
Therefore of these, the one that is fleshy, its cutting is easy.
Which is to cut it at its root with a broad spatula.
But treatment of that whose growth is at the root of the joint is difficult.
Therefore refrain from cutting it.
In that which grows in the finger near the last knuckle, then you must first cut its flesh with a circular incision up to the bone.
Then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for that.
Then treat it until it heals.
The joining together which occurs between fingers often occurs either from a person's birth, that is, they are born with it, or from the healing of a wound, or burning by fire, and similar things.
Therefore you must cut that joining until the fingers return to their natural form.
Then place between them lint or cloths soaked in rose oil: so that they do not join together quickly, and separate them: or place between them a thin lead plate, until the area heals as it should.
And similarly if joining occurs between some fingers and the palm, then cut that joining, according to what is prepared and suits the shape of the limb.
¶ On the Incision of Varicose Veins.
Chapter 92.
Varicose veins are twisted veins, thick, full of melancholic superfluities which occur in many parts of the body: and most commonly they appear in the legs, especially in the legs of travelers and laborers, and those who carry loads.
Therefore, you should administer a purging of the body from black bile several times with strong purging.
Then perform phlebotomy on the patient's basilic vein.
The treatment with the iron instrument, however, is according to two methods.
One of them is that they are cut, and the black blood is drawn out.
And the other method is that the vein is raised up and extracted completely.
The cutting of it is according to this method.
First, bathe the leg with very hot water, until the thick, murky blood is dissolved.
Then bind the patient's leg from the upper part of his hip down to the lower part of the knee with a band.
Then cut the vein in one place, or two, or three with a wide incision.
Then lift up, that is, extract the blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg up to the top, and from above it is necessary that you first perform phlebotomy on the patient.
Then cut the edge continuously downward until there comes out an amount of blood which you see to be sufficient, and which the patient's strength can tolerate.
Afterward bind it: and instruct him to abstain from foods that generate black bile: and repeat the evacuation and phlebotomy when the veins become full, and this troubles the patient.
The lifting up of it, however, is according to this method.
Shave the patient's leg, if there are many hairs on it.
Then place it in a bath: or bathe it on his leg with hot water until it becomes red and the veins swell: or let him exercise with strong exercise, if a bath is not available to him, until the limb becomes warm.
Then cut the skin directly over the vein with a lengthwise incision: either at its end near the knee, or in its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and strip the vein from all sides until it becomes visible to the senses: and upon its appearance, you will see it reddish tending toward black.
When therefore it emerges from the skin, you will see it white as if it were a cord.
Then insert beneath it a probe, until when it is raised and comes out of the skin: suspend it with a blunt, smooth hook.
Then make another incision near that incision at three fingers' distance.
Then strip the skin from the upper part of the vein until it appears.
Then lift it with probes as you did before, and suspend it with another hook, as you did at first.
Then make another incision, or several incisions, if you need that.
Then lift up and cut it with a final incision near the heel.
Then draw it out to the incision which is above it.
And do this until you draw it out from the incision which is higher or highest of all the incisions, until when it comes out completely: then cut it.
If however it does not yield to you for extracting and lifting: then insert a needle with a strong double thread, and bind it, and extract it: or insert a probe under it: and turn your hand to every side until it comes out.
And take care that it is not cut.
For if it is cut, it becomes very difficult for you to lift it, and harm enters upon the patient from it.
When therefore you have extracted it completely: then place upon the site of the wounds wool soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil: and treat it until it heals.
But if the varicose vein is contracted, having tortuosity which has coiling to the sides, and is not as we said in a straight line: then it is necessary that you split over it at every part of its tortuosity and the places of its coiling.
Then suspend it with hooks until you lift all of it: and take care in your cutting over it that you do not cut or wound the vein: for it will become difficult for you to lift or extract it.
Therefore preserve your prudence.
Form of the Cutting Instrument with which Varicose Veins are Treated.
Form of the Blunt Hook.
It does not have curvature like the other hooks, nor does it have a sharp end, so that it does not wound the vein: and it has thick bendability, smooth: because if it were thin, it would cut the vein by its thinness: rather it should have excessive thickness, as we said.
On the extraction of the Leg Vein.
Chapter 93.
This vein forms in the legs in hot lands, like Alizegi and the land of the Arabs, and in dry, hot lands of little tenderness, that is, in which neither herb nor tree grows.
And perhaps it forms in other places of the body besides the legs.
And indeed its generation is from putrefaction that occurs under the skin, just as snakes and ascarid worms occur within bodies, and worms generated between the skin and flesh.
And the sign of the beginning of this vein's appearance is that severe inflammation occurs in the leg.
Then the area becomes blistered.
Then the vein begins to emerge from the site of that blister, as if it were a plant root or an animal.
When therefore its end appears, then it is necessary that you wrap around it a piece of lead, whose weight should be from one drachma keil up to two drachmas, and tie it: and leave the lead hanging from the leg.
Therefore whenever something of it comes out to the exterior, wrap it in the lead, and tie it.
But if it extends greatly, then cut off part of it, and wrap up the remainder.
And do not cut it at the root before it has all emerged.
Because if you cut it, it contracts and enters into the flesh: and causes an abscess and putrefaction in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore it is necessary that it be handled gently and extracted gradually until it emerges from its end, and nothing of it remains in the body: and indeed from this vein there emerges in some men what is in length five to ten palms.
And it has come to my attention that it came out of a man to twenty palms.
If however it is cut during your treatment: then insert a probe into its opening, and pierce it with a long hole, along with the body, until all the matter that is in it is evacuated: and treat the putrefaction of the area with medicines for several days.
Then treat it with the treatment for abscesses.
And indeed this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears in the ankle joint or in the foot itself: and many openings occur to it, and from each opening emerges a branch.
Therefore treat it as we said in the division, and with what was previously mentioned.
¶ On cutting for Worms generated under the skin, and it is called the Bovine Disease.
Chapter 94.
This Disease is named in a certain land among us the Bovine Disease: because it very often occurs in cattle, and it is one small worm that is born between the skin and flesh.
And it goes throughout the whole body, ascending and descending: and is detected by sensation during its movement from member to member, until it breaks through wherever it breaks through the skin, and emerges.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humors.
Just as worms and serpents occur, that is, roundworms and ascarids in the belly.
And its malice is feared.
Because when it travels in the body and rises to the head and reaches a spot: then perhaps it opens in it and emerges and destroys the eye: and this happens many times.
When therefore you wish to treat it and extract it, then this is not done except during its movement and when it is detectable by sensation.
Therefore it is necessary that you bind what is above it and what is below it with a bandage with strong constriction.
Then cut above it and extract it.
But if it goes deep into the flesh and you cannot find it: then place a cautery with fire over the spot until it dies.
And the greatest thing that is feared is its corruption by which it corrupts the eye, as we said.
If however you see it has already reached the head near the eye, then bind below it the eyebrow with good constriction.
Then cut above it and extract it.
And it is necessary that the patient use purification of his body with medicines that dissolve putrid bad humors.
And let him beware of foods that generate putrefaction.
¶ On the surgery for the illness of Nakir.
Chapter 95.
This illness is called Nakir in our land: and it is a pain that occurs in certain body parts: then it moves from one part to another.
And indeed I have seen it myself, according to what I tell you.
I was called to a sick woman in certain villages.
And she uncovered her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the rope-like vein of the arm.
And after I remained for one hour, I saw one swelling moving along the forearm, like a worm moves, ascending to her shoulder more quickly than possible: so that it was like quicksilver when it runs from place to place.
Therefore the pain left from that place, and became fixed in the shoulder.
Then I sat for one hour, and it ran through the rest of the body, until it reached the other arm.
Then she told me that it was revolving throughout her whole body, according to what I saw.
I was amazed therefore at the speed of its movement from member to member.
And indeed before that I had not seen this illness with my own eyes in such a way according to this manner.
However, I saw many who experience pain moving from member to member: but I did not see it with my eyes as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think it to be anything except that it is because the woman was one of those from the villages with a dry body and visible veins: hence therefore that moving windiness appears to the senses.
And it is necessary that they do not appear in this way in those who are delicate, and with moist bodies, of hidden veins.
Therefore when you want to cure it, and the patient feels that pain: then if it is visible to your eye, as we said, then bind above it and below it quickly: and cut above it until that confined windiness comes out: and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your eyes: then treat it with shaking of the body, and with that which expels windinesses, and disperses them with fetid pills, and pills of sagapenum, and similar things.
¶ On the Extraction of Arrows.
Chapter 96.
Arrows are differentiated according to their types, and according to the places in the body where they fall.
Their diversity indeed is: because among them are those which have large and small points: and hollow ones, and solid ones.
And among them are those which have three angles.
And among them are those which have barbs.
And among them are those which have splinters.
Those indeed which are according to the members in which they fall, are in two ways.
For either they are members from the principal hollow members, such as the brain, and heart, and liver, and lung, and kidneys, and intestines, and bladder, and similar ones.
Therefore when it falls in any of these members, and the signs of death appear to you, which I tell you about afterwards, then it is necessary that you avoid extracting that arrow from them, for death follows that person in whom it is, in most cases.
And when these bad signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the member, then extract it, and treat the wound.
Indeed among the signs of the brain when an arrow falls in it, and penetrates the bone, and exits the membrane which is over the brain, is: that from it occurs severe headache, and dizziness, and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and swelling, and redness of the tongue, and spasm, and confusion of intellect, and vomiting of bile.
And perhaps blood comes out from the nostrils, or ears.
And perhaps speech is cut off, and the voice is destroyed.
And from the place of the wound comes out a white moisture similar to porridge.
And from it exits something like watery flesh.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment, and extract the arrow if it does not exit.
The signs indeed of an arrow when it falls in the heart, are: that it is near the left breast: and one feels with it as if it were fixed in a hard thing, not in an empty thing.
And perhaps the arrow has a motion similar to the motion of the pulse: and black blood runs from the wound.
And this is followed by coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting.
Know therefore, that death descends without doubt.
And the sign when an arrow wounds the lung is the exit of foamy blood from the wound: and the vessels that follow the neck swell, and the color of the patient changes.
And he exhales great, deep breaths: and seeks to draw in cold air.
If the arrow falls into the membrane which is in the chest: then it is near the small ribs: and there is great breathing with severe pain, with sighing: and all parts of the shoulders move.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach, then perhaps something undigested from the food exits from the wound.
And if the arrow falls into the Belly, and is fixed in it: something of excrement exits from the wound, or omentum, or intestine which has already been ruptured.
There is therefore no hope in its cure, nor in the extraction of the arrow.
If the arrow falls into the Bladder, and urine comes out: and something proceeds from it to the exterior, and the pain becomes stronger upon the patient: then know that it is mortal.
The remaining members, however, such as the face, and neck, and throat, and shoulder blade, and upper arm, and vertebrae of the back, and collarbone, and hip, and leg, and similar members can be saved in most cases: when the arrow does not find an artery, or nerve: and the arrow is not poisoned.
And I tell you what I have seen of these arrows, so that you may understand through it your treatment.
Which is, that to a certain man an arrow fell in the tear duct of his eye, at the root of his nose.
I therefore extracted it from the other side, under the fat of his ear: and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which fell into the fat of his eye under the lower eyelid: and the arrow was already hidden, and I only found its small end, with which it adheres to its wooden shaft: and it was a large arrow from Turkish bows, with smooth iron, in which there are no barbs.
The Jew was therefore healed, and no bad accident happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arabic arrow, and it is one that has barbs.
I therefore cut between two veins: and it was already submerged in his throat.
I therefore worked carefully with it, until I extracted it: and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his belly: and we thought that he would die from it.
After therefore thirty days had passed, and nothing had changed in his condition, I cut upon the arrow: and I worked upon it with skill, and extracted it: and he was healed, and no bad accident happened to him.
And I saw a man to whom an arrow fell in his back, and the wound healed over it.
After therefore it was seven years later, the arrow came out at the root of his hip.
And I saw a woman in whose belly an arrow fell, and the wound healed over it, and the arrow remained within her: and none of her conditions changed, and the woman found no harm from it in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man, in whose face an arrow fell: and the wound healed, and remained, not causing him much pain: and many similar cases to these.
And I extracted an arrow from a man among the King's princes, which had fallen into the middle of his nose, and had already declined slightly to the right side: and the entire arrow was hidden.
I was therefore called to treat him three days after the arrow's fall.
I found therefore the arrow wound very narrow.
I therefore searched for it with a fine probe, and did not feel it: and he indeed found a pricking and pain under his ear on the right side.
I therefore hoped that this pricking was from the tip of the arrow.
I therefore plastered the place with a plaster in which was attractive and digestive power, trusting in myself that the place would form an abscess, and the sign of the arrow would appear to me, so that I might cut upon it: and no symptom occurred in the place indicating that the arrow had reached the spot.
I therefore continued with the plaster upon him for many days: and no symptom occurred, and the wound healed meanwhile: and the patient remained concerned about its extraction for a period of days, until one day he felt the arrow within his nose.
And he told me that.
Therefore I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to that wound for many days, until it opened: and with a probe I probed it, and felt the thin end of the arrow which was stuck in the bone.
Then I increased the opening of the wound through that sharp medicine, until the end of the arrow became visible to us: and about four months of time passed with this.
Then after the wound was widened, and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it, and move it, and it did not yield to exit: then I did not cease to think carefully, and work on it with ingenious methods, until I took hold of it one day with properly made forceps, according to the form that will come at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it.
Then I restored the wound.
And indeed doctors judge that the cartilage of the nose does not restore itself.
Therefore I restored it, and the wound was consolidated, and the patient was healed with complete health: nor did anything harm him at all.
And I tell you the method of extraction of certain arrows: so that you may make it a rule and indication for what I have not said.
Since the parts of this art and their distinctions are not comprehended through narrative: nor does a book comprehend it, and the prudent craftsman compares with little against much: and with what is present, against what is absent: and finds new operation, and new instrument for the descent of foreign things of this art when they occur.
Therefore I say that arrows do not exit from the members in which they are fixed, except according to two modes.
Either through pulling from the place from which it entered.
Or contrary to the other part.
And that which exits where it entered, either is an arrow proceeding in a fleshy place: and it is pulled, and exits.
But if it does not yield to exit from the hour in which it falls: then it is necessary that you leave it for some days, until the flesh which is around it putrefies: and its extraction and its exit become easier.
And similarly if it is fixed in bone, and does not yield to you for exit, then leave it also for some days: and attempt it with pulling and moving every day, until it exits.
But if it does not yield to you for exit after days, then it is necessary that you perforate around the arrow in the bone itself from all parts with a fine drill, until they are widened for the arrow.
Then pull it, and extract it.
If however the arrow is fixed in the bone of the head, and has already proceeded into one of the ventricles of the brain, and certain of those accidents which I mentioned appear in the patient: then abstain from pulling the arrow, and leave it, until its being is equalized after days.
For if the arrow reaches the membrane, then death is not prolonged for him.
And if the arrow is not fixed, except in the body of the bone only, and does not penetrate up to the membrane: and the patient remains for some days, and none of those accidents happens to him: then be ingenious in pulling the arrow and extracting it.
But if it is very firmly fixed, and does not yield to you for pulling: then employ small drills around the arrow, as I told you: then treat the place, until it heals.
If however the Arrow is already hidden in a place of the body, and is concealed and hidden from sense: then investigate it with a probe.
If however you feel it, then pull it with certain instruments which are suitable for pulling it.
If however you cannot [succeed] with it, because of the constriction of the wound, and because of the length of the arrow in depth: and there is neither bone there, nor nerve, nor vein: then split [the wound] above it, until the wound is widened, and it becomes possible to extract the arrow.
But if it has ears by which it is held, then free the flesh fixed in them from all parts, by every ingenious means by which you can do that, and be ingenious, if you cannot [succeed] in freeing the flesh in the breaking of the ears and twisting them, until it comes free.
And when you perform the extraction of an Arrow, wherever it may be, then use the twisting of your hand, with forceps in all directions, until you make it come out: and be extremely subtle, so that the Arrow does not break, and its removal and extraction does not become difficult for you.
If you cannot [extract] it at once, then leave it for several days, until what surrounds it putrefies.
Then try again: for then it will be easier. But if bleeding causes you trouble, then apply what we described about treatment in its chapter: and guard your skill from cutting a vein, or nerve, or cord.
And employ every possible method of Arrow removal that is possible for you: and let it be with ease, and calmness, or slowness, and firmness, as I have described to you.
And it is necessary, when you pull the Arrow, that the patient be in the same position as when the Arrow struck him.
For this is most helpful.
If however this is not possible for you, then employ whatever position is possible.
But an Arrow which exits from the opposite side, or has already proceeded somewhat to the exterior: or the tip of the Arrow makes a bulge that can be felt from above the nearby skin, and you see it protruding: then cut above it, and let the incision be of such size that the forceps can grasp it.
Then pull it: for it becomes easy to remove.
If however it is stuck in bone, then twist your hand in a circular motion, until the Arrow makes an impression in the bone, and widens [the space] for itself: then pull it.
And if not: leave it for several days: then try again, until it comes out.
If the wooden shaft of the Arrow is still in it, then push it with that.
If however the wood has fallen off, and you wish to push it: then insert a hollow instrument, so that its cavity enters the Arrow's tail: then push it with this.
If however the Arrow is hollow, then push it with an instrument that enters into it.
For the Arrow becomes easier [to remove] by this method.
If the Arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary that you hollow out all the flesh in which there is poison: if this is possible for you.
Then treat it with what is appropriate for that.
If the Arrow has fallen into the chest, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side: and it is close enough that you can touch it with a probe, and it is possible for you to cut above it: then cut, and beware of cutting a vein or nerve, and extract it.
Then sew up the wound, if you think appropriate, and treat it until it heals.
And this is the form of the Forceps, with which Arrows are extracted.
Let the tips be like a bird's beak, which are made like irons: when they grasp the Arrow, they do not let it go.
And many types of these are made, large, and small, and medium, according to the size of the Arrow's magnitude, and its smallness, and the width of the wound, and its narrowness.
Form of the hollow Pusher.
And form of the solid Pusher.
This is with a solid tip like a Rod, so that its entry into the hollow arrow may be easier, and it may be pushed with it.
The tip of this hollow pusher is hollow like a bird's feather: so that the entry of the arrow into it and its pushing may be easier.
¶ On the Bloodletting of Veins.
Chapter 97.
The veins to be opened for bloodletting, which are commonly used in the body, are thirty.
Of these, sixteen are in the head: two pulsating veins, which are behind the ears, called the two Herbs: and two Arteries, which are visible in both temples: and two veins which are in the tear ducts of both eyes, which are called the Watching ones: and the vein prepared in the middle of the forehead: and the vein which is at the tip of the nose.
And two Guidegi, which are in the neck.
And two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth.
And two veins which are in the lower lip.
And these are the veins which are called Algecherich.
And two veins which are under the tongue.
The veins which are opened for bloodletting in the arms are five veins in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalic, and it is on the outer side: and common people call it the head vein.
And the Dark one.
And there is the Middle vein, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilic and a branch of the Cephalic: and common people call it the body vein.
And the Basilic, which is positioned on the inner side: and is also called the Axillary.
And common people call it the belly vein.
And the Rope of the arm, which is positioned over the forearm: and it is the one which is opened for bloodletting, and it is the one which appears above the thumb with clear visibility.
And the Salvatella: and it is the vein which is between the ring finger and the little finger: and it has two branches.
And in the leg and foot there are three veins.
One of them is that which is under the curve of the big toe on the outer side.
And the second is the Saphenous: and its location is near the heel on the inner side.
And the Nesse vein: and its location is near the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three similar veins.
¶ On the bloodletting of the two veins behind the Ears.
The bloodletting of the two veins which are behind the Ears is beneficial for chronic catarrhs, and migraine, and sahafati, and old evil ulcers of the head.
And the method of bleeding it is as I describe.
That is, that the patient's head should be shaved: and his occiput should be rubbed in the location of the two veins with a rough cloth with good friction.
Then the patient should bind his neck with a band, until the two veins appear.
And their locations are behind the Ears in two depressed areas of the head.
Search for them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark there with ink.
Then take a knife-like lancet, which is called the Cutting one.
Then insert it under the vein in the skin, until the lancet reaches the bone.
Then raise your hand with the vein and skin upward, and cut the vein with the skin in an incising cut.
And let the length of the incision be the width of two joined fingers, or thereabouts: and let out as much blood as you wish.
Then bind it with small pads, and leave it until it heals.
And their incision and cauterization has already been addressed at the beginning of the book.
¶ On the bloodletting of the two Arteries in the temples.
The benefit of bloodletting from the two Arteries which are in the temples is for chronic migraine: and severe headache, and persistent eye inflammation: and the flow of acute excessive fluids to the eyes.
And the method of bleeding them is as I describe to you.
The patient should bind his neck tightly, until the two veins appear clearly visible to the senses: and their pulse is demonstrated under your finger.
Then mark with ink.
Then lift the skin above the vein upward with your index finger, and insert the cutting lancet from below, and lift the vein upward.
And cut it, as you did with the other two veins: and let the blood flow according to your required amount.
Then release the patient's constraint, and place your finger over the vein for one hour.
Afterward, place cotton and small pads over it, and bind it above with strong constraint: and leave it until it heals.
And their mention, their incision, and their extraction has already been addressed at the beginning of the book.
¶ On bloodletting of the forehead vein after bloodletting of the Cephalic vein.
Indeed, the benefit of bloodletting from the forehead vein after bloodletting of the Cephalic vein is for chronic diseases of the face: and for migraine, and redness: and foul ulcers.
And the method of its bloodletting is according to what I will tell you.
Let the patient constrict his neck with a band until the vein appears.
Then take the instrument which is called the Fossorium.
This is its form.
Place the protruding point that is at the head of the Fossorium over the vein itself, and strike it with a comb, or with another thing like it: and let the blood flow according to the quantity you want.
Then loosen the patient's perforation, and bind it until it heals.
And sometimes it is opened with a broad lancet.
However, it is not necessary that the lancet have a sharp end, like other lancets, rather it should have a broad end.
And perform the bloodletting crosswise: since the bone is near: and perhaps the lancet would break in it when it is thin.
¶ On bloodletting of the Veins in the two Tear Ducts.
Moreover, the benefit of the two veins which are in the two tear ducts of the eyes is for the eye affected by scabies and redness: and sebel in the eye, and diseases of the face.
And the method of their bloodletting is that the patient should bind his neck with a band.
Then perform bloodletting on them: and you should be standing over his head.
And let the bloodletting be done somewhat crosswise to the length, with a small, somewhat broad lancet, for there is no flesh in the location.
For if the lancet is of thin end, perhaps it breaks.
Then let the blood flow as much as is necessary for you.
And place cotton over them, and bind them for one night, and loosen them.
¶ On bloodletting of the Veins of the Nose.
Moreover, bloodletting of the veins of the nose is beneficial for acute fever, and severe headache, and diseases of the face, such as migraine, and redness which occurs in the face: especially when it is chronic.
And the method of its bloodletting is that the patient should bind his neck.
Then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a thin, long lancet: and fix it in the middle of its hare-lip, between that which divides the nose according to straightness.
For the vein does not appear visible there, and because the blood begins to flow immediately.
And it is necessary that you steady your hand with the lancet somewhat: and let the blood flow according to your need.
Then bind it for one night.
For it heals quickly.
¶ On bloodletting of the Guidegi veins.
Indeed, the benefit of bloodletting of the two Guidegi is for constriction of breathing, and the beginning of leprosy, and melancholic diseases, and impetigo and bad ulcers, and corrosive conditions, that is, spreading herpes.
And the method of their bloodletting is that the patient should bind below both in his neck with a bandage: and the practitioner should stand over the patient's head, and the patient should be sitting on a seat.
Then perform bloodletting on the vein lengthwise with a somewhat wide incision.
Then draw out a moderate quantity of blood, or according to what you see from the necessity which exists for it.
Then do similarly with the other vein.
Afterwards loosen the bandage, and bind the two veins with moderate tightness: so that the patient is not suffocated.
And leave it until morning.
For the wound heals.
On the bloodletting of the Algeberich veins.
The benefit of bloodletting from the Algeberich veins is: because it helps with thrush in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and bad ulcers, and cracks in the lips, and bad ulcers, even those that are in the nose and around it.
And the method of bloodletting from them is that you make the patient sit before you, and bind their neck with a band.
Then turn back their lip, and look for two veins: one of which is on the right of the lip, and the second on the left of it: and both can be identified by their blackness.
And this is true: because around them are minute, black veins.
Therefore cut both with an incising cut.
If however there is doubt upon you, and you do not know which of them it is: then intend to cut the larger of them, and the more visible one.
And similarly do with the two veins which are in the upper lip: and indeed most commonly practice runs with it: and cut the two veins which are in the lower lip.
On bloodletting of the vein under the tongue after Cephalic bloodletting.
The benefit of bloodletting from the two veins which are under the tongue is: that bloodletting of the Cephalic is for suffocations which are in the throat, and sickness of the uvula, and sicknesses of the mouth.
And the method of bloodletting from them is that the patient sits between your hands facing the sun: and raise their tongue, and look under the tongue on one side for a vein, and on the other side for a vein: and the color of both tends toward blackness.
Therefore let blood from both: and be careful, lest you take your hand too far in letting blood from them.
For under them are arteries, and perhaps there will occur a flow of blood from those arteries.
On the three veins in the elbow.
The three veins which are bled in the elbow are those in whose bloodletting practice has run in all people.
And their bloodletting is done according to two methods.
Either by puncturing with a broad myrtle-leaf lancet: or with an olive-shaped one inclined to fineness: or by cutting with a knife-like lancet, which is the Alnesil.
And this is its form.
Thus Alnesil is what is suitable for cutting.
And among them are broad and fine types according to the width of the veins and their narrowness. The form of the myrtle-leaf lancet should be broad, as you see.
And it is suitable for opening hollow, full, protruding, visible, thick veins, and those which contain thick, turbid blood.
And this lancet is of lesser width, and finer at the tip.
And it is suitable for bloodletting of fine veins, which contain thin yellow blood.
On bloodletting of the Basilic vein.
The benefit of bloodletting from the Basilic, which is one of these three veins, is:
Because it draws blood from sicknesses which are below the throat and neck, from that which follows the chest and belly: and it is proper for the bloodletter when he lets blood from it, to be careful, and to be fearful of it for under it is an artery.
If therefore he errs, and adds to the puncture of the lancet, he will cut that artery, and there will occur a flow of blood.
Wherefore it is necessary, not to let blood from it with a puncturing lancet: rather it should be cut with the Alnesil.
But if the Basilic does not appear with clear appearance, then it is necessary that you avoid it, and change to another, or seek some branch of it: or let blood instead from the Rope of the arm.
For it is visible, and cut it with the Alnesil lancet, as we said.
If however you want bloodletting from it itself, then it is necessary before binding the arm, that you touch the place, until you know the place of the pulse.
Then mark the place with ink, afterwards the arm, and cut the vein with a hollow cut with the Alnesil lancet, as we said.
And be careful lest the strike fall through space from the place of the artery.
And when you see, when you tighten the binding, swelling in the place which you marked with ink, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery: therefore avoid it.
If you see blood at bloodletting leap out like the urine of a boy, and it is thin, red blood: then know that it is from the blood of an artery.
Then therefore hurry, and place your finger over it for a long time, then remove your finger.
If therefore the blood stops flowing (for it often does stop), then bind the arm and release it: and the patient should avoid moving it, and should be cautious with it.
And he should not move it for several days, until it heals.
If however the blood does not stop, and overcomes you, and you don't have medicine present at the moment, then cut the artery, if it appears to you.
For its ends contract, and the blood stops.
Or take the bark of the pistachio tree, and split it: take one half, and bind it over the place of the vein with proper binding, with bandages and small cushions until the next day.
Then if the blood stops, it is good.
And if not: then treat it with what was previously mentioned from the section on powders that stop blood flow: and stopping its blood is not difficult in most cases, because of the small size of the wound, and the possibility of binding from the arm.
¶ Concerning phlebotomy of the Median vein.
The benefit of phlebotomy of the Median vein is that it draws blood from the upper part of the head and lower part of the body: because it is composed of a branch of the Basilic and a branch of the Cephalic, as we said.
And it is proper for one performing phlebotomy to be cautious when bleeding it, for under it is a nerve.
If therefore he goes too deep in the puncture with the lancet and finds the nerve, numbness occurs in it, and healing becomes difficult, and perhaps it never fully heals.
And this nerve is often visible to the senses.
If however it is hidden in some people, and is subtle, and not visible: then you should make your phlebotomy incision with the Alnesil, and avoid the nerve with all your strength.
But if the vein is between two nerves, then split the vein lengthwise.
¶ Concerning the Cephalic vein.
The benefit of phlebotomy of the Cephalic vein is that it draws blood from the head, and helps with diseases of the eyes.
And it is necessary specifically for this vein, if you wish to perform phlebotomy on it, to puncture it with an olive-shaped lancet, or with a myrtle-shaped lancet: since it is the safest of all veins, as there is neither artery nor nerve beneath it.
Nevertheless, during phlebotomy, it is proper for you to avoid the head of the muscle with the lancet, and seek a softer spot: and it does not harm it if it is not found with the first strike to repeat the phlebotomy on it several times.
Nevertheless, it may become swollen in some people when it is not bled on the first strike, but that swelling does not harm it in any way.
¶ Concerning the utility of Phlebotomy, and precautions to be observed in it.
The quality of Phlebotomy, and its effects, about which rectification you should think.
First therefore of that which you should know, that phlebotomy is not administered except in preservation of health, and its maintenance, and precaution against the arrival of diseases.
And that phlebotomy should be done in one of the three veins which are in the elbow, namely the Cephalic, the Median, and the Basilic.
And that phlebotomy should be done at the beginning of Spring on an empty stomach, in spring and summer: and in autumn and winter after eating, when signs of repletion appear.
And phlebotomy should be done on the first day, or on the third, after three hours of the day have passed.
But phlebotomy which is administered in diseases has no determined hour: but when convenience and necessity provoke it, at night or day, and at any hour, and at any time.
And children should not be bled until fourteen years have come upon them.
Nor should a pregnant woman be bled, except from the fourth month until the seventh.
Nor should the elderly be bled, who have already passed sixty years.
Therefore when anyone wants to be bled for whatever reason it may be: it is proper that he clean his intestines before phlebotomy with a gentle clyster, if there is much retained waste in them: so that the veins do not draw putrid superfluities harmful to the principal members from the intestines during phlebotomy.
Neither should one suffering from toctina, that is, nauseating satiety be bled: nor one who is drunk, nor one recovering from drunkenness, until that condition is removed from them.
Nor one sick with dropsy.
And bloodletting should be avoided after colic pain, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive sexual intercourse, labor, exercise, sleeplessness, fasting, and anything that dissolves strength from bodily or mental causes.
Then consider the thinness of humors before that, if the blood is thick with foods and drinks, and medicines: if that is possible for him.
Then let him enter the bath, if nothing prohibits it: or let him exercise with some exercise, so that the blood becomes thinner.
And place his bloodletting on the chest, around the third hour of the day, as we said: and strive that his chest be emptied that day of all harmful mental accidents: such as anxiety, anger, and fear: and of all bodily accidents, such as labor, pain, excessive intercourse, and similar things.
And let him have present in his seat things which people customarily use, such as aromatics, fragrances, dancing and similar things according to possibility.
Then let the bloodletter sit on a cushion that is higher than the cushion of the one to be bled: afterwards let him extend his arm, and let the bloodletter rub it with his hand two or three times.
Then let him tighten the binding with a strap, and wrap it twice.
And let the tightness be moderate, since when the tightness is uneven, or excessive in tightness, then it prevents the flow of blood: or in looseness, then it also prevents the flow of blood.
Then after the binding, it is necessary that the one to be bled rub his hands together until the veins swell and become visible to the senses.
Then let the bloodletter wipe the lancet with a little oil, specifically old oil.
Then let him place his left hand's index finger on the vein itself slightly below the place where he wants to perform the bloodletting: so that the vein doesn't slip away and the strike miss.
For among veins there is one that you will find like a fleeing string during bloodletting: and among them is one that is full of windiness: and when you place the lancet on it, it contracts under the lancet, and the bloodletter is deceived, and the lancet does not open the vein: and if it opens it, it is narrow: therefore for that reason it is necessary that the bloodletter be steady, and be careful in all these matters.
Then let him lower the lancet.
If therefore the vein opens from one attempt, then it is good.
And if not, then repeat it another time slightly below that place, or above it with haste: if the place is not swollen.
But if it swells, or if the patient becomes frightened, then leave him for a day or two days: and do not tighten the binding.
For perhaps it will draw a hot swelling.
Neither should he enter the bath.
Then let him repeat the bloodletting, if he wishes.
But if the opening is small, or the blood runs thinly: and you fear that the amount of blood you want will not come out: then repeat the lancet in the same hole in a straight line, and increase the operation slightly.
And do that with haste, before the place swells, for a swollen place occurs in many people during a small operation.
If therefore you see it already swollen, then do not repeat over it at all, for nothing will excuse you in this.
And place on it some of the dregs of oil: for it eases the flow of blood.
And it is better in this place than the oil itself, and than other oils.
And similarly administer oil dregs in all your bloodletting, with which you let blood from veins when the flow of blood is prevented: and theriac alfarach and sagzenea do that, when one of them is placed on the spot.
For blood becomes thin and dissolves, when it is thick.
But if a large swelling occurs in the place of bloodletting, for it often occurs, especially in one who has not been bled except that time, or if the opening of the vein is small, then hurry and place on the spot a sponge soaked in water and salt, warmed slightly: and bind it for one hour: for it will be resolved.
And it is necessary that you do that after the exit of blood from that vein itself, with its completion, or from another vein.
If any blackness or greenness remains in the place after some days, this is not harmful: and if you wish, put on it some aloe, or dissolved myrrh: or some juice of wild mint, that is, calamint or something similar to it.
And very often swellings and protuberances occur during the bloodletting of the basilic vein.
Therefore place your hand upon it, then if you find softness when you press upon it, then that swelling is bad.
Take care, therefore, not to put anything from what we mentioned on it.
For perhaps arterial blood will flow from it.
But make a plaster for it with something that has astringent properties, so that the area hardens.
Then treat it with the remaining treatment until it heals.
And it is necessary to draw blood from every person according to the measure of their strength.
And according to what appears from the predominant color of the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it flow until it becomes red: and similarly if you see it is thick: then let it flow until it becomes thin: and likewise if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And for one who is full-blooded, strong, and needs blood extraction at once, the vein opening should be made wide, and the lancet should be broad: and for one who is weak, it should be the opposite of this.
And it is necessary to draw it out in stages, and the opening should be narrow: and the best procedure in bloodletting is that the cut should be transverse, not a puncture.
And this method of bloodletting is safe from blood flow and from nerve cutting: and it is more praiseworthy and safer than bloodletting through width and length.
And for one who is accustomed to fainting during bloodletting, it is necessary to feed them before bloodletting a little bread soaked in pomegranate water mixed with honey: or oxymel, if they are heated: and draw blood from them in three or four stages.
And if they are of cold complexion, then they should take before bloodletting bread soaked in grape syrup, or in honey syrup flavored with spices, or in aromatic fragrant syrup.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting, and its cause is the exit of much blood: then it is necessary that they drink meat broth and light fragrant syrup: and use aromatics with civet, and their chest should be anointed with it: and use the remaining things which we mentioned in the division in the chapter on fainting from evacuation.
For one who wants to open their arm and release their blood a second time: it is necessary for one whose bloodletting is for much evacuation, and whose strength is weak, to release blood gradually according to their strength over continuous days.
But one who wants to open their arm and release their blood a second time, and their body is strong: then let them do this after seven or nine hours from their first bloodletting.
And one who wants to draw blood from their body to the opposite of the part to which it inclines: it is necessary that they be opened on the second or third day.
In whose body there is much blood that has become heated, and becomes sharp, and causes fever to occur: it is necessary that blood exit from them at once, and much quantity should be drawn from them: and the opening should be widened until fainting occurs: after you have observed all the conditions of bloodletting: and that you place your hand on their pulse during the flow of blood, so that death does not occur instead of fainting.
For often this happens when the bloodletter is ignorant, and negligence occurs.
And when you want to open the arm and release blood a second time, when the opening of the vein has already closed, and the exit of blood becomes difficult, it is not necessary to press upon it with force, and quickly extend what is curved.
For that is very bad, rather leave it until it is bled a second time.
Or remove with the sharpness of the lancet that which has coagulated from blood in the opening of the vein, and put on it some salt dissolved in water.
And put on it some of the theriac alfaroch, or sagzenea, and place it with gentle pressure, until the blood comes out.
If indeed the vein has become swollen, then leave it, and do not cover it until the swelling subsides.
But if necessity calls for drawing blood a second time, and there is no excuse, then either perform phlebotomy above that place, or on the other arm in another vein.
Moreover, the cord of the arm is bled in place of the Fusca and Basilic veins, when they cannot be found and are hidden, since it is composed of them.
And the method of bleeding it is that the patient should put their hand in warm water until the forearm reddens and the vein appears clearly.
Then it should be bound above with a bandage, with moderate tightness.
Then perform phlebotomy gradually across the vein, not widthwise, nor lengthwise: and let the opening be wide, and let your incision with which you perform phlebotomy be slightly above the wrist joint.
If the blood flow is blocked, then put the hand back in warm water, and let the blood flow into the water until you achieve your need.
If however you are in the summer days, then you are excused from repeating the hand in warm water.
And indeed the flow of blood is mostly placed in warm water in winter time.
And the bleeding of this vein is safer than all veins, since there is neither a pulsing vein nor a nerve beneath it.
The bleeding of the Salvatella vein of the right hand is beneficial for diseases of the liver.
And the method of bleeding it is that you bind the palm of the hand with a bandage, or with your hand, after you put it in warm water, until the vein swells and becomes very apparent to the senses.
Then bleed it crosswise.
And if you cut it completely, this will not harm it in any way.
And take care not to extend your hand too far with the lancet.
For beneath it are the nerves of the fingers, stripped of flesh.
Then return the hand to warm water, and let the blood flow into it.
For if you do not return it to warm water, the blood coagulates in the opening of the vein and is prevented from flowing.
When therefore you have drawn the necessary amount of blood, then put oil and salt on the vein: so that it does not heal too quickly.
And similarly you should do this with every constricted branch.
The benefit of bleeding it from the left hand is: that it is beneficial for diseases of the spleen: and similarly do in bleeding it as you did in the second case equally.
The benefit of bleeding the Saphenous vein is for diseases which are in the lower part of the body, such as diseases of the womb, and retention of menstruation, and infirmities of the kidneys, and old ulcers of the hips and legs, and similar diseases.
And the method of bleeding it is that the patient should put their foot in warm water: and rub it until the veins become prominent: and bind above the foot joint with a strap.
And indeed the location of the vein is at the heel, appearing toward the big toe: and many branches spread from it on the surface of the foot.
Therefore bleed it in its larger branch, or at the heel where it collects: since this is better and safer.
But if you bleed it on the surface of the foot, then beware of the nerves which are beneath it on the surface of the foot.
And make your incision with which you bleed it crosswise, as if you wish to cut it: and let the lancet be Nesil.
If however the blood flow is blocked, then return their foot to warm water: and let the blood flow into it until it is evacuated.
But if the phlebotomist misses the vein on the first attempt, then let him repeat with the phlebotomy slightly higher up, for the location is safe, and you need not fear harm from it, when you beware of the nerves, as we said.
And similarly with the Saphenous vein of the other foot equally.
The location of the Sciatic vein, as we said, is at the heel on the sylvan (outer) side.
And the benefit of bleeding it is for pain of the hip, when it is due to hot blood.
And the method of bleeding it is that the patient should enter the bath, and hurry: and bind their leg from the side of the hip to above the heel four fingers' width, with a long thin band: for it does not appear except because of that.
When it appears, then perform bloodletting on it by whatever method is possible for you, either crosswise (which is better), or by making an incision, or by cutting it with a section.
Indeed, its location is safer, and in most people it is very well hidden.
But if you cannot find it, and it is not at all apparent to the senses, then perform bloodletting on one of its branches, namely the one that appears on the top of the foot and toward the little toe: and beware of the nerves, and let flow the amount of blood that you wish.
Then loosen the binding, and place leather over the site of bloodletting, and bind the area.
For it heals quickly.
¶ On Cupping, and the nature of its use.
Chapter 98.
Cups are made from horns, and from bronze, and from glass.
And indeed cupping is done in two ways.
With scarification and blood extraction: and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping which is without scarification is done in two ways.
For either it is with fire.
Or it is without fire.
And for cups which are administered with scarification and blood extraction there are fourteen places on the body.
Of which one is the Cupping of Alnocrati, which is the back of the head: and Alkehel, which is the middle of the neck: and the cups of Alhagdahim, which are the two plates of the neck on both sides together: and the cups of the chin, which is under the jaw in the lower part of the mouth: and the cups of the two shoulder blades: and the cups of Alachosos above the end of the tail: and the cups of the two forearms, which are the middle of the two arms: and the cups of the two legs: and the cups of the two ankles.
And indeed cupping does not extract blood for you from the tiny veins scattered in the flesh, and because of that strength does not fall from them, as it falls from bloodletting.
