Nor should cupping be administered, whether with fire or without fire, in any of the illnesses that occur from repletion, until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity calls for cupping due to illness, or because of custom, we administer it at any hour at the beginning of the month, at its end, and in its middle, and at whatever time it may be.
This is because among people there is one who, when blood multiplies in him until he needs its extraction through cupping, finds heaviness in his head, and headache.
And among them is one who finds fullness and redness in his face, his head, and his neck.
And among them is one who finds itching in his face and his forehead, and darkness and dullness in his eyes.
And among them is one who scratches the place of his cupping.
And among them is one whose laughter multiplies.
And among them is one who finds the taste of blood in his mouth: or his tongue swells, and he spits blood.
And among them is one whose sleep multiplies.
And among them is one who sees in his sleep blood and redness, and the slain, and wounds, and things similar to those.
Therefore when we see any of these things, and especially if it is in the middle third of the month: we then prescribe cupping to be done, after two or three hours of the day have passed.
The benefit of cupping of the nape is: that it helps with heaviness in the head, and what flows to the eyes.
But indeed it is necessary that this be after the evacuation of the whole body.
And this cup indeed is in place of bloodletting of the Cephalic vein.
And it is not permitted that one who is of cold complexion use it, or one in whom there is catarrh.
For it brings great harm to him.
And because of that it is not proper that old people use it, and those in whose head are cold illnesses.
Moreover, whoever becomes assiduous in its use, forgetfulness is generated in him.
And because of that it is necessary that you instruct the cupper to move his hand with the cup a little downward, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
But cupping of Alkehel is in place of bloodletting of the Fusca and bloodletting of the Basilic vein, and because of that it helps with asthma, and constriction of breathing, and fissure of the breathing apparatus, and cough, and repletion.
And it is necessary that the Alkehel Cup be pushed a little, because if it is placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
Cupping of the alhagdahim (scalp) benefits pains occurring in the head, eye inflammation, migraine, perforation, and pain in the roots of teeth: and it serves in place of bloodletting from the basilic vein.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper not to press his hand during scarification: so that he does not cut an artery, and bleeding occurs.
Cupping under the chin benefits ulcers in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar diseases which are in the mouth: and it serves in place of bloodletting of the alieherich, which are in the two lips.
Cupping of the shoulder blades benefits alchafacham (palpitations), which occurs from repletion and heat.
Cupping of the inner parts of the two forearms provides the benefit of bloodletting from three veins - the Basilic, the Dark, and the Cephalic: because it draws blood from all those minute veins which are in the flesh: and those minute veins draw from other thicker veins, until the drawing reaches the three veins.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper not to proceed too far in scarification: because the place is devoid of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
The cupping which is performed over the alchausos (coccyx) benefits hemorrhoids of the Anus, and lower ulcers.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper that the cup should be large, and that it should be made of bronze: because the place needs strong suction, and perhaps delicate cups might break: and it should be scarified with large scarification.
Cupping of the legs diminishes with manifest diminution: because they draw blood from the whole body, and benefit old pains in the kidneys, and wombs, and bladder: they provoke menstruation, and benefit bothor (pustules), and ademenul: and they serve in place of bloodletting of the two saphenous and alhorcobeni veins.
However, they greatly weaken the body, and cause fainting in many people.
And the benefit of cupping the alhorcobeni (sciatic region) is close to the benefit of cupping the two legs.
The method of placing cups is that you first place an empty cup, and suck with moderate suction: and do not prolong the placement of cups, but place them quickly, and remove them quickly, so that the humors come to the place with equal arrival: and do not cease to repeat and continue this, until you see the place has already reddened and swollen, and the redness of blood appears: then therefore scarify, and repeat with gradual suction.
Then consider the disposition of bodies.
For he among men who is of tender flesh, with rare pores, it is necessary that you scarify him once, no more: so that the place does not become ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to widen the scarification, and deepen it a little: and to moderate the suction in ease, and speed, and gentle movement.
If there is thickness in the blood, then it is necessary that you scarify it twice.
In the first time indeed the way is opened for the thinning of blood, and its wateriness: and in the second indeed for the exquisite investigation of extracting thick blood.
If truly the blood is very turbid, then let scarification be done a third time: so that the ultimate is achieved.
And finally, when we wish to extract blood gradually, or little by little, one scarification suffices for us.
If we wish to extract much blood, we scarify with multiple scarifications.
And if we measure that the blood is thick: then it is necessary that it be scarified with deep scarification: and the equal limit in scarification is the depth of the skin.
What are the oils that should be administered when applying cups, and what should the person being cupped avoid.
For one whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are tight, the areas to be cupped should be anointed with opening, softening, and dissolving oils: if it is in summer time, such as alkanna oil, or violet oil, or sweet almond oil, or pumpkin seed oil.
And if it is in winter time, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or sarbatinum oil: and similar ones to these.
If indeed the excess is thick and cold: then let the oil be marjoram oil, or balsamita oil, or alben oil, or dill oil: and similar ones to these.
If indeed the person to be cupped has wide pores and tender flesh: then it is necessary that they be kept from oil, and it is necessary that these people wash the cupped areas after cupping with rose water, or with cold water, or with nightshade water, or with pumpkin water, or with purslane water: and similar ones to these.
He whose blood has much moisture should wash the cups with vinegar, or with myrtle water, and sumac: and similar ones to these.
One whose excesses are thick should wash their cups with aged wine, or with marjoram water, or a decoction of dill, or chamomile: and similar ones to these.
And cupping should be avoided in the bath, or immediately after bathing, rather it should be administered after leaving the bath for an hour, or for two hours.
And one should not sleep after cupping.
What is it by which the person to be cupped or bled should be governed before cupping.
AND after it, you should first consider: then if the person cupped or bled is choleric, and sharpness and inflammation dominate their blood: then they should take cooling things, like sweet pomegranate: and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and sekanjabin, and julep: and similar ones to these.
And make their foods chicken, and sekebiget sheep meats, and foods seasoned with verjuice: and similar ones to these.
And one whose complexion is cold should drink honey syrup, or almiua, or sekanjabin made from seeds: and should take fragrant wine of medium age, which is between old and new: and should be instructed to eat sparingly.
And make their food chicken, and canebir: and sparrows, that is, small birds: and young pigeons prepared issiadebet.
And the drink on the day of cupping and bleeding should be more than the food: and it is necessary that some people be given to drink at times theriac alfaroch, or musk medicine, that is, muscata, or alesselithe before cupping: and before or after bleeding: so that the principal members are strengthened and the blood is made thinner.
And they should not drink it when heated.
Concerning Cups without scarification.
Cups which are without scarification are cups which are placed over the liver, and spleen, and gout, and over the belly, navel, and areas of the kidneys, and hip socket: since these members do not tolerate scarification upon them.
And nothing is sought through them, except either the drawing of blood from one member to another: just as we place a cup over the breasts in illness of nosebleed.
And we use them to release cold windiness stuck in a member: just as we place a cup over the belly and navel.
For it rarefies the member, and warms it, and removes pain because it dissolves that windiness.
It is also placed over the kidneys, when obstruction or stone occurs in them.
Indeed by its power of attraction it perhaps opens the obstruction, or uproots the stone from its place.
And similarly it does when placed over the liver and spleen for grinding down windiness in both of them.
And these cups are administered empty with cutting only.
And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of tepid water in illnesses of pleurisy: and that is, that the cup should be filled, and should be large, with hot water alone, or water in which suitable herbs have been cooked.
Shape of a Cupping Glass administered with fire.
Let the width of its opening be two spread fingers, according to what we have shaped: and let its depth measure half a palm: and let there be on its side, around its middle, a small hole, of such size that a needle may enter it: let it be made entirely of brass, or yellow copper, with thick, smooth, even, polished edges: so that it does not harm the limb when placed upon it: and let there be in its middle a crossbar of brass, or iron, where a candle is placed with fire.
And this cupping glass is made large, larger than the one we described, and a little smaller than that one, according to the illnesses and age of the one who uses it.
Indeed, the cupping glasses for children and thin people are different from the cupping glasses for men and people of thick bodies.
The method of placing this cupping glass with fire on a limb is that you light a wick with fire made properly from linen, or a small wax candle: and place it upon the middle of the crossbar which is in the middle of the cupping glass: so that the lit fire is around the lower part of the cupping glass, so that the patient's body is not burned.
Then let it be placed on the limb: and let a finger be on the hole which I mentioned, until when we hold the cupping glass: when we need to, we remove the finger, and vapor will escape through that hole, and the cupping glass will be released immediately.
Then light the wick in its usual way, and repeat it, if you need it.
However, in the cupping glass which is administered in the illness of pleurisy with water, there is no crossbar, nor hole: and it is administered only because it is filled with water, and placed on the limb only.
And this is its shape.
And this cupping glass is better the larger it is, so that it may hold much water.
On the Application of Leeches.
Chapter 99.
Leeches are not administered in most cases, except on those limbs where the placement of cupping glasses is not possible, due to their smallness, such as the lip, and gum, and similar parts.
Or on a limb stripped of flesh, such as a finger, and nose, and similar parts.
And the method of using them is: that you obtain leeches which are from sweet waters, clean from putrefaction.
Then let them be left for a day and night in sweet water, until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their bellies.
Then evacuate the body first with bloodletting, and with cupping glasses.
Then clean the patient's limb, until it reddens.
Afterwards let them be placed upon it.
When therefore they are filled, and fall off, and it is possible to suction the area with a cupping glass, then this is most ultimate in benefit: and if not, then wash the area with vinegar.
Then with much water, and let it be rubbed, and squeezed.
But if the flow of blood continues after the falling of the leeches, and it is oozing: then soak a linen cloth in cold water, and place it on top, until the oozing is stopped.
If the blood multiplies, then sprinkle ground vitriol upon it, or gallnuts, and similar astringents: until the blood is stopped.
Or let halves of peeled beans be placed upon the area: and let them be left until the beans adhere to the area, for the blood will be stopped.
And it is necessary if the repetition of leeches is needed, when it is possible, to change them.
If however the leeches are prevented from attaching, then clean the area with fresh blood.
Or pierce the areas with a needle, until some blood comes out.
Then let them be placed, for when they sense some blood, they attach immediately.
When therefore you want them to fall off, sprinkle some ground aloe upon them, or salt, or ash: and they will fall off immediately.
End of the Second Part.
¶ Book III of Surgery by Albucasis.
¶ General discourse on restoration, dislocation, treatment of twisting, and similar matters.
Chapter I.
This Chapter is also frequently necessary in the art of Medicine, and it is about the restoration of fractures and dislocations occurring in bones.
Know, O Sons, that the foolish among physicians and common people boast about this Chapter, those who have never examined a book of the ancients, nor read a letter from it.
And for this reason it happens that this branch of knowledge in our land is lacking.
And I never encountered anyone good at it at all, and I did not attain what I have attained in it except through lengthy reading of ancient books and studying their meaning, until I extracted this knowledge from them.
Then I devoted myself to experimentation and study throughout my life.
And I have already described to you in this Chapter everything that my knowledge contains: and what my experience has covered, after I abbreviated it for you, and freed it from lengthy digressions, and abbreviated it to its final form, and showed it without deviation: and I have drawn in it many illustrations of instruments which are used in it, with some additional variations, as I did in the two preceding Chapters.
¶ Summaries and collections about the nature of bone fractures, among those things whose introduction is necessary before we begin the discussion of fractured and various members, each individually.
Chapter II.
It is necessary that we state in the prologue of this Chapter the main points of discussion and chapters, whose understanding is first required for the practitioner to stand upon their truth: namely so that you, and those who are ready, may know or learn this noble art, and others from you.
Therefore, I say that when someone suffers a fracture, or dislocation, or twisting, or a fall: then it is necessary that you hurry first to bleed him, and purge him, or both together: if nothing prohibits it, such as weakness of constitution: or if the one to whom something of these happened is a child, or a very old man: or if it is a time of intense heat, or intense cold.
Then let him be content in his diet with cold vegetables, and the meat of birds, and young goats: and forbid wine, and heavy meats, and being filled with foods and any food that fills the veins with blood.
Until when you are secure from hot swelling, and do not fear the flowing of matter to the limb: then let the patient return to his first regimen with which his custom proceeded.
When therefore the broken bone begins to heal, then it is necessary that the patient be fed foods that nourish with much, thick, heavy nourishment, in which there is viscosity: such as foods made from wheat cooked with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and cow stomachs, and eggs, and fresh fish: and thick wine, and similar things to these.
With this regimen indeed, the healing of the fracture happens more quickly and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in early old age, and the decrepit, it is not possible that they join and consolidate according to their nature always, because of the dryness of their members: and those of them that are extremely soft consolidate and join, like the bones of small children.
Nevertheless, nature attaches over the broken bone from all sides something like glue, in which there is thickness, by which it adheres and binds it, until they are glued together, and bound together, until it comes to complete strength and firmness, as it was at first, until nothing prevents it from its functions: and for this reason it is necessary that you make the patient's nourishment foods in which there is heaviness, and viscosity, and thickness, as we said.
And know that there are different types of fractures, according to the diversity of limbs: for a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the skull bone; and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the spine bone; and similarly all other limbs are different from each other: and I have come with the remembrance of every type of fracture explained in its own Chapter, distinct from others.
And the types of bone fractures also differ in themselves.
For there occurs an arched fracture, without splinters occurring in it.
And sometimes a fracture occurs along the length of the bone: and there are splintered fractures, that is, with splinters, and protruding additions, like those in a reed when it is prepared with a knife for writing.
And non-protruding ones: and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head: and there is a fracture that is a small fissure.
And for every type there is a technique, or proper instrument for its restoration, according to what will come in its remembrance, distinguished in their places.
And among the ways by which a bone fracture is recognized, is its crookedness, and its prominence: and its appearance to the senses, and its sound when you compress it with your hand.
Therefore when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor sound upon your touch when you touch the bone with movement, nor does the patient find great pain: then there is no fracture there, rather it is possible that there might be a twist, or a small fissure: therefore you should not move it with hand and compression at all, but rather place upon it from the medicines, whose remembrance comes, after that moment which suits the place: then let it be bound with a subtle binding.
And know that when a place is bent, and split in two, without splinters occurring in it, but rather each part is removed from its companion: then you must begin at your hour to rectify it, straightening it before a hot swelling occurs.
But if a hot swelling occurs to it, then leave it for some days, until the swelling subsides.
Then straighten it according to whatever method is possible with gentleness and skill.
And know that its restoration, and its straightening, is easier than that of a bone in which splinters occur.
Then bind it, according to what will come to you in its remembrance.
But if there are splinters in the bone, then extension of this fractured limb from both parts is necessary, if it is a hand, or foot: or with your hand, if the limb is small: or with two ropes, or with a rope and hand.
And let your position in which you place the limb be on an even place, according to its natural shape, until when the parts of the fractured limb are extended: then strive to make those additions return by every skill, and according to whatever method you can to their places: and take care according to your strength, lest pain or injury occur to the patient through your operation: and strive according to your strength, to join one of the two bones with its companion, according to the better forms.
And it is necessary in that hour, that you test and touch it with your hand.
If therefore you see anything irregular there, rectify it, and straighten it according to the measure of your ability.
And beware of violent extension, and strong compression, as many of the foolish do.
For many times they cause through that operation of theirs a hot swelling, or contraction in the limb: as we have already seen that from their operations many times.
Then attach after the straightening, and decent operation, and binding of that limb, and rest, and tranquility: and advise the patient about it.
And make him beware, lest he move it in the hour of his waking, and his sleep during his turning, and his movement, and during his evacuation, and all his movements to the utmost of his ability.
And that he should beware, lest the position of the limb be a position with which pain follows.
Which is, because when he feels pain or injury in the condition of the limb's position: then it is necessary that you change it to another condition from that with which he does not feel pain.
And beware with that, that its position be even, straight: so that no crookedness occurs in the limb, when it is restored.
And the method of binding a broken limb is as I will describe.
Know that broken limbs differ in their smallness and their size, and in their shape.
Those among them that are small, like the arm, finger, and forearm bone, and similar ones, then it is necessary that the wrapping be of soft, thin cloth.
But those among them that are thick, like the thigh and leg, it is necessary that they have a wide, firm wrapping.
Since a wide bandage connects a large limb and binds it from all sides with even pressure, so that no mark enters it.
Then begin after your cleaning by evening out and placing a suitable dressing for that purpose, with soft tow over the fracture site itself.
Then begin to wrap the bandage over the fracture site with three or four turns, according to what the limb requires: and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
Then go with it to the upper part from the fracture site, and tighten less than was your tightening with which you bound the broken site.
Then distance yourself with the wrapping from the fracture site a little, and loosen the binding gradually, until you take up a large portion of healthy area.
Then take another band, and wrap it again over the broken site with turns.
Afterward go with the wrapping to the lower part from the fracture.
And let your procedure in tightening the wrapping, and loosening it, be according to what we said in the first upper wrapping.
Then place between the wrappings some soft tow, or cloth, with which to even out any crookedness of the fracture, if there is crookedness in it: and if not, do not place anything in it.
Then wrap over it another band.
Afterward align over these wrappings splints properly made immediately, if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the area.
But if there is swelling or inflammation in it: then place over it what calms that inflammation and removes the swelling: and leave it for several days.
Then tighten the splints over it at that time.
And let the splints be made from halves of wide cane, scraped, wisely shaped: or let them be from wood from which sieves are made, which are made from pine, or geraid, that is, palm branches, or calingi: and let the width of each splint be according to this form and this same figure.
However, it is necessary that the splint which is placed over the break itself be somewhat thicker and wider than the other splints: and let the length of the splint be according to what is appropriate for the limb in terms of size and smallness.
Then tighten over the splints with another band, according to that same first binding.
Then bind over it with threads properly, according to what we said about binding: that is, that your binding should be tighter over the fracture site: and the further it extends from the fracture, let the binding be looser.
And it is necessary that the threads be medium in thickness and thinness, and be of soft linen.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen in the work of fools who put their threads from twisted linen cords, it is a great error: since the binding with them falls outside proper measure.
And thin threads again are not suitable: since you will not achieve through binding with them what you want.
And there should not be less than a finger's width between splint and splint.
But if the patient is harmed by the ends of the splints after binding in healthy areas: then place under them some soft tow, or carded wool, until nothing is harmed from that.
When however the fracture is with a wound, and disruption occurs in the skin, I will come with mention of it in a chapter according to its particularity.
And know that not every broken limb should be bound with splints from the first day.
Which is because when a limb is large, you should not place splints over it except after five days, or seven, or more, according to your confidence about the occurrence of hot inflammation.
And this is the method of plasters, with which the ancients restored [bones], which are placed over fracture and dislocation.
Method of a common abbreviated plaster, restoring fractures, suitable for many constitutions, especially women and children: since it is one in which neither heat nor cold predominates.
And it is that you should take dust from the mill, which is the flour meal that hangs on the mill walls near the movement of the millstone: and prepare it as is, except that you should sieve it with egg white: and make the preparation neither thick nor thin.
Then use it.
Method of another plaster, restoring fracture, dislocation, and sprain.
Take almess, laudanum, acacia, elecampane, and mugwort: and suc, 10 drams each of all; of myrrh and aloe 5 drams each; and 20 drams of alethil; let all be ground and sieved, and mixed with alethil water: or with egg white, if the patient's constitution is heated.
Then use this plaster.
For it is of good composition, restores broken bones quickly, and helps many people.
Method of another plaster, which also helps fracture and sprain.
Take mugwort, and mess, and white marsh mallow, 10 drams each of all; of myrrh and aloe 5 drams each; 6 drams of acacia; 20 drams of Armenian clay; let all be ground with much grinding, and sieved, and prepared with water, or egg white: and administered.
Method of a plaster for joints and bones separated from their places: and it calms the pain occurring in them, and bone splitting, and fracture.
Let unwashed wool be taken, and submerged in vinegar and cooked oil: and placed on the spot.
And in this plaster indeed there is no restorative power, but it is good in calming hot swelling: and especially drives away pains.
Method of a plaster which restores broken bone.
Take leaves of deaf fig, and leaves of wild poppy: and grind all, and make a plaster from both.
Method of an abbreviated plaster, administered for the restoration of bone fracture: and when you want resolution of residual swelling.
Take of marshmallow root, and chamomile, and violet flowers, and vetch flour, one part each of all: let all be ground, and prepared with wine, if the limb is not heated.
But if it is heated, then prepare it with water of fresh coriander, or with water, and use it.
Method of another plaster stronger in resolution than this first one, when hard swelling occurs during limb restoration.
Take of marshmallow root, and linseed, and pomegranate flower, and melilot, and marjoram, and violet flower, and chamomile, one part each of all, let all be ground, and prepared with willow water, or with sweet water, or with wine.
All that according to the heat of the limb, and the quieting of its heat.
Hippocrates indeed did not say in his book that anything should be placed on a broken limb during its restoration, except a wax plaster made from wax and oil: nothing else: and he related that it should be medium between thickness and thinness.
But it seemed to Galen that things should be placed on a broken limb during its restoration in which there is drying of heat, such as myrrh, and aloe and frankincense, and similar things to them from those which we have related.
Method of a plaster which helps injury and pain.
Take equal parts of mugwort, and chickpea, and cut human hair, or bird feather, and marsh mallow, and salt: let them be ground, and sieved, and prepared: and let a plaster be made from it.
The duration for which it is proper that the bandage should remain, before it is loosened, is that you should consider then, if no pain occurs to the patient, nor itching, nor does the broken bone move from its place: then do not loosen it for many days.
If there occurs in the place intense itching, or disturbing pain, or swelling: then hurry, and loosen it immediately, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or soft, moist sea sponge: and submerge it in lukewarm water, and wash the area with it until the itching subsides and the pain is relieved.
Then let the limb rest for one hour.
Then place upon it unwashed wool, soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil: and bind it over it for one night, until you are safe from hot swelling, and the swelling of the limb subsides, and its pain is removed.
Then repeat it with a gentle binding, and a small plaster: and do not bind it as tightly as your first binding, and proceed gently with it until it heals.
But if you see that the swelling, and redness, and pain, and inflammation, and all symptoms have now completely subsided, and you need a plaster and binding, then repeat it just as you did at first, equally.
But if none of those things we mentioned happens to the limb, then do not loosen it except after three days, or four, or five, or seven.
And sometimes all of this is left for twenty days, according to what appears to you regarding the loosening of the limb, as we said, until when the fracture adheres and the flesh begins to coagulate over it: even then increase the binding more than your first entire binding: and also increase the thickness of the patient's food, according to what was previously mentioned.
However, if you see the fracture site becoming dry and thinner than it should be: then know that nourishment is being prevented from reaching it.
Therefore moisten it with lukewarm water whenever you loosen it, and let this be every three days, and slightly loosen the binding.
Through this procedure, therefore, nourishment flows to the limb, and it heals quickly.
That which foolish bone-setters do with a fractured limb a second time, when it is not set properly at first, and heals crookedly, is an error in their procedure, and a great danger: and if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and would have practiced it.
And I have not seen any trace of them doing this at all, and it is more correct that it not be done.
¶ Concerning fracture occurring in the head.
Chapter III.
The types of fracture are diverse, and its forms many: and its causes are distinct.
For among fractures there is that which occurs from a blow with a sword: and the blow removes the entire bone until it reaches the membrane which is under the bone, just as an ax does in wood: and because of that this type of fracture is called Axial.
Or the sword cuts part of the bone, and cuts its surface, and the cut does not penetrate to its depths: and this type of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, Elevation absolutely.
And the wound of these two fractures is either large or small.
And among fractures there is that which is crushing or grinding: and its cause is being struck with a stone, or falling onto a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which has already approached the membrane that is under the bone: or it is on the surface of the bone: and the wound of these two fractures is also either small or large.
And among fractures there is that which is hidden in the bone with the fineness of a hair: and it is a small fissure, and because of that this type of fracture is called Capillary.
And among them is a fracture which occurs from a fall, or strike of a stone, and similar things, which cause the layer of bone to go inward, and a hollow forms in the place: just as happens to brass cauldrons when they receive a blow, and part of them goes inward.
And indeed this happens most often in the heads with moist bones, such as the heads of children.
And separate treatments are made for all these types of fractures, and I will come with the mention of all of that in its place.
And we recognize all these types of fractures through exposing them, and examining them with probes, and removing corrupt flesh that is over them.
The capillary-type (fracture) is recognized by this: that it is exposed on the bone, and when it is cleaned and painted over with ink, the fracture appears black.
In treating a fracture, first consider the patient's symptoms: then if you see from the symptoms something that clearly indicates danger, such as bilious vomiting, tenseness, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, acute fever, protruding eyes, their redness, and similar symptoms in most cases, it certainly cannot be cured: but if you see symptoms that do not frighten you, and you hope for recovery: then begin its treatment.
That is, if an injured person comes to you immediately after being wounded: and if it is in winter days, then you must endeavor to remove the bone before the fourteenth day under all circumstances.
And if it is in summer days, then you must hurry to remove the bone before the seventh day, before what is under the bone from the membrane becomes corrupted, and those symptoms we described occur.
If the bone fracture has already reached the membrane touching the brain, and is with fragments and contusion: then you must cut away the comminuted and crushed part, according to what I will tell you.
And it is that you should shave the wounded person's head: and expose the bone in whatever way possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound, and what is manageable for the patient.
If during the exposure of the bone there occurs bleeding or hot swelling: then treat it with what is necessary.
That is, fill the area with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil: and let the wound rest until the swelling subsides, and you are safe from bleeding.
Then begin the treatment of the bone and its removal.
And this is done according to one of two methods.
One method is that you cut the bone with a narrow-edged Incisor: and this is its form.
Then use another Incisor after the first one, slightly wider than it: and this is its form.
Then also use another Incisor even wider than the second.
And the second method is that you should have a number of different Incisors: some wider than others, and some shorter than others: and they should be extremely sharp at their ends, and be made of Indian iron or steel: and use gentleness in striking the Incisor, so as not to shake the head and harm it.
If however the bone is strong and hard: then you must drill around its circumference, before you use the Incisors, with drills called non-penetrating Trephines: and they are called thus only because they do not pass beyond the limit of the bony skull to what is behind it: because the Trephine has a round extremity above its sharp head, similar to a margin, and the small circle prevents it from sinking in and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is appropriate for you to take many of these Trephines, each one suitable for the thickness of the bone, until you have for every skull a Trephine: whose sharp extremity's quantity in length and shortness is according to the quantity of that skull's thickness.
And this is the form of three types of Trephines.
The method of drilling around the fractured bone is: that you place the Trephine on the bone, and rotate it with your fingers until you know the bone has been penetrated.
Then move the Trephine to another location, and place what is between each hole the width of a needle, or approximately that.
Then cut with the Incisors what is between each hole from the bone: and make it as thin as you can manage with utmost gentleness, as we said, until you extract the bone, either with your hand or with some other of the instruments which you prepared for that: such as forceps and fine scissors.
And you must take every precaution not to touch the dura mater with either the Trephine or the Incisor.
When you have gone around drilling the bone, and it has been separated from the membrane, if it was continuous with it, and you have extracted it, you should scrape and smooth the edge of what remains in the bone with another similar cutting instrument.
However, it should be thinner and more delicate than other cutting instruments.
And if any small bone fragments and splinters remain, then remove them carefully with whatever instruments you have at hand.
Then treat the wound afterward with lint and the ointments we have mentioned.
And there is another method of operation that is easy and completely free from fear, which Galen described and praised greatly: and these are his words.
First, you must begin by exposing the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and most visible: until when you have exposed that part, and placed under it the tip of this lenticular cutting instrument, which has this form.
And the lenticular part is smooth and does not cut anything: and its sharp part on its two sides runs lengthwise, as you see: so that the lenticular part rests against the membrane, with its sharp cutting face against the bone.
Then strike the cutting instrument from one side with a small mallet, until you cut through the entire bone easily, as it turns: and be assured regarding the membrane, that no concerning accident will happen to it at all.
And if the physician were not the most foolish and ignorant of men, and were not asleep, then if anything of the membrane remained attached to the bone in certain places, he would cut it away from it with the tip of the same lenticular instrument, and free it from it easily.
For it is freed from it without harm and without fear.
And if the bone fracture does not penetrate to the membrane, and what is cut away from the bone is its surface, and roughness and fine splinters remain in it: then you should scrape away that roughness and remove those splinters with fine rasps, of which you have prepared a number of different sizes, so that you can use from among them in any location the most suitable and most appropriate ones, according to what the operation leads you to, and the fractured bone itself, and its shape.
And in your scraping, when you first scrape the bone, you should use the widest of those rasps.
Then after that use one finer than it.
And do not cease doing this continuously until you proceed to using the thinnest and finest of them all.
In each of the remaining small capillary fissures and fine fractures, you should administer treatment according to what leads to its correction.
And this is something that is not hidden from anyone who has any study in this art and has understood what we have written and shown regarding major fractures.
If however the exposed membrane is retained when you remove the bone: then you should take a linen cloth according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound.
Then take another cloth folded double or triple, and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth: and do this as gently as you can over it, so that the membrane is not weighed down.
Then apply a wide bandage over it: and do not tighten it except by an amount that just holds the wound: and leave it with this treatment for one day or two days, until you are assured against hot inflammation.
Then loosen it, and afterward apply medicines which have excessive dryness: such as lily roots, and vetch flour, and frankincense powder, and birthwort, and similar things: and it is that you should make from these medicines a powder, which is to be sprinkled over the wound as they are dry.
And generally apply to it any medicine whose property is to cleanse, and not to irritate.
And take care according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment be clean: and let there be in it neither ointment residue, nor filth.
And do not let pus accumulate in it at all: since when pus accumulates on the membrane of the brain, it corrupts it, and putrefies it, and afterward great harm befalls the patient: and it has already occurred in the membrane of the head, when the bone is cleaned from it, especially when its care is neglected, that blackness appears on its surface.
Therefore consider then, if the symptoms we mentioned occur in the patient, know that it is undoubtedly dangerous.
And if the blackness occurs only from the medicine which is placed upon it, and it is in the power of that medicine to cause that blackness: then it is necessary that you take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and spread them on a cloth.
Then place it on the membrane: afterward treat it with the types of treatment that are suitable, until it heals.
¶ On the restoration of the Nose when it is broken.
Chapter 4.
Know that only the two upper parts of the Nose break together, or one of them: because they are two bones: since its lower part is cartilaginous, it does not break, and nothing happens to it except bruising, and twisting, and flattening.
If one of its two parts breaks, then it is necessary that you insert the little finger into the nostril, and straighten that fracture with your index finger and thumb from the outside, until the Nose returns to its natural shape: and let that be done skillfully, with gentleness.
And take care that pain does not occur to the patient through that operation of yours.
But if the fracture is in the upper part of the Nose, and the finger cannot reach it: then it is necessary that it be straightened with the end of a probe, in which there should be a small thickness.
If indeed the fracture is in both parts, then do similarly to that: and hasten with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if it is possible.
And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day when the hot swelling has subsided.
Then insert into the end of the nose a plug of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part: or insert two plugs if the fracture is in two parts: and let there be in the plug sufficient thickness to fill the nostril.
And some of the ancient healers said to soak the plugs in butter, and change them every day.
That indeed does not seem right to me, rather it is necessary that the plug be soaked in egg white, mixed with mill dust.
Then leave the plug until the bone becomes firm and the cartilage hardens.
And sometimes a goose quill is inserted into the Nose instead of the plug, after it is wrapped with soft cloth: and it happens that it holds the fracture of the Nose more firmly, and so that the patient is not prevented from breathing.
And this thing is not necessary.
If you wish, do that: and if you wish, make plugs.
But if while you are working a hot swelling happens to the Nose, plaster the nose with wax ointment, or with cotton soaked in rose oil, or a little diachylon.
If however no hot swelling occurs, then it is necessary that you plaster it with sesame flour and powdered frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then place soft tow upon it, and do not bind the Nose with anything at all.
If indeed the bones of the Nose break with a small fracture, or are shattered, then it is necessary that you cut over them, and extract them with instruments that are suitable for them.
Then sew up the incision, and treat it with what causes flesh to grow and consolidates from suitable plasters or ointments for that.
But if a wound occurs between the Nose, then it is necessary that you treat it with plugs: and use a lead tube, until it heals.
On the restoration of the lower jaw, when it is broken.
Chapter 5.
When the lower jaw is broken, and its fracture is not accompanied by a wound: observe then if the fracture is only external, and is not broken in two, and curves inward: then its diagnosis is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, you should insert the index finger of your left hand into the patient's mouth: similarly if the fracture is in the left jaw, then insert the index finger of your right hand: and push with it the protrusion of the fracture from inside gently outward with your other hand from the inner part of the bone properly, and with it align it.
If the fracture of the jaw is already separated in two, then you must apply traction from both parts, according to straightness: until its alignment becomes possible.
If moreover there has already occurred loosening or disruption in the teeth: then bind those which you hope will remain with gold wire, or silver, or silk.
Then place a wax plaster over the fractured jaw.
Then place over it a folded cloth, and place over the cloth a large splint properly made: or an even piece of sole leather, along the length of the jaw.
Then bind it from above, according to how its bandaging is prepared for you, and its application is suitable, until it does not come apart: and instruct the patient to maintain tranquility and rest: and make his food soft liquid meals.
If however you think that some part of its shape has already changed in some way: then hurry, and loosen it on the third day.
Then rectify what has been altered from it, and plaster it with mill dust mixed with egg white, or with sesame flour, after you have removed the wax plaster: and place soft wool over the plaster.
While therefore that plaster remains attached over it, and the position of the bone is not altered, leave it, do not loosen it, until it heals: or tighten the fracture.
Indeed this fracture often heals in three weeks.
But if meanwhile a hot swelling occurs, then administer what we have mentioned many times for its sedation, until that swelling is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, examine it.
For if a fragment has already separated from the bone, or from splintered pieces: then be subtle in this, that you remove those splintered pieces with whatever instruments are suitable for their removal.
If indeed the opening of the wound is narrow, then widen it with a lancet according to your need.
Then when you have removed those splinters, and none of them remain, suture the opening of the wound, if it is wide: and if not, place upon it plasters which are suitable for that, and help it heal, until it heals.
¶ On the restoration of the Collarbone, when it is broken.
Chapter VI.
The collarbone is most often broken at the anterior part near the shoulder, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
Either it breaks and separates in two, without any splinters occurring in it: and this is easier to restore.
Or such that splinters occur in the bone: and this is more difficult to restore.
Or such that the fracture comes with a wound.
And the procedure for it when the fracture is without a wound is that you should have two assistants present, so that one of them holds the arm that follows the broken collarbone, and the other extends the neck to the side.
Then align the fracture with your fingers, until its form becomes as it should be: and there should be neither protrusion nor cavity in it.
If you need more extension, then you should place under the patient's armpit a ball of cloth or wool: and its size should be according to your need: and extend and elevate the collarbone, and compress the ball with your hand until you align the fracture as needed.
If you cannot pull the end of the collarbone outward because it has gone deep, then the patient should lie back on their neck: and place under their shoulder a medium-sized pillow, and have the assistant press their shoulder downward until the collarbone bone that is deep rises upward: and then straighten the fracture and align it with your fingers.
If you feel splintered pieces from the collarbone, and it moves: then you should cut into it and extract the splinters carefully.
If it is stuck in the bone, then devise to cut it with one of the cutting tools that you have prepared for this, after you have placed under the collarbone an instrument that protects the membrane: and it is an instrument of wood or iron.
And this is its form.
It resembles a spoon that has no cavity.
And its width should be according to what you need of it based on the size of the bone, whether large or small.
Its length also according to what is possible for you to operate with.
And it should have two ends, as you see: and one end should be wide, and the other narrow.
If the disruption of the wound that you cut when you extracted the bone splinters is wide, and you are safe from hot inflammation: then bring together the two lips of the wound with sutures.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear hot inflammation: then fill the wound with cloths and small pads according to the size of the wound's cut.
If hot inflammation occurs, then soak the cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and place it over it.
The binding of the bone when it is without wound and without cutting is that you should place over the bone a plaster made from mill flour with egg white.
And place soft tow over it.
Then place a ball under their armpit, if you need it.
Then take a himemah, that is, a bandage that the Saracens use, which should be very long: and its width should be one palm or thereabouts.
Then place a doubled pad over the tow and plaster.
Then take a splint from a thin board, whose width should be three fingers, and similarly in length.
Then wrap it in cloth.
Then wrap that cloth with the splint in the place of the bandage that falls over the fracture and area.
Then place the bandage over the fracture as it is wrapped, and wrap it over their neck, and under their healthy armpit, and under their injured armpit: and return it over every part, and however you see that the binding holds the fracture with proper tightness: and all this is not hidden from you, and everything, so that the splint does not move from over the broken bone.
And because of this you should examine the patient every day.
When therefore the bandage becomes loose, and you see the splint has moved: then straighten it, and tighten the bandage.
Then put the patient to sleep on his back, and place under his armpit during his sleep a small pillow at night, so that with it his upper arm is elevated from his side.
Therefore the fracture of the collarbone is elevated by raising the shoulder.
Either tie his arm to his neck: and do not loosen the bandage, unless there occurs in the area a symptom from itching, or from swelling, until twelve days have passed.
Then renew the plaster, if you see its surface requires it.
And increase the tightness, and leave it until it heals and solidifies.
And indeed the fracture of the collarbone becomes tight and strong typically in twenty-eight days: and sometimes it happens in some people in less than that.
¶ On the Treatment of Fracture of the Shoulder Blade.
Chapter VII.
The shoulder blade rarely breaks, and [when it does] in its wide part: and nothing breaks from it except its edges.
When therefore a place breaks from it, and it breaks in its middle, and this is not known except by touch: then according to the nature of the fracture, attempt its preparation.
And return it to its natural shape by every method of technique that is possible for you.
Then place on the area mill powder with egg white, and soft tow, and place above it a pad of folded cloth.
Then place over it a wide splint from a thin board according to the size of the entire shoulder blade, or slightly larger than it.
If indeed under the splint there is a hollow in any areas of the shoulder blade, then level that hollow with soft tow, until the splint descends evenly.
Then tighten over it with a long bandage, with decent constriction, and firmness of the splint, until it does not move from its place.
And check the bandage every day.
Whenever therefore the bandage becomes loose, tighten it, and adjust the splint if it moves from its place: and let the patient lie on his healthy side.
And indeed the shoulder blade heals in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When therefore this number is completed, then loosen the bandage: and you may be secure.
For it is one of the bones whose joining is not feared, nor their separation.
If however a fragment protrudes from the bone, and pricks under the skin: then cut over it, and remove it.
And do what we said in the fracture of the collarbone regarding the treatment of hot swelling, if anything of that sort occurs.
¶ On the repair of a fractured Chest.
Chapter VIII.
The chest sometimes breaks in its middle, and this rarely occurs.
Its extremities indeed most often break and are crushed from its accidents.
When its middle breaks, which slopes downward, and severe pain occurs, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing: and perhaps he vomits blood, and a hollow appears in the broken bone, and is not hidden to the senses, its Repair is that the patient should lie back upon his back: and place a pillow between his Shoulder blades.
Then press his shoulders, and bring together the ribs from both parts or sides with the hands: and be careful in aligning it, according to every way in which this is possible and appropriate, until the shape of the bone returns to what it should be.
Then place upon it a plaster and tow, and place above it a splint from a thin board of willow, or teak, and from similar things in lightness, after you wrap it in cloth.
Then be careful in binding it over the broken bone, so that it does not move: and go with the bandage according to the roundness to the back several times, and tighten it with decent tightness.
Then check the bandage every hour, and whenever it loosens, tighten it: and if necessity compels its loosening due to itching, or irritation which occurs in the place, or pain, or swelling: then hurry, and loosen it, and remove the plaster, and correct what has occurred with the method of its treatment.
Then replace the plaster, if you see fit for that: and attach the tightening to it, until it heals.
¶ On the repair of Ribs, when they break.
Chapter IX.
Know that Rib fracture does not occur, except in the thick places which follow the spine, and from their extremities in the anterior part.
And crushing does not occur to them, except because they are cartilaginous.
And knowledge of it is not hidden from the senses during examination with the fingers.
And its repair is done thus, that you align the fracture with the fingers according to the possible way, until the shape is aligned, according to what is necessary.
Then apply plasters, and tighten the broken bone with a splint, if it needs that.
And if the fracture of the Ribs is sloping towards the interior: then severe pain occurs to the patient, and stabbing, like that stabbing which occurs to one who has pleurisy: because the bone punctures the membrane, and also difficulty of breathing occurs to him, and coughing, and vomiting of much blood: and this is difficult to cure.
And indeed the ancients have contrived in it with many devices: and among them are those who say that you should give the patient foods which generate inflammation and windiness, so that the belly inflames and extends, and pushes the fracture to the exterior.
We however abhor that a quick cause should not be made for making a hot swelling, if it has not occurred.
But if it has already occurred, then it increases in it.
And some of them said: let a cupping glass be placed over the place, then let it be sucked with force, and it is more similar to reasoning.
However it is feared that the cupping glass might draw superfluities to the place, because of the disposition of weakness.
And some of them said: it is necessary that the place be covered with wool which has already been submerged in hot oil: and let cushions be placed between the Ribs, until it is filled: so that the binding might be equal when you wrap it according to the roundness.
Then let the patient be treated with the treatment of pleurisy from food and medicine.
But if a severe matter constrains the patient, which he does not tolerate, and the bone punctures the membrane with a harmful puncture, and we fear for the patient: then it is necessary that we cut over the place, and uncover from the broken Rib.
Then let us place over it the instrument which preserves the peritoneum, whose mention preceded: and let us cut the bone with ease, and let us cut it.
Then let us bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large with suture: and let us treat it with plasters, until it heals.
But if meanwhile a hot swelling occurs, then hurry, and pour cushions in rose oil, and place over the place: and let the patient be treated with what suits the swelling.
Meanwhile also let him lie back on the side on which he sleeps, until he heals.
¶ On the repair of fractures of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
Chapter 10.
Indeed, fracture sometimes occurs to the bones of the neck, and rarely does crushing happen to them: and similarly also to the vertebrae of the back.
Therefore when this happens to someone, and you wish to know whether it will heal or not heal: then observe.
For if you see their hands already softened, and numbed, and dead: and they cannot move them, nor extend them, nor grip them: and when you squeeze them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, they do not feel it, and do not feel pain in them: then know that it will not heal, and in most cases it is fatal.
But if they move them, and feel in them the squeezing with fingers and the pricking: then know that the marrow of the bone is safe, and that the patient will heal with treatment.
But if something similar happens to the vertebrae of the back, and you wish to know whether it will also heal or not: then look at their feet: then if you see that they are softened, and there occurs in them what occurs in the hands: then when they lie on their back, wind and excrement come out without control: and when they lie face down on their belly, excrement comes out without control: and when they lie on their back, and want to urinate, and cannot: then know that it is fatal.
Therefore do not labor in treating it.
But if none of that happens to them, the matter is lighter.
And the treatment of what happens from that is that you should attempt to calm the hot swelling: thus, that you place upon the crushed vertebra rose oil alone: or with the yolks of roasted egg.
Place this upon it several times in the day, until when the hot swelling subsides: then place upon the location one of the strengthening, drying plasters, and bind it with a bandage: and instruct the patient to maintain rest and tranquility.
And not to sleep on the part with which they find pain, until it heals.
But if there occurs with the crushing of the bone splintering, or something that has become separated from it: then it is necessary that you cut into the skin, and remove it.
Then join the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with sutures.
Then treat it with some flesh-forming substance, from the ointments, until it heals.
But if the end bone of the coccyx breaks, which is the end of the tail: then it is necessary that you insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and align the broken bone with the other hand, according to what is possible, and with secondary alignment.
Then place upon it, and remove it: and treat the wound with the treatment which preceded, until it heals.
¶ On the repair of the Hip.
Chapter 11.
The hip bones rarely break.
But if they break, then their fracture is nothing but fragmentation in their extremities: and they split lengthwise, and decline inward: and pain occurs to the patient in the location, and stabbing pain: and the leg which breaks becomes numb.
And indeed its repair is that you extend your hand over it, until you determine the nature of the fracture's shape.
But if the fracture is in its extremities only, then align that fracture, according to what is prepared for you from alignment, until it resembles its natural shape.
But if the fracture is lengthwise, or has already declined inward: then make the patient lie on their belly, until the repair of that fracture is prepared for you.
Therefore when you have aligned it, place a plaster upon it: then place upon it a splint of wood, or of leather: and bind with a binding over which you do not fear movement of the fracture, nor removal of the splint.
And align their concavity with that which fills it, until the binding takes it evenly: and instruct the patient to sleep on their back, or on their healthy side.
But if a hot swelling occurs to it: then cease from its extension and its repair, until the hot swelling subsides: and place upon it what settles it, according to what preceded, then return to its repair: and bind it as necessary.
But if splintering occurs in the bone, or it is fragmented, extract it.
On the Restoration of a Fracture of the Upper Arm.
Chapter 12.
The upper arm is that which is between the elbow up to the head of the shoulder blade.
If therefore it breaks, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
Of which one is: that you take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form which follows.
And let two bindings be tied at its two ends.
Then let it be suspended from an elevated place: and let the patient sit upon a seat.
Then let him cast his broken arm over the wood, until it reaches his armpit, in the middle of which let there be attached the curve of the wood.
Then suspend something heavy above it, so that the assistant may extend it downward.
Then let the physician align the fracture with both his hands, until the fracture returns to how it should be.
And the other method is that the patient lie back upon his neck: and suspend his hand from his neck with a binding.
Then instruct two assistants, that one of them hold what is above the fracture with his hands: and that the other hold below it, and let each of them extend toward his side.
And if you wish the extension to be stronger, then bind below the fracture with a binding, and above it with another binding: and let each of the two assistants extend them toward their two sides.
If therefore the fracture is near the extremity of the humerus, then it is necessary that one binding be placed under the armpit, and another under the fracture toward the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow: then it is necessary that a binding be placed above that place, and above the elbow itself.
Then align the fracture with ease without labor, until when the fracture is aligned as it should be, and is applied with good application: then bind it, if no hot swelling occurs.
But if a hot swelling occurs, then leave the binding until the seventh day.
And place upon it unwashed wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until when the swelling subsides: then bind it: and the method of binding it is that you place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster.
Then let him gather the forearm over the upper arm itself, and place his open hand over his shoulder.
And place cloth, and bind the forearm over the upper arm, so that the forearm stays in place of splints: if nothing prevents you from that, nor anything of the fractured bone shifts upon you.
But if you fear that something of the fractured bone might shift upon you, then use splints: and it is that you place over the fracture itself a splint which should be wider and stronger than the other splints.
And place between each splint the width of a finger: and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers on each side.
Then bind over the splints with the binding which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your binding over the place of fracture should be tighter.
And the further the fracture extends, let the binding be lesser.
If therefore you see the position of the splints and the binding, as we said, in the hour in which you restore the limb immediately, then do it: and if you fear a hot swelling, then leave the binding and splints until the seventh day, as we said.
Then examine the binding every three days, so that there does not occur in the place itching, or swelling, or food is prevented from reaching the limb, due to the condition of excessive binding.
Therefore rectify all that, according to what we said.
But if you are confident that nothing of that will occur: then do not loosen the binding, except after three days, or more.
And let the patient's sleeping be upon his back, and his hand upon his stomach: and place under the upper arm a cushion filled with wool evenly.
And observe in every hour of night, or day, that the position of the fractured limb does not loosen, or the binding become slack.
Therefore rectify all that with your skill.
And arrange the patient's food according to the order we prescribed, so that the food should be light at first, until the bone begins to bind: then it is necessary that you make his food more substantial.
For it is the custom of the upper arm and leg that they bind in forty days: then therefore it is necessary that it be loosened, and a bath be administered, and treated with ointments which restore it.
But if the fracture should be ugly, abraded: then do not loosen the bandage and splints from it for up to fifty days, and up to two months.
¶ On the restoration of a Fracture of the Arm.
Chapter XIII.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are named the two focuses.
Of which one is small: and it is that which follows the thumb.
And the other is large, and it is that which is positioned under the small one from the lower part.
Perhaps therefore the larger focus breaks alone, or the small one: and perhaps they break together.
When therefore the small upper focus breaks, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower focus breaks, its fracture is bad, and its healing difficult.
And worse than this is when the two bones break together.
If therefore the bone which breaks is the small upper focus, then it is appropriate for the physician during its restoration to apply a small extension with ease, until he aligns it.
But if the large focus is that which is broken: then it is necessary that he apply a very strong compression.
And it is necessary that he position the figure of the hand during its restoration, and its extension stretched over a pillow.
And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all fingers toward the top: and let the index finger be lower than all fingers, or the rest: and the patient sitting square upon himself, and let the pillow be before him, or directly in front of him, at a height so that the patient does not incur strain.
Then let an assistant extend the arm from the lower part, either with his hand or with a bandage.
And another assistant should extend similarly from above.
Then let the physician align the bone, until he reduces it according to the best figure that he can.
But if in the fracture of the bone there are splinters, then try to reduce every fragment to its place with your strength.
If indeed a separated splinter appears in it, and pierces the skin, and you have no hope in its restoration: then cut over it, and remove it according to the method which we described in those things which were mentioned before.
But if the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a singular chapter for that, therefore take its treatment from there.
But if in the beginning of your restoration a hot swelling occurs, then smear the cloth with a cerate made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be medium between thickness and thinness: and bind the cloth over it with a light binding, until when the swelling subsides: then remove the cerate, and apply a plaster prepared from mill dust with egg white.
Then place the splints, and let the splint which is placed over the fracture itself be somewhat wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most conditions: whether the fracture is in one focus, or in both focuses together.
Then place your binding over the fracture site stronger and more forceful, and somewhat more with binding above the fracture, or below it: or place the binding somewhat softer than its binding, according to what was mentioned before in the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths which are wrapped over the fracture be soft, moist cloths: and not hard, rough.
And let the thread with which it is bound be specifically of flax, medium between fine and thick, as we narrated.
And examine the limb and bandage after days.
But if something occurs whose correction is necessary, such as itching which occurs to the limb: then it is necessary that you bathe the limb with heated water, until that itching subsides, and you leave the limb not constricted, until it subsides, then repeat the binding.
But if the binding has already softened, and the bone has already moved, and similar things to those: then correct all that with your strength, and examine again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, due to excessive tightness, then it is necessary that you soften it a little, and leave it for several days, until nourishment flows to it, then tighten it again.
But if none of those things we mentioned happens to the patient, then it should not be loosened, except after twenty days, or thereabouts.
Then let the patient's hand be suspended from his neck.
And let his neck be level, and let it be protected in its strength from agitating movements.
And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture heals in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it heals in twenty-eight days.
All of this depends on the conditions of the complexions, and the conditions of strength.
¶ On the Treatment of Fractures of the Hand.
Chapter 14.
Know that fracture rarely occurs in the metacarpus of the Hand and in the knuckles of the fingers.
And nothing happens to it except bruising many times.
Therefore when a fracture or bruising occurs to the palm: then the patient must sit square: and before him should be a seat at the same level.
Then place his Hand extended upon it.
Then let an assistant extend the broken bones, and let the physician align them, until they are joined in good connection.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and tow, if no hot swelling occurs.
Then place a splint above according to the size of the area: and you should have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is toward the lower part near the belly of the palm, then make something like a sphere from cloth: and instruct the patient to press upon it with his fractured palm: then bind it with a long cloth.
And let the splint be of leather, in which there is softness, so that the leather may be applied to the body of the palm: and tighten it as necessary.
But if the fracture is toward the exterior, then it is necessary: that you place a splint above, and another splint below on the palm: and let the hand be open and straight.
Then apply the binding, as the hand is turned: and extend between the fingers with the bandage.
But if a fracture occurs to any of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, let it be aligned as necessary.
Then let it be bound with the palm: and if you wish, place a straight, small splint on it: so that the fracture is elevated and does not move.
But if the fracture is in the remaining fingers, such as the middle and index, or little and ring fingers, then let it be aligned, and bound with the healthy finger that follows it: or let them all be bound in continuation: and this is better.
Or place upon it a small splint, as we said for the thumb.
And consider in your treatment, and after it the condition of hot swelling: and oppose it with what is necessary, when something of that occurs, according to what is repeated in its description.
On the Repair of a Fractured Hip.
Chapter 15.
The hip bone is often broken, and it becomes apparent to the senses, since it turns toward the inner and posterior part.
And its repair is done thus: a bandage is tightened above the fracture, and another bandage below the fracture.
And the patient should lie face down.
Then the assistant should extend each bandage to its side evenly, and this is when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if the fracture is near the root of the hip, then the bandage should be placed toward the pubic area, so that the extension goes upward, and another bandage below the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is near the knee, then the bandage should be placed near the knee, so that the extension is downward.
Then the physician should align the bone with both his hands until he returns it to the likeness of its natural shape, and the bone is joined with a good connection.
Then it is necessary that you apply a plaster and binding, if no swelling occurs in the limb.
But if swelling occurs in it, then leave it for several days until the hot swelling subsides, then return to your treatment.
The binding should be done thus:
It is necessary that you wrap over the fracture a hard bandage, that is, a strip of cloth, and double it two or three times, and let there be excess remaining from it.
Then wrap the leg until the heel reaches near the root of the buttock.
And insert a long thread between the hip and leg near the knee downward, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of the two parts.
Then wrap over the leg and hip what remains of the bandage.
Then place splints over the hip at the location of its fracture, and place one of them over the leg bone.
Then fill the empty spaces between the leg and hip with soft cloths, so that the binding is even.
Then begin with strong binding from the middle over the fracture site with three or four wrappings.
And the further you move with the bandage from the fracture site, let your binding be lesser and softer and gentler.
Then attend to the two ends of the thread which you inserted between the hip and leg.
Therefore tie with both the three splints which are above.
Then go with the two ends of the thread downward until you reach the ankle of the foot.
And with both also bind the ends of the splints from the other side, so that the bandage does not move from its place.
Then leave the binding upon it while it remains thus, provided that no erosion occurs to the limb, nor swelling, nor inflammation, and similar things.
But if anything of that occurs:
Hasten therefore to loosen it, and rectify everything that occurs from it, according to what we have said many times.
If indeed there is a fragment of bone that is piercing, then it is necessary that you align it, if it is possible for you.
And if not, cut over it and remove it, and treat with that whose remembrance has preceded, until it heals.
And sometimes this hip fracture is bound without splints being added to it, as we said regarding the shoulder and arm.
However, in repairing it when fractured in such a way, lameness does not occur in the patient.
And if it is repaired alone, without the other leg being attached to it, then it is necessary that its owner will always limp.
And know that the hip is bound for fifty days, or a little more is added, or a little less: all that is according to the diversity of constitutions and other conditions.
On the Repair of Fractures of the Kneecap.
Chapter XVI.
Know that fracture rarely occurs to the kneecap, but bruising happens to it many times.
If indeed a fracture occurs to it, then it is nothing but either a crack, or a hole in its parts.
And this is either with a wound, or without a wound.
And all of this is determined by sensation.
And its repair is thus, that what is broken from its parts should be evened out with the fingers, until it is brought together and joined according to what is possible in terms of alignment, ease, and craftsmanship.
Then apply a plaster made of sesame flour, or mill flour, with egg white: and place upon it a round splint, if you need it: and bind it above with a binding suitable for that.
Then consider all the conditions which we have described in other fractures: such as hot swelling, and similar things: so that you may counter every symptom with what is appropriate for it, until it heals.
On the Repair of Fractures of the Leg.
Chapter XVII.
Know that the Leg has two bones, one of which is thick, and is called by the name of Leg: and the other thin, and is called the Splint-bone.
And both types of fractures occur to them which occur to the two bones of the arm: and because of that its repair is done just like the repair of the arm equally, and the procedure is one.
If the two bones break together, the Leg turns to all sides.
And if the thinner bone breaks, the Leg turns to the anterior part.
And if the thicker bone breaks, you find it lower: and it is among those things which are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, alignment, and binding of splints according to that equally.
However, it is necessary, if the fracture of the Leg is an ugly fracture, having many fragments: that the extension be lesser, and gentler in your relief.
And indeed in the Leg there is something in the procedure beyond the arm: and it is that when you align the splints, and complete all your procedure, then take two bands of pine wood, which is used in the construction of floors that is placed under boards: and they are made from palm spatulas: and choose from those which have small thickness, and are not from the minute ones: and let their length be according to the length of the Leg from knee to below.
Then wrap each one with cloth in two wrappings according to its length.
And place one part of the Leg from one side, and the other from the other side: and let them be from the knee to below the foot.
Then bind the two bands in three places from the two extremities and middle.
For when binding is prevented, the Leg declines to right and left, and stands in a good position.
And indeed a cradle of wood should be administered according to the length of the Leg, and the Leg should be placed in it, so that it may preserve it from movement.
And most importantly it is necessary that you do this in a fracture when there is especially a wound with it.
Then examine the Leg every day, and study it with ultimate attention for swelling, or inflammation, or other things.
If therefore any of these occurs, then counter it with what is necessary, until it heals, if God wills.
On the fracture of the bones of the Foot and Toes.
Chapter 18.
Indeed, fracture never occurs in the heel bone.
However, fracture sometimes occurs in the bones of the Foot, and fracture rarely occurs in the Toes: and nothing happens to them except crushing in most cases.
If fracture occurs in the bones of the Foot, and you see those bones raised up against each other, then let the patient put his Foot on prepared ground, as if walking.
Then stand, and place your Foot on what is elevated from those bones: then press, or step on it: and level it until it returns to its place.
Then apply a plaster, and put tow on top: and place under the sole of the Foot a small board, which should have two flat ends.
Then bind it with the Foot with proper binding, after you wrap it with cloths and other things that are needed.
When therefore three or four days have passed, then loosen the bandage.
