For you will find the bones leveled, whether they were fractured or dislocated.
Therefore the procedure for them is as you see.
But if any toes are broken, then restore them, and align them according to what I told you about restoring the fingers of the hand.
Then place on your finger a splint according to the length of the toe, and let it be slightly wider than it: then place under the Foot this board which I described to you, and bind it with proper binding.
If indeed what is broken from the bones is two, or three, or more, then place over each toe a splint of its size, wrapped in linen cloth: and bind the board to the bottom of the Foot: and let it stand on one of the ends of the board outside the sole of the Foot, so that it holds with good retention.
And it is proper for you not to forget to consider everything I told you in the remaining fractures about the accidents we mentioned: and oppose each accident with what is necessary.
¶ On the fracture of a woman's Vulva, and of the pubic bone, and of a man's penis.
Chapter 19.
When a woman's Vulva is broken, then make her sit square.
Then incline her slightly toward her back, and let her be held from behind.
Then let the midwife fill her Vulva with cotton, until she fills it: and let it become in the Vulva like a sphere from it.
Then let the woman be shaken, and raise her back gradually.
For that cotton comes out, until it becomes before the entrance of the Vulva like a sphere, and the broken bone returns.
Then place a small cushion on her back.
Therefore when she wants to urinate, let her remove the cotton carefully, then urinate: and return it according to the way in which you first inserted it: then let her return to her first cushion.
Let her do that for seven days, or thereabouts, for the bone itself heals.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder, and bind over its opening a reed pipe, and insert the whole bladder into her Vulva.
Then blow into the pipe with force, until the bladder inflates inside the Vulva, for the fracture returns.
Then let it be filled with cotton, and let it remain for some days, according to what we described, until it heals.
And when the pubic bone of a man or woman is broken, then administer in its restoration and its leveling that which we described for the hip bone.
And the correct way is not hidden in these nearby fractures, which rarely occur, especially for one who has some study or practice.
And understand this book of mine much, since the fracture itself indicates to you in most cases the right way in its restoration and its binding: therefore understand.
But when a man's penis is broken, take a goose's throat: and insert the penis into it.
Then place over it a wrapping of cloth, and let it be wrapped, and let it be left for three days, or thereabouts, and it will heal, God willing.
¶ On the restoration of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
Chapter 20.
It is appropriate for one who has suffered a fracture with a wound, especially if it is large, like a hip bone or shoulder bone or similar to it, that you hurry and bleed them immediately, if the conditions for bloodletting are favorable, as we mentioned before.
If the wound is flowing with blood, then it is necessary that you hurry to stop it, by sprinkling powdered vitriol over it, if nothing else is available to you.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day: and do not postpone it, unless inflammation occurs.
If however hot inflammation occurs, then leave its restoration until the ninth day, until the inflammation subsides.
And do not approach it on the third and fourth day at all, for you would cause bad complications.
If however the fractured bone is protruding above the skin exposed: then you must try to reduce it and align it with your hands with gentleness and slight extension.
If however its extension and alignment cannot be achieved with your hands: then reduce it with this instrument.
And it is an instrument made of iron, whose length is about seven or eight fingers, and its width is according to the size of the wound.
And for this reason it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of them according to the amount needed for treatment in every type of fracture.
And let it be round: and let there be a small thickness in it, so that it does not bend during compression upon it at the time of operation: and let it be sharp-pointed, with an edge at its extremities.
And let its upper part be of thickness from its middle down to its lower parts.
And this is its form.
It is necessary in this operation that its curved sharp end be placed on the end of the protruding bone: and push it with it once, until when the bone returns and is somewhat aligned.
Therefore try to align the ends of the fracture with each other.
But if its fractured end is thin, and the instrument cannot get a good grip on it, then it is necessary that you cut the end of that bone until the instrument has power over it.
If however you cannot reduce them with what we have described at all, then cut it with what is suitable from the cutting instruments we mentioned: or saw it with one of the saws in whatever way possible for you.
Then scrape away what remains on the bone of roughness and small fragments.
When therefore the bone is reduced, and the patient finds severe damaging pain after its reduction, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Therefore do this.
For you bring great benefit to the patient when your restoration of the bone is completed.
Then submerge a cloth in astringent black wine.
And especially if it is in summer.
And do not put wax ointment on the wound, nor anything containing oil: lest it cause putrefaction and corruption in it.
Then apply splints at the time you complete the bone restoration.
And leave the wound exposed: so that you cut with scissors a hole in the bandages according to the size of the wound.
And take every precaution not to constrict the wound with the fracture.
For many times foolish physicians have done this: and have caused either death, or gangrene, or zuche (that is, pus) to occur in their patients.
And let your binding be soft, gentle, different from the binding of other fractures.
But if the wound is bad, or if the wound is large, and you fear over it certain bad complications which we have described, and you find persistent pain in the area: then you should not put splints on it.
And make bandages for them from hard cloths in place of splints, and bind it with these.
When it has been one day or two days, and you see the wound already beginning to generate pus: then remove from above it the cloth which you placed over it with wine.
Then administer lint cloths and ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds: such as tetrapharmacon ointment and similar ones to it.
And it is proper for you to loosen the bandage.
And examine the wound every day morning and evening, until it consolidates and heals.
And it is necessary that the limb be positioned in such a way that pus flows from it downward with ease.
But if many days pass for the wound, and it does not consolidate, nor does the pus cease from it: then know that there are small bone fragments there.
Therefore it is necessary that you examine the wound with a probe.
However many of those bone fragments are loose, remove them and extract them.
And those which are not separated, and which prick the limb and cause pain to occur, try to cut them and remove them in whatever way possible for you.
But if zuchen or ekileten (that is, gangrene) occurs in the wound, or another type of corruption and putrefaction: then it is necessary that you treat each of these with what is appropriate for it from the treatment, whose description preceded in its chapter.
And among that which it is necessary that my discourse should emphasize, and to which you should give your present attention when a bone is broken and protrudes over the limb, like the hip bone and shoulder bone and similar ones from the large limbs, is that you should not attempt to pull it, for often death occurs from that, but rather leave it until it decays.
For perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then therefore treat the wound, if it seems right to you: and if not, leave it alone.
¶ On the Treatment of Nodules in the Site of a Fracture.
Chapter 21.
This Nodulation often occurs in the site of a healing fracture.
Especially that which is near the joints: and the appearance of the limb is disfigured by it.
And perhaps it prevents the limb from its natural function.
Consider therefore then, if the nodule is recent: administer medicines which have astringency to it, such as aloe, and frankincense, and myrrh, and sarcocolla, and acacia, and similar things to these.
Thus, take some of these, or all of them: and combine them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar: and place them over the nodule in tow, and bind them over it with good binding.
And leave the binding, do not loosen it for many days.
Then loosen it, and repeat another, until the nodule is removed.
Or bind a lead plate over it appropriately.
For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from limbs.
But if the nodule has already turned to stone, and is tight, and necessity quickly calls for removing it: then cut into it from its upper part, and cut away the protruding excess: or scrape it with certain rasps, until it is removed: and treat it until it heals.
¶ On the Treatment When it is Restored, and the Limb Afterward Remains Thin, Not According to its Nature.
Chapter 22.
When a bone fracture is restored, and the limb afterward remains thin, weak: then this happens only because of many causes.
Of which one is, frequent loosening of the bandage and binding it not according to what is proper: or because of excessive tightness of the bandages, until nourishment is prevented from flowing to the limb: or because of excessive moistening at the wrong time: or because of scarcity of blood in the patient's body, and its weakness.
And its treatment is to feed the patient, and to make his body soft, until blood multiplies in it.
And administer a bath.
And introduce joy and happiness to him.
Then place pitch on the limb: so that the pitch draws much nourishment to it.
And continue its moistening with tepid water, until nourishment flows, and it returns to its natural appearance.
¶ On the treatment of fractured bones when they heal crookedly.
Chapter 23.
When it happens that a limb that has already healed after treatment becomes crooked, and there is a protrusion of the fractured bone, or a nodule: and the form of the limb is disfigured by it, yet the limb is not prevented from its natural function: then you should not accept the advice of one who thinks that the limb should be broken again.
And there have indeed been many foolish physicians and bone-setters who have done this in our land.
And this procedure is indeed very blameworthy, leading to death, or great fear, namely that it might be fatal.
But if the lameness and nodule are recent: then it is necessary to apply fomentation with tepid water, in which softening herbs have been boiled, such as marshmallow leaves, and its root, and melilot, and similar things.
And make a plaster for it with a softening plaster, such as properly made diachylon.
Or take the mucilage of marshmallow root, and shake it with chicken fat, or sesame oil: and make a plaster with it.
Or take fat figs, and grind them with pigeon dung, and similar things which are named among medicines that reduce consolidation.
And sometimes the nodule is dissolved with persistent, long-term, and gentle friction, which is done with moist hands.
And movement of the limb should be administered in all directions at all times.
But if the crookedness has already preceded, and has become strong, and has turned to stone, and there has arisen a necessity for treating it with iron: then it is necessary that its upper part be cut, and the continuity of the bone be broken, and what is excessive from the nodule or bone be cut away with fine cutting tools: and ease should be administered in that with care and attention.
¶ On the Discussion of Dislocation.
Chapter 24.
Dislocation is the exit of any joint from its place.
Whereby it is prevented from its movement, and the shape of the limb is disfigured.
And it causes severe pains and injuries to come upon the patient.
Therefore when dislocation happens to someone, then it is necessary to hurry to its treatment: and do not delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the area becomes inflamed, and perhaps with it the reduction of the dislocation.
Because of that therefore it is not proper to move it, nor to extend it during the time of its inflammation.
Since many times it causes spasm and harmful pains to occur.
But when that happens, then it is necessary that you hurry to bleed the patient.
Then let it be left until the inflammation subsides.
Then the limb should be fomented with hot water and oil.
Then it should be reduced with care.
And every limb should be treated with that which mention will come of in its place.
I indeed have already arranged the chapters on dislocation, also according to what preceded in fracture, from the upper part of the body to its lower part.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
Chapter 25.
The two jaws are rarely dislocated except on rare occasions.
And the dislocation of both occurs in one of two ways.
Either they are removed from their places by a small displacement, and are slightly loosened.
Or they are dislocated with a complete, full dislocation, until they loosen towards the chest, until the patient's saliva runs, and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue becomes involved in speech.
When its dislocation is small, then it returns in most cases by itself with little intervention.
But if the dislocation is complete, full: then it is necessary that its reduction be administered quickly, and not delayed at all: and it is that an assistant should hold the patient's head: and the physician should insert the thumb of one of his hands into the root of the dislocated jaw between its bone, if the dislocation is on one side.
Or he should insert both his thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides.
And the remaining fingers of his hands should be outside, with which he aligns it.
And instruct the patient to clear his jaws, and release them for chewing in every direction.
And the physician should align the jaw, and push the jaw, until it returns to its place.
But if its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are involved together: then administer steam with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both becomes easy: and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we said.
Therefore when they return and are aligned, and the patient's mouth closes, and they do not loosen: then place cushions of cloth over them, with a wax plaster that is made from wax and rose oil.
Then it should be bound gently with soft bandages.
And the patient should sleep on his back: and his head should be positioned between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right and left: nor should he attempt, or be forced to chew anything: rather his food should be made into a mild soup, until when the pains are removed, and the jaw is knitted: then he may eat what he wants, and this should be administered with ease.
He should not strain over the operation of his mouth in eating and drinking, and yawning, until the jaw is knitted, and healed.
But if the reduction of the two jaws becomes difficult, when they are dislocated at some time, and do not return to their places: then often fevers occur from this, and persistent headache: and perhaps the patient's bowels are loosened, and perhaps he vomits pure bile.
Therefore when you see this: then know that it is destruction, and often the one to whom this happens dies within ten days.
On the reduction of dislocation of the Clavicle, and the extremity of the upper arm.
Chapter 26.
Indeed the clavicle is not dislocated from the inner side, because of its connection with the chest.
And it is sometimes dislocated to the outside: and this is apparent to the senses.
And its restoration is that the patient should lie on his back, and extend his arms.
Then compress the area with your palm with strong compression, for it will return.
Then place a plaster over it, and cushions, and bind it.
Indeed its extremities which follow the elbow, and are connected with it, are not dislocated, except rarely.
But if it is dislocated on some day: then it is necessary that it be reduced and aligned according to what we said, and what is prepared for you.
Then place a plaster over it, and cushions, and a bandage.
And instruct the patient to maintain tranquility and rest, until he is healed.
And with this same treatment the extremity of the upper arm is reduced, when it is also separated from its place.
¶ On the Reduction of Dislocation of the Shoulder.
Chapter XXVII.
Know that the shoulder can only be dislocated in three ways.
One of which is when it is dislocated towards the armpit downward.
The second is when it is dislocated towards the chest.
And sometimes it is dislocated to the upper part of the shoulder: and this happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated posteriorly, because of the shoulder blade.
And it is not dislocated anteriorly, because of the nerves.
And most often it is dislocated and moves downward towards the armpit: especially in those whose flesh is sparse.
Because in them it moves out quickly, and moves back in quickly.
In those whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite of this, namely that it moves out with difficulty, and moves back in with difficulty.
And sometimes it happens to some people that due to a blow, or a fall, the shoulder becomes swollen with a hot swelling, and because of this it is thought that it has been dislocated.
Therefore, it is necessary that you examine it, until you are certain of it, and then proceed with its treatment.
And a dislocation is recognized when it is downward towards the armpit: and you should distinguish between the dislocated shoulder and the healthy shoulder.
For you find between them a clear difference, and you find in the head of the shoulder a cavity, and under the armpit upon touching the head of the shoulder it is as if it were an egg: and the patient cannot raise his hand to his ear, nor move it in any direction.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest, or upward: then you find clear evidence upon touching, nor is it hidden.
And indeed the reduction of this dislocation becomes easy when it is recent, or the patient is a child.
And its reduction is thus: let an assistant raise his hand upward.
Then place your thumbs under his armpit, and raise the joint forcefully upward to its place: and let the assistant raise his hand, and extend it upward.
Then let him lower it, for it returns quickly.
But if it does not return by what we have described, and many days have passed since the dislocation occurred: then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water, and an embrocation be administered which softens and soothes, such as if marshmallow roots, fenugreek, and melilot are cooked in water, and administered.
Then let the patient lie on his back: and let there be placed under his armpit a sphere of wool, which should be balanced between softness and firmness.
Then let the physician place his heel on the sphere, and raise the head of the shoulder with force, and pull the patient's arm downward.
And let another assistant hold the patient's head, so that it does not move downward: for it returns immediately.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method: that is, have present a man who is taller than the patient, and let him stand at the side, and insert his shoulder under the patient's armpit: and raise his armpit upward, until the patient is suspended in the air.
And let another assistant pull the patient's arm downward towards his belly.
But if the patient is light: then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him, to weigh him down: for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also reduced according to another method: that is, let a long piece of wood be fixed in the ground, whose head is round in shape: like the handle of a mortar: which should neither be thick nor thin.
Then let it be placed under the patient after soft cloths are placed on the head of the wood: and let the patient be standing according to the length of the wood.
Then let his arm be extended downward on one side, and let his body be extended on the other side with force: for the joint returns to its place quickly.
If its reduction is difficult through all that we have said, then use this treatment: that is, take a wood whose length is the measure of two arms, and its width the measure of four fingers, and its thickness the measure of two fingers: which should have a round head, so that its entry into the depth of the armpit is eased, according to this form.
Then let soft cloths be tied over the round head, so that the wood does not harm the patient.
Then let it be placed under the patient's armpit, so that the hand or arm is extended over the wood down to the lower parts: and let the wood be tied over the upper arm and forearm and extremity of the hand.
Then let the arm be placed over the step of a ladder by its width, and let the hand be extended downward, and let the rest of the body be left suspended from the other side.
For the joint goes in immediately.
When therefore its entry is completed in whatever way possible: then it is necessary that you place under the armpit a sphere of equal size made of wool.
Then place a plaster prepared from mill dust with frankincense and egg white over the entire shoulder, as it is wrapped over it.
Then bind beneath the armpit with proper binding: and let the bandage be wrapped over the plaster above: and let his hand be tied to his neck, and let it be left, nor let his hand be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary that the patient's food be little, until the limb is strengthened: since it is quicker for its healing.
Then let it be made more abundant after seven days, or five, and let it be exercised with movement.
If therefore it becomes firm and does not soften, then it has already healed.
If however the joint dislocates many times, because of moisture that affects it, or because of another cause: then it is necessary that cautery be administered to it with three holes, according to what was mentioned in the chapter on cautery.
If all this is done, and the bandage is loosened after seven days, and the joint is not secured, and the plaster and binding are reapplied over it several times, and it does not hold firm, and falls, and cannot be raised upward: then know that its nerves which are at the head of the humerus have already been cut, or stretched, or softened: and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
The reduction of a dislocation that occurs toward the chest and breast, or toward the back, is accomplished with pushing and extension using the hands: until it returns, and the remaining binding and treatment should be administered to it until it heals.
If after healing heaviness occurs in the limb, and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use the bath many times, until that heaviness is relieved, and it returns to its original nature.
¶ On the treatment of Elbow dislocation.
Chapter 28.
Know that the Elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty, and similarly returns with difficulty also: and it can indeed be dislocated to all sides, and especially to the front or to the back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you: since it is apparent to sight and touch, in whatever way it is dislocated.
And when you compare the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, this becomes clearly evident to you.
For the joint becomes hollow, and cannot bend the arm, nor touch its shoulder with it.
And it is necessary that you hurry, and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot swelling occurs in it.
For if a hot swelling occurs in it, its reduction becomes difficult: and perhaps it will not heal at all, especially when it is toward the back.
For it is the worst of all types of dislocations, and causes the most severe pain of them: and death often follows with it.
And its restoration when it is among those that are possible to reduce, is that his hand should be extended with both hands: and his arm should be expanded, and the physician's hands should be above and below the elbow, and he should push the joint with the thumbs of both his hands together, or with the heel of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or if the dislocation is toward the front: then it returns thus, that he should bend the hand with his hand, until he strikes the dislocated elbow with the heel of his palm.
If the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer very forceful, strong extension: that is, two assistants should extend the arm: and two assistants should also hold it, so that it does not move during extension.
Then the arm should be rotated to every side after a long folded cloth or wide bandage is wrapped around his hand.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, he should anoint it with oil: so that this may be among those things that help in the lubrication of the joint with ease.
Then he should push the joint with forceful pushing, until it returns.
And after its reduction, it is necessary that a plaster be placed over it, in which there should be astringency and drying with egg white: and it should be bound with proper binding.
And the arm should be suspended from the patient's neck: and left for several days.
Then it should be loosened.
If therefore the joint becomes firm in its place, then remove the bandage from it, and leave it.
And if you see the joint is not well tightened, then repeat the plaster and bandage, and leave it for several days, until it tightens.
Then loosen it.
If however heaviness occurs in it after reduction of the joint, and there is a condition in movement: then administer moistening in bathing, and gentle friction, and compression, until it is soothed, or place upon the joint the fat of a ram's buttock.
Then it should be bound, and left upon it day and night.
Then remove it, and put him in the bath.
When therefore it sweats, then rub the joint with moderate friction.
Then repeat the aleliati [possibly a medicinal preparation] on it once, and a second time, and a third time upon entering the bath, until it softens.
And if you wish, place wet cow dung on it, warmed with butter, and bind it tight.
Do this several times.
For it will soften and return to its normal condition.
¶ On the treatment of dislocation of the Almahassem [wrist].
Chapter 29.
The Mahassem of the hand is often dislocated, that is, the joint which is between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy.
Unlike other joints.
However, it is necessary that there be swiftness in reducing its dislocation in the hour in which it is dislocated, before the area swells: or a hot swelling occurs in it.
And the reduction of the dislocation in it is that you place the Mahassem of the diseased hand on a board, and the assistant extends his hand: and the physician places his palm over the prominence of the joint, and pushes it until it returns.
However, it is necessary that it be observed, if the palm has already receded to the belly of the hand: then the patient should place the back of his hand on the board during the extension and reduction: and if the dislocation is proceeding to the back of the hand, then the position of his hand should be internally on the board, that is, the inner part of his hand should be placed on the board, so that the physician's hand falls upon the prominence of the joint.
If therefore it returns immediately, it is good: and if not, then bind it with a poultice that reduces swelling: and leave it, and do not repeat it.
For if it cannot tolerate it, nor can you achieve its reduction, after days pass, however the joint remains according to its crookedness: and it does not harm the patient in any way, except that the hand is weakened, and cannot grip anything: then know that the nerves are severed, or worn away.
There is therefore no technique for it, except that it should be bound with a cautery near its fall.
And perhaps that will not help at all.
When therefore the Almahassem does not return, then place upon it the poultice, which we have described.
Then let it be bound, and left for five days: afterwards let it be loosened and left, and let the hand be exercised.
If however its movement is lost, and some heaviness occurs in it: then soften it with heated water, and with friction several times, until it softens.
¶ On the treatment of dislocation of the Fingers.
Chapter 30.
When a Finger from these is dislocated to any part, or from these a Finger to the back of the palm, or its belly: then extend the Finger, and push the dislocation with your thumbs, until it returns.
Then support the head of the Finger, and suspend it toward the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend it, and extend it, until it is made straight on that day of its own.
When therefore it is night, bind it according to the same account: and do not cease to loosen it in the day: and exercise it with movement: and bind it at night.
Do this for several days, until it is tight.
And similarly do with it, if it is dislocated to the belly of the hand: and bind it to that part, and do your first operation with it, until it heals.
And similarly do with it when it is dislocated to the remaining parts.
¶ On the treatment of dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
Chapter 31.
When someone suffers a complete dislocation of the vertebrae of the back or neck, or many vertebrae are moved from their places: then there is no cure for it, since death hastens to the patient.
And the sign of this is that the patient's waste comes out without control, and he cannot hold it: and often certain of his limbs become paralyzed, or his two legs, or his two arms, or one of them: or a single vertebra is moved from its place.
Indeed it often happens that their displacement occurs in four directions.
That which is displaced toward the back is called Gibbosity [hunchback].
The treatment for this is that you should then consider, if the Gibbosity occurred from childhood: then there is no cure for it, nor is there any healing from it at all.
But for that which occurs from a fall, or a blow, or similar things, the ancients have already described methods of treatment in lengthy discourse.
Most of which does not return with benefit.
I have indeed abbreviated from it, whose little excuses from much of it from my proximity to the intention: and in my exposition which I have explained it, and I have formed its instrument in a different way from that which they showed it, and explained it.
Therefore I say that in gibbosity which occurs from the anterior part, there is no remedy, nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which occurs to the two sides also.
And none of these are cured, except that which occurs in the back specifically, with that which I describe: and it is that the patient should be stretched out on his face on a level platform near a wall: and a moist bedding should be spread under him, so that his chest is not injured.
Then let a straight pole be fixed in a pit in the ground, at the end of the platform, around his head: and another pole around his feet at the other end of the platform: and let an assistant hold the pole, and let it not be firmly fixed in the pit.
And let another assistant hold the other one.
Then wrap around the patient's head, and under his armpits a soft, firm bandage: and extend the end of the bandage to the pole which is at his head: and tie it to it.
Then tighten another bandage above his hips, and above his knees, and at his ankles.
Then gather all the bandages together, and tie them to the other pole which is at his feet.
Then let each assistant pull the bandage toward the pole's side, and let the two poles not be moved from their places in which they are fixed: however they should not be firmly fixed, as we said.
And let the physician place his palms on the vertebra with force, until it returns: or let him place a board on it.
Then let him attach his feet over the board, until it returns.
But if it does not return through this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three arms' length.
And hollow out in the wall which we said is near the patient, a place into which the end of the board may enter.
Then place the middle of the board over the vertebra.
And let the physician place his feet on the other end.
And let him press well, until the vertebra is compressed, and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, work with the winch which is turned with your hand: and it is that you fix in the ground at the head of the patient at the back of the platform two poles, whose length should be one arm's length: and at his head at the back of the platform two poles.
And let the length which is between each pole be one palm: and already a hole has been made in each place, in which the winch runs: and let each pole be fixed very firmly in the ground, lest it move at all.
And let a round pole be inserted: and it is a winch in which the bandage is wound in the two holes of the two poles.
And at its end there is a hole, in which is fixed a wooden piece, whose length is a palm including what revolves: and in the other two wooden pieces it is similarly so.
Then tighten the bindings which you fastened on the patient's chest with the turner that is at his head: and the bindings which you fastened on his legs with the turner that is at his feet.
Then let an assistant stand at each turner, who should turn his hand with the strap by which the turner is rotated: and the physician should align the vertebra, according to what we said.
This is the form of the turner that follows.
Then after the vertebra returns and the area is aligned, then it is necessary that you apply a drying plaster with egg white.
Then [apply] tow (hemp/flax fibers).
Afterwards place above the plaster a splint made from a board, whose width should be three fingers, or thereabouts.
And its length should be enough to cover the area of the hump and over part of the healthy vertebra.
Then let it be bound with the binding we mentioned.
And let the patient use light nourishment until he is healed.
But if some protrusion remains in the area at the end of healing: then it is necessary that medicines which soften and soothe be administered, with the application of the board which we described for a long time.
And a lead plate is now applied to it.
And sometimes a protrusion occurs in the back of the spine's vertebrae: and through this a dislocation is suspected, and it is that bone which protrudes.
Therefore one should not attempt this treatment on it: for perhaps it will cause death.
¶ On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Chapter 32.
Know that the joints of the hip and the joints of the shoulder suffer nothing but complete dislocation: nor do they suffer what happens to other joints from slight displacement and hollowing.
And indeed the hip joint is dislocated in four directions.
That is, because it dislocates forward and backward.
And most often it dislocates inward: and rarely dislocates forward and backward.
And the treatment of its dislocation inward is: that when you join the patient's healthy leg with the injured one, it is longer: and his knee protrudes more than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend his foot at the groin: and the area following the groin is swollen with hot swelling, because the head of the hip has arrived there.
And the sign of one who has suffered dislocation outward is that his symptoms are contrary to these symptoms.
And the sign of one who has suffered dislocation forward is that he extends the healthy leg completely: however he does not bend it without pain which is in the knee: and if he tries to walk, he cannot [move] forward on it: and his urine is retained, and his groin swells, and when walking his stepping is on the heel.
And the sign of one who has suffered dislocation backward is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it before bending the groin: and his leg is also shorter than the other: and the groin is softened, and the head of the hip is at the location of the ilium.
The reduction of these types of dislocation is that you should then consider if the dislocation is old, which has already become chronic in the one having it.
Then if you administer its reduction, and it remains in its condition: then there is no treatment for it at all.
Therefore one should not attempt it at all.
But in one whose dislocation is recent, and is of one of the four types of dislocation: then hasten to rotate the joint, and extend it inward and outward, and move it right and left: for perhaps it will return, and does not need other treatment.
But if it does not return this way, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong assistant, and he should extend his leg from the lower part: either with his hands, or with a binding, which is tied over his leg above the knee: and another assistant should extend his hand from above, so that he puts his hands under his armpits.
Then let him bind with a soft bandage above the root of the hip, and hold the end of the other bandage.
And let its extension be either from the anterior part of the groin to the collarbone area, or from the posterior to the back area.
And let the extension of all of them be at once, until the patient is lifted with his body from the ground, and remains suspended.
And indeed this type of extension is a common type among the four types.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good: and if not, then each type needs that which I will speak of regarding proper treatment.
In its proper reduction when the dislocation is toward the interior, it is necessary that the patient lie back on his healthy side.
Then place the bandage above the root of the hip, in that area which is between the head of the hip and the place below the groin.
Then extend the bandage upward from the groin area to the higher part of the body, to the collarbone area.
Then let another strong assistant take his arms, and reduce the thick area of the diseased hip, and extend it outward with vigorous extension.
Indeed it has already returned to its place.
And this type is easier than the remaining types of treatment by which this limb is reduced.
But if it defies you, and does not yield to entry with this type of treatment at all: then it is necessary that the patient's two feet be bound together with a strong, soft bandage, above the two heels and above the two knees: and let the distance of each from its partner be four fingers' width: and let the diseased leg be extended more than the other by two fingers' width.
Then let the patient be suspended over the head of a wooden beam, which should be in the house: and let its height from the ground be two arms' length.
Then order a strong young man to secure the head of the hip: and let another young man be suspended with the patient: and let another young man, securing the hip, push with force, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
In its proper return, when the dislocation is outward, it is necessary that the patient lie on a bench, according to what we described for one having a hump.
And let the bandage be bound over his diseased leg specifically, and over his chest.
Then let two wooden pieces be placed, one at his feet, and the other at his head.
Then let an additional piece of wood be placed in the middle of the bench, firmly fixed: over which wet cloths are wrapped, so it does not harm the patient: and let the wood be between his hips, so it is not pulled downward during extension.
Then let each assistant extend toward his side, and let the physician equalize the reduction with his hand.
If therefore it yields to return, it will be good.
And if not, place a board over it, and press it, as we said in the case of the hump, evenly.
However, it is necessary that the patient lie on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is toward the front, then it is necessary that the patient's leg be quickly extended: and it is according to this same account upon a podium: and let the physician place the palm of his right hand over the patient's groin.
Then let him compress it with the other hand, and with that compression place it extended downward toward the knee.
And if the dislocation is toward the back: then it is not necessary that the patient be extended downward, and be elevated above the ground: rather it is necessary that he be placed upon a hard surface, just as is necessary if his hip is dislocated outward, according to what we said about his lying on both hips: and he is on his face, and the ligaments extended: according to what we just said.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a board also over the place to which the joint exits, when the reduction is completed as necessary.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you: and it is that you extend the patient's two legs: and when you see them equal, and the patient tightens his leg, and extends it without excuse: then know that the limb has already returned as necessary: then join the two thighs, and apply a plaster, and bind with an imemati, that is, a wheel: so that the hip does not move to any side: and let the patient adhere to rest for three or four days.
Then loosen the bandage and the plaster, and compare the leg with the other: then if you see them equal in position, then know that the dislocation has already been secured: therefore release the patient to walking.
And if you see any looseness: then return, and plaster it, and bind it according to your first binding: and leave it also for three days.
Then loosen it and make him delay in walking on both for some days, until it is well strengthened.
¶ Concerning Dislocation of the Knee.
Chapter 33.
The knee is dislocated in three ways.
It is dislocated outward, inward, and downward, that is, backward.
And it is not dislocated forward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you order the patient to join his leg to his thigh.
If however it does not join with the thigh: then know that the Knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all types of its dislocation is that you make the patient sit upright, so that he extends his leg if he can upon it, and your back joins to his face: and place his foot between your feet.
Then make his Knee join with your palms, and straighten them between your fingers over his Knee.
Then apply with your palm both sides of his Knee with force.
And let another assistant extend his foot, until the Knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg joins with the thigh in smoothness, not laboriously.
Then plaster it, and make the leg join with the thigh.
Then bind them together with a band for three or four days.
Then loosen them.
And let the patient not use walking for some days, until he is healed.
If however its reduction is refused to you, then administer strong extension with bandages, whose account preceded in the treatment of the hip, until it returns.
On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Heel
Chapter 34
The heel sometimes moves with a small displacement, and sometimes becomes completely dislocated.
And its dislocation occurs either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen, protruding toward the side to which it is dislocated.
The treatment of its displacement is easy to reverse: it is to extend it gently with the hands and align it until it returns.
In treating it when it is completely dislocated, it is necessary that you have the patient sit upright: and a very strong assistant should hold him from behind his back, in the middle of it.
Then you hold his foot with your right hand from its upper part, and with your left hand from the lower part of his foot at the ankle.
Then pull the foot toward you with your right hand.
Then with the left hand twice.
Then extend with the left, and push the instep with the right hand toward the leg without strain.
Do this twice, as we have described.
Then push the instep toward the leg a third time, while you pull the ankle.
If it returns in one or two attempts in this way, and you see the foot aligned, it is good: and if not, then repeat the procedure on it: for it will return.
But if you are prevented from reducing it with what we have described: then have the patient lie on his back on the ground, and fix a peg firmly in the ground, so that it falls between his hips: and you have already wrapped cloth around it, so it does not harm the patient.
Then let an assistant take his thigh: afterward let another assistant extend the foot, either with his hands or with a bandage tied over the neck of the foot.
Then let each assistant pull against the extension of his companion: and let the peg stand between the patient's two thighs holding him, so that his body is not drawn downward during the extension.
Then let the physician align the dislocation with his hands, and let another assistant hold the healthy leg downward.
For the dislocation returns quickly.
When therefore the dislocation returns, and its proper return becomes clear to you: then apply a plaster and tow, and bind it firmly with bandages, and tie the foot to the leg with a bandage.
And it is necessary that you beware or fear for the nerves, which are behind above the heel, so that the bandage is not too tight over them, and harm them.
Then leave it for two or three days.
If the bandage loosens, tighten it: then loosen it on the third or fourth day: and forbid the patient from walking for forty days.
If indeed he attempts to walk before that period, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not recur in him, and become corrupted, and not accept treatment after that.
But if a hot swelling occurs to him: then it is necessary that you administer for its calming that which we have previously described in another place regarding treatment and fomentation, until it is removed.
On the Dislocation of the Toes, and Treatment
Chapter 35
It is necessary that whatever is dislocated from them be aligned with little extension without strain: and that indeed is not difficult, rather it is easy.
If however the dislocation is in some of the joints of the top of the foot: then it is necessary that the patient sit with his foot on a level place on the ground, or on a board: and he is standing as if walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot over what protrudes from those joints.
Then press it with your foot with force, until it returns, and you see it aligned, and no protrusion appears in the place.
Then place under the sole of his foot a board which takes the whole foot, which has two ends.
Then bind it with appropriate binding for three days.
Then loosen it, and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is tightened and secured from recurrence.
¶ On the types of dislocation with wound or fracture.
Chapter 36.
When something of this sort happens, and you attempt its treatment and its restoration, death often follows: and because of this, it is not proper that anyone should undertake the treatment of such a case, unless he is skilled in the art through long practice, compassionate, dutiful, cautious, neither bold nor presumptuous: and that he should use at the beginning only medicines which reduce hot swellings: and leave the patient to divine judgment.
Unless you see or hope for recovery from destruction with mildness of the illness, and in which there appears some hope.
Therefore attempt its reduction immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot swelling occurs.
If then the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regimen that reduces swelling: and treat the wound with what is suitable for it from drying ointments.
But if the dislocation is with fracture, and separated bone fragments occur, then attempt their removal, and do in that case what we said about simple conditions from what preceded in their sections.
And be careful according to your ability, and guard yourself from entering the path of fear: according to what I previously advised you.
For that is better for you before your Creator, and more lasting to your glory, and safer for your body among those in your time.
